                             LOANPROGRAMSUMMARY

                                            MEXICO
   FIRSTPROGRAMMATICCOMPETITIVENESSDEVELOPMENT POLICYLOAN

Borrower       The United Mexican States

[mplementing   Secretariat of Economy
4gency
4mount         US$300,760,000

rerms          The borrower has requested that the loan be a fixed spread loan in US dollars, commitment
               linkedwith a total maturity of 15 years and a grace period of 5 years. The borrower has
               opted to finance the front-end fee with the proceeds of the loan. Standard commitment
               charge of 0.85% for the first 4 years and 0.75% per annum thereafter.
l'ranching     One tranche to be disbursed upon effectiveness

Description    The proposed loan will support the first stage of the Government of Mexico (G0M)'s
               medium term program to improve Mexico's lagging competitiveness. Specifically, the loan
               aims to:


                 a. Build on past competitiveness efforts, strengthen new and existing programs, and
                      promote a holistic, cross-governmental approach to improving productivity in order to
                      accelerategrowth and increaseexport and employment levels; and
                 b. Contribute to the formulation of a medium-to-long term competitiveness policy agenda
                      and help lay a solid foundation of competitiveness programs in advance of the next
                      administration, which takes ofice in December 2006.

               The World Bank would support the achievement of the overall objectives through a three-
               phase Programmatic Competitiveness Development Policy Loan (DPL). The first phase
               would support policy and institutional reforms in the following areas: a) innovation.
               technology development, and training; b) the investment climate; c) logistics and trade
               facilitation; and d) energy. These areas are based on the central pillars of the GOM's
               Competitiveness Agenda, with the exception of financial deepening, which i s left to          E
               separate but concurrent Finance DPL. The second phase of this DPL is planned for mid-
               2007, allowing the new administration a semester to transition into office.

               This loan buildson Banksupport to Mexico on competitiveness issuessince 2003. Since thai
               time, the Bank has conducted competitiveness focus groups, helped the GOM formulate it:
               Competitiveness Agenda, and completed the first phase of a competitiveness AAA tha
               involved a wide range of technical assistanceand analytical work.

               While the Mexican economy has been improving throughout this decade and extremz
               poverty has been reduced, the economy has not grown at the rates necessary to significantlj
               generatejobs. Real GDP per capita only grew at an annual rate of 1.2 percent between 199L
               and 2004. Incontrast, GDP per capita grew at 3.3 percent in Chile, 7.7 percent in China, anc
               5.9 percent in the East Asia and Pacific region as a whole. Productivity growth has also beer
               disappointing and Mexico has been losing its share of the U.S. import market, mainly tc
               China. In addition, comprehensive competitiveness measures from a variety of sources shop
               that Mexico i s lagging, especially relative to per capita income. All of these results point to
               an urgent need to enhance competitiveness in Mexico if the country i s going to further
               reduce poverty.




                                                  11

3enefits    Mexican growth and productivity stand to improve through programs that tackle constraints
            :o competitiveness in a wide variety of sectors. As a result, standards o f living should go up
            mdpoverty should fall inthe mediumterm.

            More specifically, this loan aims to improve the capacity of small business to participate in
            the formal market and to increase their competitiveness. This is extremely important
            :onsidering that SMEs may represent as much as three quarters of total employment in
            Mexico, and 40 percent of the active population works in the informal sector. Specific
            measures will have major positive impacts as follows: (1) Measures aimed at reducing costs
            of business entry and regulatory compliance are expected to reduce fixed costs and the
            burden of bribery; (2) Formalization of businesses should increase local government
            revenues, providing the fiscal space to municipalities to increase social investments, and
            increase employees' access to social security, training opportunities, and pensions; (3)
            Improved application of the anti-trust law at a decentralized level will also contribute to
            improve the business environment for small businessesand their opportunities in the market:
            (4) Improved efficiency of customs and ports will reduce corruption and benefit businesses
            interested in improving their delivery time.

            The actions supported by the loan should have a direct and positive impact on the level and
            the quality of employment of skilled labor. Increased issuance of secondary educatior
            certificates to adults has the potential to increase incomes: household heads with a secondarj
            education earn, on average, roughly 40 percent more than those without. Increased trade i s
            expected to bring long-term benefits in terms of competitiveness, economic growth, anc
            employment generation. In the energy sector, increasing transparency and accountabilitj
            should have positive effects on the efficiency and the quality of service delivery.
Risks       The proposed program faces two main risks, one political and one institutional. The politica
            risk is associated with the presidential elections planned for July 2006. While the progran
            has developed a significant sense of ownership and commitment among the differen
            stakeholders, a new administration may choose to shift priorities within or away from the
            program. As a mitigation strategy, the second loan i s planned for mid 2007 in order to
            provide enough opportunity for the Bank to establish a dialogue with the new administration
            on the competitiveness program. The choice of instrument also reduces the political risk: a
            single-tranche DPL in a medium-term framework allows adaptation to changes on the
            ground. Another mitigating factor is that the second phase of the Mexico Competitiveness
            AAA will be completed by early 2006 and will provide an opportunity and basis for
            engagement with the various stakeholders and the new administration on the proposed
            competitiveness-enhancing reforms.

            A further political risk mitigation strategy has been to include the private sector in the
            formulation of the country's competitiveness priorities. Engagement with the private sector
            has meant that the GOM's Competitiveness Agenda and the areas that the DPL addresses are
            in line with the constraints perceived by the private sector. This broad public consensus
            around the bases of the program decreases the risk that the next administration will shift
            priorities. Lastly, political risk i s also limited by not including major labor and energy
            structural reforms that are politically sensitive and could engender opposition from one or
            more of the major political parties. I n this sense, the Bank-supported actions are non-
            controversial. An alternative, to avoid the political risk of the transition, is to only continue
            engagement on competitiveness through the AAA. However, this alternative has been ruled
            out since it does not provide continuity to existing programs or promote a medium term
            holistic, cross-governmental program for improving productivity.

            Institutional coordination issues also present a risk. A competitiveness program i s
            necessarily a broad operation that involves many secretariats and agencies and there is a risk
            that all of these agencies could have difficulties coordinating among themselves. This risk
            again, has been mitigated by the choice as lead counterpart in the GOM of the Secretariat oj


                                                ...
                                                111

              Economy, which has the technical credibility and has proved to be a capable coordinator
              during preparation. Pastexperiencefrom elsewhere also suggests that this design can work.

              Mexico faces macroeconomic risks related to a possible deterioration of the currently
              favorable external environment. Debt Sustainability Analysis shows Mexico's overall
              resilience to plausible shocks. According to the analysis performed by the IMF as part of its
              2005 Article IV Consultation, standardized stress tests, based on the historical volatility of
              key macroeconomic variables for the past 10 years, indicate that the level of public debt
              would not increase substantially over the medium term if macro conditions turned out to be
              significantly worse than projected. However, the analysis also notes that more extreme
              shocks or a combination of shocks-such         as a sudden permanent decline in oil prices, a
              "hard landing" in the United States, or an arbitrary shock to market confidence in Mexican
              assets-would    result in a rise in the public sector financing requirement, reflecting the still
              relatively short average maturity of domestic debt. Aware of the potential threats to financial
              stability stemming from such shocks, the GOM has dedicated significant efforts to
              developing the domestic government bond market over the past few years and has made
              substantial progress, diversifying the investor base and extending the yield curve. Additional
              measuresto mitigate the risk of public debt refinancing have also been taken.

                                          I. INTRODUCTION

1.    Mexico has shown lackluster growth and productivity performance in recent years. Over the
last decade, Mexico has benefited from a relatively stable economy and has avoided the pattern of
financial crises that have plagued many of its past political cycles, thanks in large part to prudent fiscal
and monetary policies. Despite this stability, structural economic constraints have held back growth and
therefore poverty reduction. Growth rates have beenparticularly low over the last decade when compared
with similar income countries in East Asia. While real GDP per capita in Mexico only grew at an annual
rate of 1.2 percent between 1994 and 2004, it grew at 7.7 percent in China, and at 5.9 percent on average
inthe East Asia and Pacific region. Productivity growth rateshave also beendisappointing. Annual labor
productivity growth was negative in Mexico between 1990 and 2004 (Figure l),                    in sharp contrast to
strong positive growth rates in Chile and Argentina. The share of growth attributable to total factor
productivity was also negative on average between 1980 and 2003, although there has been a slight
increase inrecent years (Figure2).

 2.    Given the productivity numbers, it is not surprising that comprehensive competitiveness
 measures show Mexico to be weak relative to per capita income-and                       getting worse.' The World
 Economic Forum's 2005-2006 Global Competitiveness Report gives Mexico a rank of 55 out of 117




 'Competitivenessi  s broadly defined as the "collection of factors, policies and institutions which determine the level
 of productivity of a country and that, therefore, determine the level of prosperity that can be attained by an
 economy." World EconomicForum (2005), p.xiii.

countries, down from 48 in 2004.2In comparison, Mexico's GNIper capita rank within that same sample
of countries i s 39. While this gap between income and competitiveness rankings i s not atypical for Latin
America, it indicates that Mexico is falling well short of its potential. Incontrast, Chile, India, China, and
other East Asian economies enjoy competitiveness rankings that exceed their income placing (Figure 3).
The World Bank's Doing Business in 2006 reports similar findings. Mexico ranked 73 (out of 155
countries) on the publication's "Ease of DoingBusiness" scale, compared with an income per capitarank
of 38.

3.    A n often used (albeit imperfect) proxy for competition is export performance. On this basis,
the weak competitiveness of Mexico's economy would be reflected in the reduction in its share of
exports to the United States. Mexico i s highly dependent on the U.S. economy, which absorbs 88
percent of its total exports. After experiencing an export boom in the 1990s thanks to NAFI'A, Mexico
has been losing U.S. market share, particularly to China (Figure 4). Mexico's share of U.S. imports fell
from a high of 11.6percent in 2002 to 10.6 percent in 2004. During that same time period, the Chinese
share went from 10.8 percent to 13.4 percent. According to the governor of the Banco de Mhico, lost
competitiveness has caused the economy to lose an additional US$30 billion in exports to the U.S. and
has restrained economic growth. Particularly worrying i s the fact that Mexico i s losing U.S. market share
in heavy goods such as furniture and household appliances, in which it should have a transport cost
advantage due to its proximity to U.S. markets. While one concern i s that Mexico suffers from high labor
costs, this i s not the whole story. A recent World Bank analysis of the computers and peripherals market
found that while labor accounted for 4 percent of costs in China, it only accounted for 3 percent in
Mexico. In non-leather apparel, labor accounted for 15 percent of costs in China and 20 percent in
Mexico. Clearly, there are other factors impeding Mexican competitiveness.

4.    The Mexican Competitiveness Institute (IMCO) has also shown that the country is lagging.
Out of 45 economies that IMCO defines as Mexico's competitors, the country ranks 31, in the bottom
third, behind Brazil and Thailand among others. IMCO considers ten separate aspects to be critical to a
country's competitiveness, measuredas the ability of a country to attract and retain investment, local and


  This ranking is for the Growth Competitiveness Index. Two countries (Qatar and Kuwait) that were not ranked in
2004 placed ahead of Mexico in2005, accounting for a small part of Mexico's fall.


                                                                    2

foreign. Of those, Mexico falls particularly short on governanceand legal system indicators, foundational
or "precursor" sectors (transport, telecommunications, and financial services), efficient factor markets
(capital, labor, and energy), human capital (education, knowledge and innovation), and environmental
sustainability. Simulations by IMCO show that if Mexico were to improve by 10 percent in all the ten
aspects critical for competitiveness, investment could increase by as much as 60 percent. Among the ten
factors, five (efficient government, trade and international relations, human capital, infrastructure and
governance and legal system indicators) account for 70 of this in~rease.~

5.    Aware of this predicament, the Government of Mexico (GOM) in 2003 declared increasing
country competitiveness to be a key priority of the Fox Administration. This was a response to the
disappointing growth, poverty, inequality, export, and competitiveness indicators as well as concerns
about the country's ability to respond to the pressures imposed by advancing globalization, such as
competitionfrom China and the end of the textile quota systemin 2005.


                                    11.     COUNTRY CONTEXT

6.    A combination of fiscal discipline, prudent monetary policy and a positive international
environment characterized by an improved U.S. economy has fostered continuous economic
growth since mid-2003. The institutional strengthening of Mexican economic policymaking over the last
decade has been reflected in the avoidance of instability during the last presidential transition in 2000
and inthe avoidance of a crisis during the most recent slow down of the US.economy inthe 2001-2002
period. The ensuing recovery, begun in mid-2003, has been broad-based. Business confidence and
investment have risen, foreign direct investment inflows have strengthened, exports have picked up
sharply, and market perceptions of Mexico have remained favorable.

7.    Financial sector stability indicators have continued to improve due to stronger
macroeconomic indicators and continued upgrading of the legal and regulatory framework. Since
the 1994-1995 financial crisis, the two administrations have concentrated their efforts in the financial
system on improving the legal and regulatory framework, strengthening supervision, and fostering the
entrance of foreign financial institutions as the best bulwark against potential instabilities in the system
generatedby macroeconomic shocks. As a result of these initiatives, the main component of the financial
sector, the banking system, has cleaned up its balance sheet and improved its prudential indicators to
above international standards.

8.    Mexico's consistent macroeconomic management during the last decade has generated
confidence in international financial markets with respect to the avoidance of instability duringthe
upcoming electoral period. The period that led to the election of the current Administration broke the
cycle of financial instability that had been associated with previous presidential transitions in Mexico.
International markets reflect at this stage continued confidence in the country and the increased risk
premiumfor sovereign debt that had been observed in the run-up to previous elections contrasts with the
current evolution of Mexican sovereign debt spreads (Figure 5). In addition, as in 2000, Mexican
policymakers are in a position to take additional measures to reduce economic and market vulnerabilities
incase of adverse developments or prolongedpolitical uncertainty. Already, the GOMhas announcedthe
pre-fundingand prepayment of external public debt service obligations due in 2006 and 2007.

RECENTECONOMICDEVELOPMENTSINMEXICO
9.   Since mid-2003 the Mexican economy has expanded, amid a global economic recovery and
high international oil prices, but growth is now slowing. After a strong rebound in economic activity
in 2004-when     GDP rose 4.4 percent-the            economy expanded at a more moderate 3 percent rate of
growth in 2005. Global economic growth, high oil prices and export revenues, favorable external
financial conditions, and increased domestic credit stimulated aggregate demand in all of its major
categories-private consumption, investment, exports-raising               economic activity and employment.

10. Continued price stability and increasing domestic credit have also contributed to the
recovery. Price stability, public debt management and policies aimed at financial sector development
allowed for the steady growth of domestic financial savings, while fiscal consolidation reduced the claim
of the public sector on those savings, thereby allowing for a sound expansion in domestic credit to the
private sector. Since mid-2003, for the first time since the 1995 banking crisis, commercial banks'
outstanding credit to the private sector has grown in real terms, leading to higher private consumption
and business investment. In addition, the government managed to extend the domestic yield curve by
issuing longer term fixed rate bonds (up to 20 years), thereby creating an important benchmark for longer
term private sector credit contracts, including mortgage lending.

11. Inflation has come down after a substantial tightening of monetary policy. An increase in
consumer price headline inflation in 2004, mainly attributed to supply shocks in the energy and food
sectors, triggered a cycle of monetary policy tightening that lasted until mid -2005 and increased short-
term interest rates to almost 10percent (annual). Consumer price inflation was effectively brought down
from 5.2% year-on-year in December 2004 to 3.3 percent in December 2005, according to the target 3
(+/- 1) percent. The successful reduction of headline inflation and inflation expectations allowed the
central bank to ease monetary conditions gradually throughout the second half of 2005, bringingdown
short term interest rates to 8.5 percent.

12. The government has kept the deficit within its annual budget targets and in line with its
medium-term goal of fiscal consolidation. Higher than budgeted oil revenue has played an important
role in achieving fiscal targets, compensating for lower tax revenue and higher public investment and


                                                           4

greater revenue sharing with state and municipal governments. Oil-relatedbudget revenue has grown by a
total of almost 2.5 percentage points of GDP over the past three years, allowing the budget deficit to be
cut by almost half that amount, though substantially increasing public sector dependence on oil revenue,
which in2005 accounted for 40 percent of public revenue.

13. A modest external current account deficit has been maintained throughout the recovery due
to strong oil revenue and workers' remittances. The value of external trade resumed double-digit
growth in 2004 after its stagnation throughout the recessive 2001-2003 period. Mexican exports are
closely related to the evolution of U.S. industrial productionbut face increased international competition
as Mexico's share in U.S. imports lost ground over the past two years. Though the non-oil trade balance
i s widening from a deficit slightly under 4 percent of GDP two years ago to a deficit close to 6 percent
currently, the current account balance has kept a steady deficit at around US$8 billion or about 1percent
of GDP. In addition to rapidly increasing oil revenue, the current account is strengthened with strong and
growing household transfers or workers' remittances. These transfers are estimated at almost US$ 20
billion for 2005, or slightly over 2.5 percent of GDP, making up an important revenue stream in the
country's external accounts and inthe subsistence of poorer segments of Mexican society.

14. Mexico's savings rate, at about 19 percent of GDP, is on par with the rest of LatinAmerica,
but is insufficient to provide the investment needed to boost long-term growth (Table 1). Countries
that have been able to break out of low growth paths in the early stages of development, such as East
Asia and Chile, typically have done so with higher rates of investment in fixed capital formation. The
challenge for Mexico over the next 5 to 10 years will be to provide the institutional and legal
environment necessary to encourage more private investment, particularly in infrastructure and the
energy sector, where bottlenecks may constrain or even slow economic activity.

  15. Mexico has made progress in many human development indicators and extreme poverty
 reduction, but poverty and inequality remain serious issues. Income per capita (GNI per capita) i s
 US$6,790, among the highest in Latin America; life expectancy at birth has risen to 74 years; the under-
 five mortality rate dropped from 44.2 to 25 per thousand between 1990and 2003; about 89 percent of the
 population has access to clean water; and the literacy rate i s over 97 percent. However, despite important
 achievements in health and education and some progress in extreme poverty reduction over the last five
 years, low and inequitable growth has kept poverty rates high. The percentageof people living in extreme
 poverty was reduced from 24.2 percent in 2000 to 17.3 percent in 2005. Still, the Gini coefficient has
 hovered between 50 and 55, on par with Latin American averages. According to 2004 data, 47.7 percent



                                                      5

of the country's population is poor. The richest ten percent of the population earns over 40 percent of
total income, while the poorest ten percent earns only 1.1 percent.

MACROECONOMIC OUTLOOK AND DEBT SUSTAINABILITY
16. Mexico's macroeconomic framework is solid Progress in attaining macroeconomic stability is
starting to pay off, with economic growth stabilizing around the economy's current potential output.
Improvements in Mexico's sovereign credit risk ratings are another reflection of such progress and
providereasonable assurance of continued adequateaccess to external finance, even at times of increased
market volatility.

 17. Structural economic and competitiveness constraints and the prevailing legislative gridlock
have reduced Mexico's potential output growth to slightly over 3 percent per year over the
medium term. In addition, the favorable external conditions are unlikely to prevail over the next few
years and the government's projection of 3.6 percent average annual GDP growth for 2006-08 may thus
be difficult to attain. If the resolution of global imbalances were to result in a sharp slowdown in US
growth, there is a risk that the Mexican economy could be adversely affected. Other factors such as
slightly lower oil prices, higher international interest rates, and political uncertainty related to the
presidential elections and change in administration are likely to lead to a more moderate economic
expansion over the next few years, with annual average growth of about 3.0 percent.

 18.   The government's medium-term fiscal framework aims to further advance fiscal
consolidation and reduce public debt. The government's 2006 budget, presented in September, updated
its medium-term fiscal projections for 2006-2009. The projections include ambitious targets for the
budget balance, with a small surplus in 2006, and a drop in off-budget financing. The fiscal consolidation
envisaged inthis framework would reduce net augmented debt to about 32 percent of GDP by 2009, from
41 percent in 2004. Even though this goal i s challenging, the debt-to-GDP ratio remains clearly on a
 sustainable, downward path.




                                                          6

19. Public debt management has diminished the exposure to refinancing, interest and exchange
rate risk. A consistent implementation of the public debt management strategy aimed at reducing
vulnerabilities and long-term financing costs has led to a significant strengthening of the government's
debt structure. The increasing use of longer term fixed rate peso bonds has contributed to an important
increase in the average maturity of domestic public debt. A gradual reduction of external debt, by at least
US$ 500 million a year, has also been part of the debt management strategy for a couple of years, to
reduce the country's exposure to potential market vulnerabilities and external shocks.

20.   Mexico has no Purchases and Loans outstanding with the IMF and maintains periodic
bilateral discussions as part of the Fund's regular surveillance program. The last Article IV
consultation was concluded by the IMFExecutive Board on November 9, 2005. The Executive Directors
commended the Mexican authorities for the improvements they had made in the economic policies,
institutions and structures over the last decade, which have contributed to greater macroeconomic
stability and reduced financial vulnerability. The challenge ahead will be to launch a new round of
structural reforms that will enhance the economy's efficiency and competitiveness and ensure the
transition to a more rapid growth path. The staff report, a Public Information Notice and a statement by
the Executive Director for Mexico have been published in December 2005 and are available on the
internet (www.imf.org).

21.     Debt Sustainability Analysis shows Mexico'soverall resilienceto plausible shocks. According
to the analysis performedby the IMFas part of its 2005 Article IV Consultation, standardized stress tests,
based on the historical volatility of key macroeconomic variables for the past 10 years, indicate that the
level of public debt would not increase substantially over the mediumterm if macro conditions turned out
significantly worse than projected. However, the analysis also notes that more extreme shocks or a
combination of shocks -such as a sudden permanent decline in oil prices, a "hard landing" in the United
States, or an arbitrary shock to market confidence in Mexican assets- would result in a rise in the public
sector financing requirement,reflecting the still relatively short average maturity of domestic debt.


                111.    BANKSUPPORT TO MEXICO'S COMPETITIVENESS

LINKTO CPS
22.   The Mexico Country Partnership Strategy (CPS)4supports a substantial agenda in the area
of competitiveness. The CPS - which sets poverty reduction as its overarching and ultimate objective -
seeks to improve competitiveness, as one of the aims of the Government's National Development Plan
that the CPS seeks to advance. The CPS proposed'a multi-year program of Analytic and Advisory
Activities (AAA) in the area of competitiveness and trade for FY05-FY08 that could lead to policy-based
programmatic operations, complemented by investment operations in innovation and technology, higher
education and lifelong learning, infrastructure both at the federal and state levels, and access to financial
 services and property rights.While a specific competitiveness DPL was not envisaged in the CPS, work
 on the AAA convinced the GOM and Bank team that such a loan would be an efficient way to support
Mexico's broad-based competitiveness agenda. The Competitiveness DPL i s complemented by more
 narrowly focused lending in housing, finance, highways, and innovation (see below).




  World Bank (2004d).

COLLABORATIONWITH THE IMFAND OTHER DONORS
23.   Fundand Bankstaff maintaina regularexchangeonmacropolicy.There is no IMFprogram in
Mexico. The Mexican government does maintain an active policy dialogue with the Fund, however,
through the Fund's bilateral and global surveillance activities. This summer, under Article IV of the
IMF's Articles of Agreement, the IMF held bilateral discussions with Mexican authorities which were
presented to the IMF Board in early November, as discussed above. No major issues or disagreements
were reported.

24. One of the Bank's main development partnersin Mexico, the Inter-AmericanDevelopment
Bank (IDB), is providingsupport in competitivenessinareascomplementaryto the proposedDPL.
The IDB has recently approved a US$lOO million loan for a Small Exporters Finance Program that
through BANCOMEXT, a public development bank, will support Mexican firms through financial and
advisory services. The IDB also has under implementation a US$600 million Public Financial System
Consolidation Policy Loan. This supports the implementation of reforms in the banlung system to
increase credit to SMEs and improve supervision of the regulated financial organizations, in addition to
supporting modifications to the regulatory framework for the capital markets and to facilitate the access
of SMEs to the capital markets. Inthe area of human capital and training, the US$50 million Multiphase
Skills-Based Human Resources Development Program supports the improvement of the quality and
efficiency of technical education in Mexico, by making technical education more pertinent with respect
to the needs of the productive sector. Through the US$300 million Labor Market Policy Program, the
IDB is supporting worker development, employability, mobility, and productivity in order to make the
workforce more competitive, and to develop capacity for planning, analysis, and programming of labor
sector market policies. The IDB i s also supporting, through the Multilateral Investment Fund and through
Non-Reimbursable Technical Cooperation, initiatives for risk financing for technology ventures, SME
support, formalization, and micro credit initiatives. The team has met with IDB staff to coordinate efforts
and to ensure that synergies are exploited.

25.   Other donorswho providesupportin areascomplementaryto the proposedDPL are the U.S.
Agency for InternationalDevelopment(USAID) and the Japan InternationalCooperationAgency
(JICA). USAID is supporting programs for broadening access to finance for micro-enterprises and has
been recently collaborating with the Bank's DoingBusiness department on implementing the
DoingBusiness indicators in 12 cities in Mexico. JICA has technical cooperation programs in technical
training and industrial development for SMEs. The OECD maintains a continuous dialogue on economic
analytical work and has recently undertaken a review of Mexico's competitionpolicy.


RELATIONSHIPTO OTHER BANKOPERATIONS
26.   A number of Bank operations serve as complements to this DPL (see Figure 7). The most closely
linked is the Programmatic Competitiveness and Trade AAA, which is structured in three phases.
Phase I(Ey05) built on pre-programmatic research, analysis, and consultations by supporting the
Competitiveness Agenda through separate pieces of analytical work at the GOM's request. Phase 11,
which i s scheduledto be completed in early 2006, consolidates what was learned from Phase I,    augments
it with additional analysis of agriculture, and brings the key competitiveness issues into the public
dialogue. Phase 111(FY07-08) will address the complex and politically complicated issues of structural
energy and labor reforms.

27. A Programmatic Finance and Growth DPL i s being developed concurrently with this DPL. It
will address access to finance issues which are a critical constraint to competitiveness and one of the
pillars of the GOM's Competitiveness Agenda. Specifically, it aims to minimize moral risk, strengthen


                                                      8

market integrity, promote diversified savings instruments, and increase the depth, access, and
diversification of financing. The Second Affordable Housing and Urban Poverty Reduction DPL to
Mexico is also a complement to this Competitiveness DPL. The housingDPL was presentedto the Board
in November and includes elements that will improve financial deepening, including housing credit,
household savings, and urban property registries.

28. This Competitiveness DPLbuilds on the Innovationfor Competitivenessloan to Mexicothat was
approved in May 2005. The first phase operation of the innovation loan has supply and demand
components, in addition to supporting the policy framework for, and internationalization of, Mexico's
innovation system. On the demand side, the program would increase support to business innovation,
while on the supply side, the program would accelerate the formation of human capital through a
scholarship fund.

29.  All of IFC's activities in Mexico are arguably linked to improving competitiveness. IFC
provides (i) support through direct financing in the financial sector, infrastructure, and manufacturing
and technology, and (ii)  technical assistance (TA) in the areas of sustainability and Business Enabling
Environment (BEE).On the TA side, BEE support consists of (a) the work relatedto the DoingBusiness
report, IFC's global initiative ranking selected categories of the business environment, and (b) Foreign
Investment Advisory Service (FIAS) sub-national, administrative barrier work, which is currently being
conducted in 12 Mexican cities. The latter is complemented by a similar IFC LAC Facility initiative to
devise a "municipal scorecard" on a regional level. Pilot programs to simplify municipal regulation and
licensing(operating licenses and construction permits) are planned for the next year.

30. The World Bank Institute is addressing competitiveness issues through dialogue with the
private sector. As a first step in the dialogue process, the WBI i s working with its partners inMexico to
organize a delivery of the CSR and Sustainable Competitiveness Course for an expected 500 business
representatives from Mexico, with a focus on the three main competitiveness issues. The two-month
course began inNovember 2005 and i s being held incollaboration with AmCham, ITESM and CEMEX.

31. The Bank's Infrastructure Public Expenditure Review (PER), completed in September 2005,
has informed the energy portion of this DPL.The Bank also has a number of education-related lending
programs in Mexico, which will boost competitiveness in the long run via a more skilled workforce.
These include a School-Based Management Adaptable Program Loan (APL), a Tertiary Education
Student Assistance APL, and a Basic Education APL the final phase of which was approved in2004. The
energy area of the Second Programmatic Environment DPL (approved in September 2005) i s
particularly relevant to the proposed Competitiveness DPL. It supports the development of a package of
legal reforms and regulations needed for the promotion of renewable energy sources. Since energy i s a
critical factor of production, ensuring a secure and sustainable energy supply is important to Mexico's
long-term ability to compete.


LESSONSLEARNED
32. Lessons learned come from a variety of lending operations and comprehensive reviews (e.g., from
Mexico, although in other sectors: the Housing DPL (HUSAL I)         and the Programmatic Environment
Structural Adjustment Loan; in competitiveness: the Brazil First Programmatic Loan For Sustainable
And Equitable Growth, the Peru Programmatic Decentralization and Competitiveness SAL I,and the
Tunisia: Economic Competitiveness Adjustment Loans I,11 and III;and overall the Development
Committee's Review of WorldBank Conditionality):

      (i)  Monitoring indicators are crucial to monitor progress, measure impact, and adjust the pace and
           content of the reform program. Monitoring indicators have been agreed upon with the
           Government of Mexico for each component of the Competitiveness DPL.
      (ii) Government ownership of policies i s crucial. This Competitiveness DPL has ensured
           government ownership from the start by working closely with each agency to craft the matrix
           actions and triggers.
      (iii) Clear and precise (measurable) loan conditions are necessary.The current DPL has taken this
           into account in designing the Bank-supportedpolicy matrix.
      (iv) A stable macroeconomic environment is necessaryfor competitiveness reforms to translate into
           economic growth. Mexico has enjoyed stable macro conditions over the past few years, making
           this a propitious time to undertake this Competitiveness DPL.
      (v) Large and complex policy matrices should be avoided, particularly in multisectoral operations
           such as competitiveness programs. For the current DPL, a short list of actions and triggers has
           been selected for support from a much broader set of government initiatives across the
           different policy areas.

the policy area descriptions in Section V and Annex 5. This evidence complements the IMCO impact

34. The GOM's 2004 Competitiveness Agenda, developed with Bank assistance and discussed in
Section lV below, provided the framework for the proposed DPL program. Subsequent analysis,
particularly in the preparation of this proposed loan and as part of the ongoing Programmatic AAA
(FY05-08) has confirmed that these areas are an appropriate focus. The following discussion considers
alternative ways of identifying obstacles to competitiveness, while Annex 5 contains a more detailed
examination broken down by factor markets and total factor productivity (TFP).

Growth Diagnostic
35.   One way to analyze the obstacles to competitiveness in Mexico, and hence the appropriateness
of the areas this DPL (and the Mexican agenda) supports, is through a "growth diagnostic"
conceptual framework. This framework, developed by Hausmann, Rodrik, and Velasco (2004), aims to
identify the bindingconstraints to growth. While the diagnostic has not been comprehensively applied to
Mexico, it does provide guidelines. The framework is based on the drivers of investment and uses a
decision tree to first ask whether growth is constrained by low returns on investment, the high cost of
finance (both local and international), or both. The cost of finance (reflected particularly in very limited
access) is a key issue in Mexico, considering that the share of credit to the private sector as a percentage
of GDP i s about 17 percent, while it is 63 percent in Chile, 98 percent in Korea, and over 100 percent in
many East Asian countries. This issue i s addressed through a separate but complementary DPL on the
financial sector.

36. Turningto the returns on capital, an analysis by IMCO shows that low returns are a good part of the
story. Based on an analysis of the 33 largest companies on the Mexican stock market (BMV), IMCO
found that, on average, profits did not cover the cost of capital (Figure 8). Specifically, only 6 of 14
sectors had returns that exceeded their cost of capital. Moreover even in absolute terms, the returns to
capital appear low. They are in the 5-12 percent range, which i s similar to the yield on government bonds
in Mexico. The growth diagnosis framework provides guidance into the critical issues that are keeping
those returns so low. And this DPL supports measures that ought to improve the returns to capital.

                        Figure 8: Cost of Capital and Returns on Capital inMexico

                                                        12

37.   The growth diagnostic framework goes on to identify a number of possible drivers to low
privatereturns on domestic investment: a) high micro or macro risks; b) lack of innovation and R&D;
c) large externalities, spillovers, or coordination failures; d) high tax rates and/or inefficient tax structure;
and e) insufficient infrastructure, high transport, energy, telecommunications or shipping costs (see
Annex 4 for a diagram of the framework). As discussed in section 11, the macro environment has been
stable in Mexico for the last decade, making it unlikely to be a binding constraint. Micro risks, such as
from corruption and crime, could potentially be binding constraints. Indeed, they came up in
competitiveness focus groups held in Mexico and the World Economic Forum's executive opinion
survey. However, they were not included in this DPL based on what could feasibly be accomplished
given the timeframe and current political environment. Other micro risks, such as property rights and
judicial reform (contractual enforcement, are handled through two other Bank operations-a                       housing
DPLand ajudicial reformproject. Lack of R&D / innovation is certainly an issueinMexico and is being
addressed in the first policy area of this DPL. Tax rates and regulations are seen as a constraint with
significant impact (Figure 9), but the issue i s politically off-limits given the failed tax reform of 2004.
Constraints associated with infrastructure and transport and shipping costs are addressedin the logistics,
trade facilitation, and energy components of this DPL.

                      Source: World Economic Forum (2005).

 Impact Analyses
 38.   A complement to the growth diagnostic framework is an impact study conducted by the
 Mexican Competitiveness Institute (IMCO). The IMCO (2005) study, which was part of the Bank
 A M work in Mexico, develops a model to assess Mexico's competitiveness and establishes priorities
 among themes in terms of impact on investment (gross capital formation) per worker. From that model,
 "point elasticities" are estimated, giving the impact on investment per worker of a ten percent
 improvement in the respective independent variables (Table 3). As shown, the high-impact themes are
 the competition environment, taxes and tax regulations, regulatory and investment climate, education and
 innovation, trade facilitation, transport and logistics, corruption, finance and energy. These impact
 estimates provide guidelines for priorities and thus selected areas of support for this DPL. In
 consequence, this DPL (and the Mexican Competitiveness Agenda) support improvements in most of
 those themes (see bolded items in the table).

39.   It should be noted that the impact numbers in Table 3 are for single effects or isolated
improvements in each category. Since the Bank i s supporting a broad package of reforms across most of
the high-impact categories, it is anticipated that there will be a positive bundlingeffect. In other words,
the interaction between the individual variables should create an overall impact that is
substantially larger than the sum of the individual improvements.

40. The conclusions of the impact elasticity calculations are supported by two other relatively
comprehensive reports: a Competitiveness Focus Groups report and the World Economic Forum's
(WEF) Global Competitiveness Report 2005-2006. The focus group report i s based on the written and
verbal responses of over 90 businesspeople in Mexico from a range of economic sectors in 2003. The
WEF report contains information about the most problematic factors for doing business in Mexico.These
sources are "soft" in the sense that they are based mostly on subjective evaluations of entrepreneurs,
while the IMCO analysis i s "hard" in the sense that, by and large, it uses hard factual data and provides
quantitative estimates of impact. It i s reassuring that the correlation between the hard and soft studies is
quite high (over 80 percent) (JMCO2005).

42.    While enhancing country competitiveness has been on the policy agenda from the outset, in
2003 the Fox administration declared this to be a key priority. The National Development Plan listed
increasing and extendingcountry competitiveness as the second of the five specific objectives in the area
of pursuing "growth with quality" (Table 4). However, from 2003 onwards the government has devoted
more attention to the issue, starting with the development of a Competitiveness Agenda, with Bank
assistance.

43.    The GOM's Competitiveness Agenda, completed in 2004, sets out key areas for policy action.
The agenda established five key areas for action (Table 5). These areas were chosen on the basis of their
impact on Mexico's competitiveness, the feasibility of implementation (that they only require executive
and federal level approval), andthe horizon for implementation.

 44.   This DPL would support four central elements of the GOM's competitiveness agenda. While
 the agenda does not includecertain high-impact but politically sensitive reforms such as structural reform
 in the energy sector (although it does include issues of pricing, incentives, and reliability of supply), tax



                                                          15

reform, and labor reform, it still provides an excellent starting point to address competitiveness issues in
Mexico and has served as a vehicle for dialogue with the private sector. Work on the Competitiveness
Agenda led to the ongoing Programmatic AAA (FY05-08).Analyses within the AAA and a Bank mission
in September 2005 have confirmed that the Competitiveness Agenda subject areas should indeed be
priorities. (See the Analytical Underpinnings section and Annex 5 below.) As a result, the DPL-
supported themes are based on the first four pillars of the Agenda, while the issue of financial deepening
i s addressed in the proposed Finance and Growth DPL, being prepared concurrently. Government
policies in the four areas of the proposed DPL are outlined below. Further detail, including a description
of specific measuresto be supported by the proposed operation i s providedin Section V.


INNOVATION, TECHNOLOGY           DEVELOPMENT, TRAINING
                                                   AND

45.   A key challenge for the country's competitiveness and trade is the limited involvement of the
private sector in innovation activities. This arguably contributes to the country's low level of high
value-added industrial production. The Government's strategy to boost innovation i s articulated in the
2001-2006 Special Plan for Science and Technology (PECyT), which is an integral part of the 2001-2006
National Development Plan. The PECyT defines a set of strategic priorities for the science and
technology (S&T) sector that address the sector's shortcomings by focusing on three overarching
objectives: 1) implement a coherent policy framework for S&T; 2) increase S&T capacity; and 3)
promote private sector competitiveness and innovation.

46.   Mexico enacted a new legal framework for the science and technology sector in 2002, creating
several new governing bodies. The new entities define and coordinate policies across federal, state, and
private sector stakeholders. Decentralization, information and networking are important elements of the
new institutional setup. An important decentralization mechanism that helps coordinate policies at the
federal and state level is the Consejos Estatales de Ciencia y Tecnologia.At the moment 25 Consejos
have been createdand 7 more are underway.

47.   There has been a reorientation in the allocation of resources. Even though public resources for
science and technology have not increased significantly, or enough to close the gap between Mexico and
other OECD countries, the PECyT highlights a structural change regardingthe use of resources, along
the following lines:
o Giving preferential support to projects that: i)increase the competitiveness of productive sectors and
    generate linkages among firms, universities and research centers; ii)have an impact on regional
    development and contribute to the decentralization of S&T activities; and iii)address social
    problems.
o Consolidation of a base of highly qualified human capital by: i)strengthening doctoral and
    postdoctoral quality programs; ii)refocusing the scholarship program to better address the demands
    of the productive sector; and iii)providing additional monetary incentives to top researchers and
    technologists both in the public and the private sector.

48.   Several National Science and Technology Council (CONACYT) programs provide incentives
for private sector competitiveness and business innovation. CONACYT' s                      work plan has
progressively shifted away from supporting pure research toward developing applications that will help
firms become more productive. Since 2001, a series of support programs have been launched to address
different needs in the innovation strategy of firms, including sectoral and mixed funds and fiscal
incentives. The sectoral funds are in collaboration with federal ministries and the mixed funds are with
state or municipal governments. They both provide transfers to established and start-up companies on the
basis of project proposals selected through competitive mechanisms. In the fiscal incentives program,


                                                     16

firms that can claim up to 30 percent of their annual investment in R&D projects are given tax credits.
Other key programs include public-private innovation consortia and research networks, venture capital
and seed capital initiatives, scholarships for researchers to work in firms, and firm incubators and
accelerators.

49.   There is a proliferation of relatively small SME support programs. Over the last 15 years,
approximately 131 federal programs have been createdto support small and medium enterprises (SMEs),
16 of which programs provide subsidizedtraining to workers or entrepreneurs. Most of these programs
seek to improve the productivity and competitiveness of small enterprises by encouraging technology
upgradingand training. The 10 largest programs reported in the National Survey of Employment, Wages,
Technology and Training (ENESTYC) finance needs assessment, training and consulting services or
knowledge-sharing service^.^ Despite several differences among programs, they share the common goal
of subsidizing support services for SMEs, on a cost-sharing basis, focusing on improving the
performance of individual firms or groups of firms.

50. The CIMO-PAC training program administered by the STPS is the largest in Mexico. The
program provides training services to approximately 332,000 employed workers per year on a cost-
sharing basis with the employer.      Due to budget cuts and lower efficiency, the number of firms
participating in the program has declined sharply, from an estimated 94,000 firms in 2001 to 7,000 in
2005.6 The firms benefiting from the program are preponderantly micro-enterprises (70%) concentrated
in the manufacturing sector (49%) and in service industries(41%). The potential positive impact of the
program on firm productivity i s minimal due to low efficiency, as indicatedby overhead figures close to
60 percent, and scale. To address this challenge, the Government is committed to carrying out a
systematic impact evaluation of the program covering the three years of operations (2002-2004) and
using the results of this evaluation to improve program efficiency prior to expanding program coverage.
These actions are supported by an IDB loan.7

51.   Mexico has an efficient adult education program funded by the National Institute of Adult
Education (Znstituto Nacional de Educacio'n para 10s Adultos-WA).        INEAhas modernized its out-of-
school education program during its 23 years of operation and now utilizes a pedagogic approach based
on modules that are flexible and competency-based. The Institute introduced information technology to
expand the coverage of its programs and has a large and decentralized operating infrastructure consisting
of 445 regional centers, 34,000 learning points, 29,000 learning cycles and 2,500 community learning
centers (Plazas Comunitarias).




  The SME programsreportedinthe 2001 ENESTYC survey by year of creation and supervisory agency are:
