                                                     17

INVESTMENT CLIMATE: REGULATORY                 IMPROVEMENT AND COMPETITION

52.   The government has undertaken various actions aimed at regulatory improvement. The
Federal Regulatory Improvement Commission (COFEMER) was created in 2000 to coordinate the
government's Regulatory Improvement Program, ensure the transparency of the regulatory process, and
promote regulations that produce benefits greater than the costs. Mexico i s one of the few developing
countries that created a mechanism to challenge the quality of new regulation through the establishment
of mandatory Regulatory Impact Assessments (RIA) for all new regulationthat imposes costs on citizens.
COFEMER plays the key role of systematically reviewing RIAs and regulatory improvement proposals.
COFEiMER also provides support to states and municipalities ranging from technical assistance in
establishing registries of formalities to conducting reviews of regulatory authorities to designing
enforcement schemes. It is also helping implement system to simplify business opening procedures in
municipalities around the country.

53. Regulatory planning activities are underway. Regulatory Betterment Programs (RBP),
mechanisms that give the public an opportunity to participate in regulatory planning, were created in
2000. At least every two years, Ministries and regulators must prepare a RBP to inform the public of
their plans for changes to regulations and formalities. The RBPs help to achieve the central purpose of
the federal regulatory improvement policy: to create and to modify regulations and formalities according
to processes based on planning, transparency, analysis potential effects, and public consultation, in order
to obtain the highest social benefit. A regulatory moratorium introduced in 2004 also helps improve the
quality of regulation as it requires new federal regulations to demonstrate benefits that significantly
outweigh costs. (See Annex 6 for a more detailed description of regulatory improvement programs.)

54.   Competition policy and the role of the Federal Competition Commission (CFC) are being
strengthened. The CFC i s providing technical support to legislative proposals that could amend the
Federal Competition Law in order to improve CFC's ability to address anticompetitive conditions
effectively and efficiently. One major aspect of this amendment would be to avoid excessive legal
challenges by dissatisfied parties against the resolutions issued by the CFC. Other challenges to be
addressed by the amendment to the Federal Competition law include expanding the CFC's ability to
divest monopolies, undertake searches, implement leniency programs, and order preventive measures.

55. The CFC has started a process of strengthening its internal procedures. It has undertaken an
internal restructuring aimed at reallocating resources to operative areas that are responsible for major
procedures. It i s also prioritizing investigations and deepening the analysis. To maximize the probability
that CFC resolutions are ratified by courts, it is developing a comprehensive strategy for each
investigation. It i s also outsourcing the study of industries or sectors requiring a high level of
specialization to external experts or consultants.

56. The CFC is also increasing its cooperation with other institutions. Inter-agency agreements are
being signed to establish concrete mechanisms for collaboration. The CFC is also signing agreements
with state and municipal officials and local economic agents to provide training, technical assistanceand
registry information to better identify practices that limit competition. The CFC i s also increasing support
for competitionpolicy by improving communication with consumers, academia, entrepreneurs, investors,
and public officials.




                                                      18

LOGISTICS TRADEFACILITATION
             AND

57.    The government has taken a number of actions over the past five years to lower trade costs.
Mexico's tariff have been harmonized and reduced on a number of products.' Import procedures and non-
tariff technical requirements have been eliminated or streamlined under a number of programs already in
place, such as the maquiladoras and PITEX (Programa de Importacidn Temporal para Producir
Articulos de Exportacidn), which allows duty-free entry for temporary imports of raw materials, parts,
machinery and equipment usedto manufacture exports. Action has also been taken to further leverage the
use of information technology to lower trade transactions cost for firms through government programs to
expand the use of electronic documents for import control requirements. One example is electronic
transmission of certificates of origin.

58.    Participation in international organizations has been another way to facilitate trade. Mexico
i s involved in the World Trade Organization (WTO) negotiations in the Doha Agenda. The government
has also strengthened its implementation of commitments made in the Asia Pacific Economic
Cooperation (APEC) to meet the goal of a five percent reduction in trade transactions costs made as part
of the APEC action plan. According to the APEC 2004 Individual Action Plan, Mexico selected the
actions and measures that will be implemented at the federal level in four key areas: movement of goods,
mobility of business people, standardsand conformity, and electronic commerce.

59.    Significant efforts have been made to improve customs, border crossings, and transport
infrastructure. An electronic customs management system has been launched. It encompasses payment
of customs duties, deposit certificates, cargo shipping lines manifests, certain carriers' documents, and
electronic transmission of certain government permits and certificates, such as sanitary and phytosanitary
requirements. The system is to be completed in several stages, in which different control agencies are
progressively incorporated. Many of the main national highway corridors have been modernized, the
trucking industry was deregulated in 1989, and ports and railways were concessioned in 1993 and 1997
respectively. Ports improved their performance significantly through the implementation of the
Administraciones Portuarias Integrales (APIs), landlord agencies responsible for the organization of port
operations and development. The active participation of the private sector as terminal operators under
APIs administration allowed for US$ 3 billion of investment in the last decade and a substantial
enhancement of operations performance. Other relevant projects have been a new initiative to coordinate
actors in multimodal corridors, launched in 2004, and efforts to develop private sector logistics
processes, such as the promotion of truck fleet modernization and the enhancement of SMEs logistics
organization.


ENERGY

60. Mexico has adopted a strategy to strengthen and modernizethe energy sector. Specifically, the
Energy Sector Program 2000-2006 (PROSENER) includes measures to strengthen the technical,
 economic, financial and legal aspects of the different energy sub-sectors. In electricity, it places special
 emphasis on: (i)   fuel share diversification and security-of-supply measures (e.g., increasing the share of
 renewables and other sources); (ii)       energy efficiency and conservation; (iii)finding ways to increase
 private sector participation and, (iv) increasing commercial integration with regional energy markets. In
 the hydrocarbons sector, PROSENER is mostly concerned with the modernization of the oil industry
 includingthe transformation of large industrial complexes, and the introduction of incentives to increase
 the efficiency and competitiveness of the sector. Inparticular, the program emphasizes the need to adopt


   For example, the dispersionof tariff rates was reducedfrom 14to 8 levels.


                                                          19

international standards for project planning, management and development, as well as for service and
product quality.

Regulation is being improved through an agreement between COFEMER and the Secretariat of
Energy (SENER). The 2001 agreement includes measurements to modify regulatory frameworks and
ensure transparent regulatory accounting and public disclosure. To some extent, the COFEMER
agreement complements the Energy Regulatory Commission (CRE) actions on regulatory accounting and
oversight.


     V.       THE PROPOSEDFIRSTPROGRAMMATICCOMPETITIVENESSDPL

OPERATIONDESCRIPTION
61.  Buildingon the support provided through the Competitiveness and Trade AAA and other lending
operations included in the Mexico CPS, the proposed Programmatic Competitiveness Development
Policy Loan (DPL) will support faster growth and poverty alleviation in Mexico. More specifically, the
proposed Programmatic Competitiveness DPL will:

    1. Build on past competitiveness efforts, strengthen new and existing programs, and promote a
        holistic, cross-governmental approach to improve productivity, accelerate growth, and increase
        exports and employment levels;
    2. Contribute to the formulation of a Medium-Long Term Competitiveness policy agenda; and help
        lay a solid foundation of competitiveness programs in advance of the next administration, which
        takes office in December 2006.

62. The World Bank would support the achievement of the overall objective through a three phase
Programmatic Competitiveness DPL. The proposed first phase (loan amount of US$300.76 million)
would support policy and institutional reforms in the following four areas:

    a. Innovation, technology development, and training: promote business innovation and improve
        training andeducation levels of the workforce.
    b. Investment Climate: Improve regulation to facilitate new business entry and reduce transactions
        costs for businessesand individuals; strengthen the economic competition policy.
    c. Logistics and tradefacilitation: streamline trade procedures, lower trade costs, facilitate customs
        clearance, and improve the quality of port services.
    d. Energy: implement transparency measuresand regulatory instruments.
Subject to overall satisfactory program implementation, particularly with respect to the indicative actions
that are outlined as triggers in each of the policy areas below, the second phase of the DPL would be
presentedto the Board in mid-2007, allowing the new administration a semester to transition into office.
A continuation of Mexico's satisfactory macroeconomic framework will also be a necessary part of the
context for proceeding with a secondDPL.


POLICY AREAS
 63. This section examines the issues behind each of the four DPL policy areas, building on the
 discussion of relevant government policy in Section IV. A matrix that describes the Bank-supported
prior actions and triggers for the second DPL can be found in Annex 2. This matrix has been developed
through very productive dialogue with the authorities and has resulted in a significant level of ownership
by the GOM. Annex 2 also contains indicators that are usefulfor monitoring the success of the program.
 Annex 3 contains the broader government program matrix, of which the bank-supported actions are a



                                                    20

subset. Annex 6 contains more detailed descriptions of the policy areas, including relevance, challenges,
and government actions. For energy, this information i s contained in Annex 7.


POLICY AREA1: INNOVATION, TECHNOLOGY                 DEVELOPMENT, TRAINING
                                                                       AND
64. Labor productivity, and hence purchasing power and standards of living, are largely determinedby
a person's knowledge and skills and the tools at his or her disposal. Knowledge and skills can be acquired
through education and training. This DPL focuses on adult education and SMEtraining programs, as well
as improving productivity by using technology development and innovation to enhance the tools and
knowledge available to workers. This loan does not address shortcomings in primary, secondary, and
tertiary education, but leaves these issues to separate lending programs, including the Basic Education
Adaptable Program Loan (APL), the Tertiary Education Student Assistance APL, and the School-Based
Management APL.


INNOVATIONAND TECHNOLOGY              DEVELOPMENT

Description and Challenges
65.    Innovation is a major driver of economic growth and productivity. Roughly half of cross-
country differences in per capita income and growth are driven by differences in total factor productivity,
generally attributed to technological development and innovative capacity.' These should be understood
ina broad sense, referring not only to the ability to engage inresearchanddevelopment (R&D) activities,
which eventually may or may not be transformed into new outputs, but also to the efficient use of modem
operating technologies and to the adoption and adaptation of new ones. Using firm level data from
investment climate surveys, Escribano and Guasch (2005a, 2005b) investigate the determinants of
productivity using a wide array of variables in four broad categories: red tape, corruption and crime;
infrastructure; quality, innovation and labor skills; and corporate governance. They find that in most
countries of Latin America and Asia covered by their study, variables related to innovation, quality and
training are important determinants of productivity. Lederman and Saenz (2003) present econometric
evidence that suggests that innovation outcomes, specifically patents per capita, are an important
explanation of the levels of development observed around the world.

66.    Social returns to R&D investment can exceed private returns. Knowledge spillovers from R&D
can improve other companies' products and production techniques through technology adoption and
learning, causing R&D returns to increase several fold. Lederman and Maloney (2004), using data from a
panel of countries, estimate that the social returns to R&D exceed the return to investments in physical
capital by a factor of 6 to 10 depending upon the initial level of income per capita. For Mexico, the
calculations suggest a social return to R&D above 60 percent.

67.    The Mexican innovation system performs below par compared with economies of a similar
income level, with a low ability to translate investments into innovation. Lederman et a1 (2003)
 analyze two key indicators of innovation performance-total      factor productivity (TFP) growth and the
number of patents produced-and           conclude that Mexico is currently under-performing in both
 dimensions when compared with other Latin American, East Asian and OECD countries. Dahlman and
 Kuznetsov (2005) construct knowledge economy indexes for Mexico and find that its relatively low
 rankingis due to weaknesses in all four knowledge economy pillars, namely economic incentives regime,



   Hall and Jones (1999),Dollar and Wolf (1997).


                                                      21

education, innovation, and information and communication technologies. The low performance with
regards to innovation stems from a combination of factors (see Annex 6 for details on these factors):
        Insufficient private sector involvement in innovationactivities;
        Total R&D investment, public and private, is also far below OECD averages;
        High cost, excessive economic risk, and lack of access to finance are the most important
        limitations to innovationaccording to Mexican firms;
        Low levels of cooperation in the development of new products and processes;
        Weak environment for entrepreneurship resulting in few potential high-growth start-ups suitable
        for venture capital funding;
        Low science and technology skill base;
        Inadequate linkages between public R&D and the productive sector;
        Limited internationalparticipation and linkages; and
        Heavy reliance on foreign technology.


Measures to be Undertakenas Part of the Proposed Operation
68.   To promote business technology and innovation, the Mexican Government relies on a portfolio of
programs and instruments that provide different incentives and address different needs for firms at
different stages of the innovation process. Some of these programs have been operating for two to three
years; others have recently been launched or are inthe process of design. The Bank i s supporting a subset
of these programs as prior actions for this DPL and triggers for the second DPL. The matrix in Annex 2
summarizes the Bank-supported actions and indicators, while Annex 3 contains the main government
actions. Annex 6 describes the full government programand measures supported in more detail.

        (i.)Prior     actions: Promote technological innovation (scale-up existing and launch new
              innovation programs to promote technology absorption and high-value added technology-
              based business initiatives) through launching and operating Program AVANCE,
              comprising UltimaMilla and Emprendedores (Venture Capital Fund) initiatives; creating a
              National Business Incubation System (SNIE) and Acceleration Program; and launching an
              internet portal for the SNIE in Spanish and English.

        (ii.)Triggers    for Phase 2: Promote technological innovation by conducting performance
              evaluations of the CONACYT and Under Secretariat for SMEs related programs, with the
              aim to consolidate funding, increase effectiveness and reduce non-performing funds; and
              initiating programs to improve linkages between the science community and businesses,
              including the Program to incorporate Scientists, Technologists and High-Level Human
              Resources in Industry; Consortia and technology networks program (public-private
              partnerships).

69. Ultima Milla (Last Mile) is a Pre-CompetitiveFundthat targets firms and R&D institutions. On the
one hand, firms undertaking basic or applied research or technological development can receive support
to develop: i)the last round of prototypes before market introduction and final tests to determine
industrial scale of production and investment; ii)the business and marketing plan and other studies to
evaluate the economic and technical feasibility of the investment; and iii)the requirements necessary for
the protection of industrial property rights. On the other hand, universities and other public or private
R&D institutions will be supported to carry out pre-competitive studies and patent processing, and to hire
technical assistance (Promotores de Negocio) to exploit commercially the results of their research. The



                                                     22

other component of AVANCE, the Venture Capital Fund known as Programa Emprendedores, aims to
helpstart and strengthen new technology basedbusinesses, including market introduction and scaling up,
through investment in capital funds and guarantee funds constituted by financial institutions with legal
status.

70. The Program to incorporate Scientists, Technologists and High-Level Human Resources in
Industry provides scholarships to firms to hire highly qualified human resources to develop innovation
initiatives or to identify, describe, and implement new R&D proposals. The consortia and networks
program supports linkages between groups of enterprises with existing R&D absorption capacity and
public R&D centers. Each consortium has to define a portfolio of R&D projects, which are being carried
out in collaborative fashion with public research centers according to their capabilities. Incubators are
physical or virtual centers that provide information, training and technical assistanceto create, evaluate
and implement business plans. The objective of the business accelerators i s to facilitate the rapid
growth of Mexican technology-based micro and SMEs through strategic alliances, access to international
venture capital and global markets penetration.



TRAINING

Description and Challenges
71.    There is a need for more efficient S M E support programs, with particular emphasis on
training programs for employed workers. This line of policy development addresses three main issues:
(a) small and medium enterprises (SMEs) represent a significant share of the Mexican economy; (b) the
public sector operates a plethora of relatively small programs of uncertain effectiveness in support of
SMEs; and (c) there is a lack of rigorous cost-benefit analyses of existing programs. SMEs make up 99
percent of enterprises in Mexico, employ 64 percent of the workforce and account for over 40 percent of
GDP (Economic Census 1999). These firms face many difficulties, perform below the level of their
larger counterparts, and rarely export their goods and services." An assessment by the Secretariat of
Economy (SE 2001) identified lack of access to financing and skills as the main weakness of SMEs. The
mortality rate of SMEs is high-200,000          SME are created every year but only 70,000 (35 percent)
 survive two years later. The firms that fail report lack of training and expertise as key reasons for their
failure.

 72.    The extent to which S M E programs reach their objectives i s unknown due to a lack of
 rigorous impact evaluations. The evaluations carried out by program supervisory agencies tend to be
 qualitative and small in scope, measuring beneficiary satisfaction or reporting coverage indicators (i.e.
 they are monitoring studies but not impact evaluations.) Few studies measure net programbenefits. Two
 exceptions to this norm are the CIMO-PAC programevaluations" carried out by the Secretariat of Labor
 and Social Security (STPS) in 1995 and 1997 using quasi-experimental designs. But even these failed to
 account for biases from unobserved firm heterogeneity and self-selection (L6pez-Acevedo 2004-1). The
 lack of reliable evaluation limits the capacity of policymakers to determine program effectiveness and to
 assess the gains that might accrue from consolidatingprograms within or across agencies.'*



 lo The 1999Economic Census reports that only 6.7 percentof SMEs export their goods and services.
 l1Programof ComprehensiveQuality and Modernization(CIMO) re-namedin2001"Proavarna de Auoyo a la
 Capacitacidn-PAC."
 l2 Rigorous impact evaluation requires firm-level panel data on both reasonably sized samples of program
 beneficiaries and a control group of non-participant firms with similar attributes covering pre- and post-program


                                                         23

73. Evaluations of CIMO-PAC show positive or mixed results. The empirical evidence collected in
1995 and 1997 by the STPS suggests that the CIMO-PAC program of integrated training and technical
assistance-as    originally designed, was effective in improving the performance`of micro enterprises and
SMEs.I3 Compared with the control group, participating firms increased investments in worker training,
had higher rates of capacity utilization, and were more likely to adopt quality-control practices. These
improved intermediate outcomes may be associatedwith increased productivity, but the original analyses
did not lead to this con~lusion.'~Re-examiningthe evidence, Lopez-Acevedo andTan (2004-1) show that
because less productive firms self-select in the CIMO-PAC program, the evaluation results were
inaccurate. Using a different methodology, the authors show that program impact is positive or mixed
when production functions are estimatedusingfirst-differences that control for the self-selection bias.

74.   In general, SME programs cover few firms, have eligibility criteria that are easily satisfied,
and vary widely as to the levels of subsidy offered.I5Most SMEs (70 percent) benefiting from the 131
federal programs listed by the CIPI are micro-enterprises, even though program targeting criteria do not
exclude larger size firms, and are concentrated in the services (58 percent) and manufacturing (30
percent) sectors.

75.   Adult education programs are necessary to raise the skill and knowledge levels of the
workforce. This policy line aims to reduce the education lag of the workforce by increasing the number
of 15 to 35 year olds with complete basic education (the equivalent of a 9" grade certificate), thereby
contributing to higher productivity and competitiveness. The average education attainment of the
Mexican adult population is very low-7.6         years-compared with 11-12 years for other OECD countries.
In the last decade, this gap has narrowed with regard to illiteracy and incompleteprimary education, but
continues to grow at the secondary level (grades 7, 8, and 9), reflecting poor retention capacity of public
secondary schools. In 2000, 14.9 million adults (24 percent) lacked secondary education even though
they had completed primary education. This means that a very large proportion of the workforce does not
have sufficient education to benefit from job-skills training or perform efficiently in the knowledge
economy.

76.   There is evidence that receiving a secondary education certificate leads to higher
employability and higher wages, suggesting higher productivity.16 With complete secondary
education the probability of being employed in the formal sector increased, on average, 13 percent for
men and 10 percent for women, and their hourly wage increasesby almost 70 percent for men (from 1.16
to 1.90 pesos/hr) and by 65 percent for women (from 1.89 to 3.11pesodhr). The impact on wages varies
depending on the assumptionsused, but recent simulations of potential private benefits derived from the

years, if a purposive sample is added to augment sample sizes of program beneficiaries to permit more statistically
robust impact evaluation studies. As a first step in the direction of cross-program evaluation studies the SE through
the CIPI completed a beneficiary database using information reported by all SME program administrators.

percent. This estimate is a low threshold since it does not take into account the possibility of INEA
graduates pursuingfurther education.

77. The National Institute of Adult Education's (INEA) programs appear to perform well. The
education model usedby INEA is very different from those designed for children and literacy campaigns.
INEAuses basic-competency learningmodules covering the secondary education curriculum (grades 7 to
9) and basic practical skills. The program is flexible in terms of schedule and number of modules
requiredto obtain the certification. According to the results of an entry test, students may be able to skip
modules that cover competencies they already have. Based on assessments of learning outcomes by the
National Evaluation Center for Higher Education (Centro Nacional de Evaluacidn de la Educacidn
Superior-CENEVAL),         there i s no significant difference between the quality of the lower secondary
education provided by INEA to out-of-school adults and that providedby the formal school system.

78.   More resources are needed to continue the expansion of adult education. Precise demand
estimates for INEA's secondary education certificates are not available. But the number of certificates
grew over the last 10 years by an average rate of 11.5 percent per year. INEA students come from poor
and vulnerable groups, such as women, youth and indigenous peoples (Schmelkes and Kaman 1996)."
This implies that INEA is tackling both inequity andpoverty issues through its adult education programs.
In order to expand program coverage, INEA would need additional fiscal resources, improved internal
efficiency and the introduction of incentives to increase effective demand." The Government i s already
pursuingsome of these initiatives, but more needs to be done.


Measures to be Undertakenas Part of the Proposed Operation
79. The Bank supports the Mexican Government policy of subsidizing training services for
SMEs. However, the historical proliferation of SMEs support services in Mexico and the absence of
reliable information on their effectiveness, suggests that it i s necessaryto take stock and formulate a new
and comprehensive strategy for supporting SMEs before considering program expansion. For the PAC
training program, the necessary elements neededto design such a strategy are: (a) reliable information on
SMEs demand for training; (b) information on the costs and benefits of the PAC; and (c) feasibility
analyses to identify more efficient delivery mechanisms. Consideration should be given to involving the
private sector as a service promoter and service provider while keeping the government as co-financier
and provider of program oversight and evaluation. Once an improved strategy to deliver training services
to SMEs is in place, it would be possible to plan for expansion. In parallel, the Bank supports the scaling
up of the INEA education programaimed at providing secondary education certificates for out-of-school
young adults enabling themto improve their performance inthe labor market.

         200,000 secondary education certificates during 2005 to out-of-school population aged 15 to 34
         years.

POLICYAREA2: INVESTMENT IMPROVEMENT          CLIMATE: REGULATORY                                    AND COMPETITION


REGULATORY            IMPROVEMENT

Description and Challenges
81.     Mexico needs to reduce its regulatory burden to improve productivity. Several studies
demonstrate how the regulatory burden in Mexico is still hinderingbusiness growth." The OECD, in its
most recent review2' of the effect of product market regulations on investment and growth found that
when regulation i s restrictive, productivity growth is generally below the OECD average.21As the OECD
points out, this does not imply that governments should dismantle regulations. Regulation i s necessary
for well-functioning market economies. But regulation may also contribute to impede competition with
detrimental consequences on resource allocation, innovation and productivity. The challenge for Mexico
i s to design regulations that help to safeguard the public good without compromising economic
performance. It i s an area where learning from best performers is crucial. The OECD estimates that if
those sectors that lag in terms of productivity were to modernize their regulatory framework and align it
on best practice, then productivity could increaseby as much as 10per cent.22

82. Benchmarking the cost of doing business in Mexico is telling as to where improvements can
be achieved through targeted regulatory improvement reforms. The "Doing Business" report of the
World Bank                  has benchmarked indicators of the cost of doing business in Mexico against more
that 150 countries around the world. The report covers entry regulations, licensing regulation,
employment regulations, registeringproperty, court efficiency, corporate governance, creditor rights and
credit information, bankruptcy and tax issues. Policy implications for Mexico are exceptionally
important: when benchmarked against other countries in the Latin American region, Mexico clearly lags
behind in several indicators, including a) time and cost to open a business (Figure 10); b) time and cost to
obtain a license; c) cost of hiring; and d) cost of tax compliance.

83.    The Mexican Institute for Competitiveness (IMCO) produced its own benchmarking report
in 2005, which concludes that the cost of the regulatory burden currently represents 15 percent of
Mexico's GDP, while the benefits from regulatory improvement could yield a 5 percent increasein GDP.
The IMCO and Doing Business reports show that the costs related to the complex regulatory framework,
non-transparent business procedures, and weak judicial structures remain factors inhibiting investment in
Mexico. They create indirect costs that impede Mexican manufacturers in their competition with other
countries, and translate intojob loss and growth reduction.

 84.   Lack of harmonization in the regulatory environment negatively impacts businesses. Since
regulatory responsibilities are shared among the federal, state and municipal level, coordination i s
 necessary to eliminate the overlaps, duplication and inconsistency with federal regulations. A 2003 study
 commissioned by the Business Coordination Council (CCE) showed marked differences in the quality of
 the regulatory framework among states.24On the positive side, improvements in some sub-national
 entities have attracted reforms in others, as has been the case with the Rapid Business Start-up System
 (SARE). Benchmarkingdifferent states and municipalities can spur this positive competition to improve
 regulations. The result of two state by state studies was released in December 2005 by IMCO and the
 World Bank's Foreign Investment Advisory Service (FIAS)/Doing Business.

 85. Mexico needs to undertake important regulatory reforms to boost its competitiveness.
 Specific sectors, especially in the manufacturing industry, are losing exports and even domestic market
 share to China and Latin American countries such as Honduras. While Mexico's labor wages cannot be
 reduced so as to compete with its competitors' low labor costs, the government needs to undertake
 actions to reduce the indirect costs of doing business. Specifically:

       (i)Proceduresthat impose largeburdensonentrepreneurs needto beeliminated, replacedor
            improved (starting a business, licensing, employment regulation, tax regulation);
       (ii)Businessinteractionwithgovernmentneedtobestreamlined,soastoreduceuncertaintyand
            implementation efficiency issues;
       (iii) marketregulations:IndustrysectorswhicharekeytoMexicancompetitivenessand
            Product
            under specific competition threat from foreign markets could benefit from a thorough review of
            administrative barriers in order to identify where action can be taken to reduce cost. Such
            sectors and issues could include foreign trade, manufacturing, property, finance, norms and
            standards, transport and telecommunications, energy and labor among others. For each sector
            or issue, reduction of time, procedures and costs of fulfillment would help improve
            competitiveness.


24CEESP. 2003. "Calidad del Marco Regulatorioen las Entidades Federativas.Estudio Comparativo Noviembre 2003."



                                                                  27

86.   The Federal Regulatory Improvement Commission (COFEMER) needs continuing support.
COFEMER can lead the above-mentioned tasks. But COFEMER can exercise its full powers only if it
enjoys broad support and acts decisively. One challenge is sustaining the support of the private sector
and official institutions. Earlier, rallied by the domestic crisis and strong political support, the private
sector took on an active role in pressuring agenciesto improve regulation. Today many factors contribute
to the more passive role of several public and private actors: the loss of founding members of the Council
through turnover and a review process in which success has become routine. Additionally, political
circumstances and a fragmented Congress have shifted the private sector's lobbying efforts from the
executive branch and its agencies toward Congress. This trend can only be counterbalanced by a
reinforcement of COFEMER's role vis-his the institutions issuing regulations. Inthat sense, ensuring
continuous training of public administration officials on the Regulatory Impact Assessment (RIA)
framework is key to maintain momentum while improving the upstream quality of proposed regulations.
The cornerstone of this effort lies in demonstrating the benefits of the RIA process in terms of cost
reduction to the entrepreneurs. In that regard, the establishment of an ex-post evaluation framework for
RIAs would help benchmark regulatory compliance of key administrations and create strong political
incentives for those administrations to streamline and simplify their regulatory environment.

87. Public consultation needs to be strengthened. Consultation in making, modifying or repealing
legislation and regulation in Mexico i s still weak. There is still no legal obligation to undertake active
public consultation for certain federal regulatory proposals. Nevertheless, COFEMER has included a
section in the RIA questionnaire concerning public consultation. Institutional bodies are requested to
specify if they established some type of public consultation and who was consulted about the proposals.
The RIA also requests a list of all the submissions that were taken into account for the draft regulation
that is beingpresented. But the input of the private sector when it comes to regulation affecting the cost
of doing business should be reinforced.

88.   Better harmonization of regulation between the different levels of jurisdiction is necessary.
Today, any regulatory improvement process, including red-tape reduction at the sub-federal level,
depends on states and municipalities' will and capacity to coordinate with each other. COFEMER,
through the SARE system, can play a key role in helping set up similar frameworks and systems between
jurisdictions. It i s crucial to deepen the voluntary relationship between COFEMER and the states and
municipalities. As a first step, it i s equally important to diagnose binding constraints at the state and
municipal level in order to identify the different sources of competitiveness loss of specific industries or
value chains. A benchmarking exercise between the states and municipalities could help identify best
practices and drive the regulatory cost reduction agenda in that regard.

Measures to be Undertakenas Part of the Proposed Operation
89. In order to address the issues mentioned above, the measures proposed are organized around key
action items, all pertaining to COFEMER's role in regulatory improvement. The matrix in Annex 2
summarizes the Bank-supported actions and indicators, while Annex 3 contains the full government
program matrix and Annex 6 contains more description and details of government actions.
    (i)Prior actions: Reducethe cost of doing businessthrough: implementing the SARE (Rapid
         Business Opening System) in 85 municipalities: and COFEMER's evaluation of 355 Regulatory
         Impact Assessments (RIAs) in 2005, with the objective of improving the cost-benefit analysis of
         regulation.




                                                     28

     (ii)Triggersfor Phase2: FacilitatenewbusinessentrybyincreasingtheSAREimplementationtotal
         to 100 municipalities, covering 40 percent of the country's GDP; and designinginstruments and
         a methodology to measurethe performance of the SARESand of the RIAs.

90.   Generalizing the implementation of the SARE system to reduce the time and cost of entry
regulations. The target i s for COFEMER to help implement the SARE system in more than 100
municipalities, representing more than 40 percent of Mexican GDP. The output i s to be measuredin the
start-up time for small and medium-size businesses (from an average of 52 days to 3 days only) and a
reduction in the associatedcost. The outcome i s to be measuredin the number of new companies created,
the value of investment in these entrepreneurial projects and the number of jobs created. Beyond the
numerical targets, COFEMER shall also create a system to measure SARE's effectiveness and monitor
its implementation in order to keep improving the processes and facilitate the introduction of the system
to new municipalities.

91. Reinforcing the Regulatory Impact Assessment process infederal institutions. This component
aims at generalizing the current RIA process among all agencies and institutions when applicable. This
includes raising the number of RIAs being conducted (400 in 2005) as well as the quality of the RIAs
being submitted for review. In that regard, training of public servants in impact assessment practices
remains crucial. Inorder to ensure compliance and accountability in the RIA process, enactment of the
by-laws of the Federal Administrative ProceduresLaw (Ley Federal de Procedimiento Administrativo) is
also important. The area where COFEMER needs to reinforce its RIA practice i s monitoring and
evaluation. The creation of a monitoring and evaluation framework on COFEMER's RIA process i s key
in that matter. For instance, COFEMER to date does not compare the cost it estimates for regulatory
proposals to actual cost, which could be measuredex-post. Similarly, COFEMER does not track how the
regulatory alternatives it suggested during the RIA process were actually applied and implemented.
Lastly, COFMER does not formally benchmark the quality of RIAS between the different secretariats
they originated from. A framework to achieve the above points should be put in place in the context of
this component in the medium term. The impact of such actions would be, over time, to reduce the cost
of doing business for Mexican entrepreneurs, especially compared with other Latin American countries,
with an emphasis on entry, licensing, tax and labor regulations.

92.    Increasing cooperation with sub-national jurisdictions and the private sector to keep
improving regulations at all levels. This component i s not part of the matrix but i s an important agenda
 item going forward for COFEMER. It aims at improving local competitiveness conditions by (i)
benchmarking the cost of doing business in at least 12 states in 2006, (ii)      facilitating the creation of
 competitiveness action plans at the sub-national level, and (iii)building an inventory of existing state and
 municipal regulatory improvement mechanisms. Inturn, these activities shall increase the quality of the
 systems usedby the municipalities (percentage of reduction of proceedings and procedures, improvement
 of administrative processes, response times, etc) and reduce the time and costs of business operations
 pertainingto entry regulation, property registry, contract enforcement, access to credit and others.


 COMPETITION

Description and Challenges
 93.   Competition, and the benefits it brings with respect to lower prices, greater variety and the
 development of new products, has been recognized as crucial for markets to work well and for
 increasing the wellbeing of a society. Policies that enhance competition are expected to bring not only
 static improvements in efficiency, especially in "disciplining" former monopolies, but also dynamic



                                                     29

improvements in efficiency, through improvements in productivity and innovation. Recent empirical
research indicates that regulation that lowers entry barriers and state control increases productivity and
technological inn~vation,~~stimulates business investment and increases long-run employment rates.26
Therefore, reforms to enhance competition could have significant effects on growth. A study of the
impact of pro-competitive regulatory reform in several industries in the United States found that annual
welfare gains in the part of GDP affected by reform were more than 7 percent, with 90 percent of the
benefits flowing to consumers.27Increasing competitive pressure can increase the probability of firm
innovationby more than 50 percent.28

94. OECD assessments show good competition policy but enforcement deficiencies in Mexico. An
OECD assessment of Mexico's competition law and policy completed in 199829concluded that the law
was well-conceived and that the CFC had strong enforcement powers and important authority to affect
direct market regulation by other agencies. There was, however, no clear base of support for competition
policy, and the CFC's enforcement record could be questioned. Since then, a new review by the OECD3'
confirmed that the strengths identifiedin the first review still pertain, but there were now no doubts about
the CFC's willingness to engage powerful economic interests. It also ascertained that the CFC had
matured into a credible and well-respected agency that had compiled a remarkable record of achievement
given the difficulties of its environment. It highlighted, however, the following challenges and
opportunities for improvement: (i)the limited degree of general support for competition policy; (ii)
certain deficiencies in statutory authority and judicial review processes constrain the CFC's ability to
address anticompetitive conditions effectively and efficiently; (iii)a decline in resources despite an
increasing workload; and (iv) some of the CFC's procedures and methods of interface with other
government entities reduce its efficacy as a law enforcement agency and competition advocate. As both
OECD assessments show, and despite having made progress in competition law and policy, there are
several aspects that still limit competition in Mexico. The OECD barriers to competition indicator that
measures legal barriers to competition and antitrust exemptions for public enterprises shows that this
index has deteriorated in Mexico and that the country has the highest barriers to competition among
OECD c~untries.~~

95. The challenges to effective competition in Mexico are reflected both in their impact on
investment and on the perceptions of entrepreneurs. According to IMCO's research on the impact of
10dimensions of competitiveness on investment for 45 economies, Mexico ranks 33 in terms of level of
competition and formality of the markets.32Furthermore, according to a recent governance survey, the
two largest market obstacles to business development identified by firms in Mexico are government
monopolies and private monopolie~.~~The Global Competition Review 2003 ranking of competition

regimes in 25 countries places Mexico near the bottom with a score of 2.25, in a rating from 0-5, only
above Greece (2.0) and Argentina (1.5) since Mexico's competition regime i s considered to be severely
undercut by courts.34

96.    One major limitation on CFC's action arises from two types of proceedings at the Federal
Judiciary that are brought by dissatisfied parties to seek judicial relief. These two proceedings, the
amparo actions ("protection appeals") at the Federal District Court and the fiscal nullity actions at the
Federal Administrative and Fiscal Justice                  can take many years to resolve and have proliferated
over the years. On average it takes 401 days for these suits to be resolved and between 1994 and 2004 the
number of suits (amparos and fiscal nullity) increased from 41 to 170. Even if CFC's orders survive the
amparos and fiscal nullity actions, the imposed fines are not collected by the CFC but rather by the fiscal
department of the municipality of residency of the fined party. From 1993 to September 2005, only 3.3
percent of the total amount fined by the CFC was collected, while 72 percent i s under reconsideration
and 20 percent i s under amparo and fiscal nullity action.

97. Another important limitation of the CFC is that it lacks many powers common to competition
commissions in other countries. Among these i s the ability to divest monopolies, undertake searches,
implement leniency programs, and order preventive measures. Due to limitations in the Competition
Law, the CFC i s not able to prosecute and divest or impose price controls on a dominant firm that
restricts supply and raises prices. Also, without the use of search and seizure authority, it is difficult for
the CFC to detect surreptitious collusion. The lack of a leniency program for the CFC restricts its ability
to provide reductions in monetary penalties to conspirators who voluntarily reveal collusive agreements.


98.    CFC has limited coordination with other regulators at the federal, state and municipal levels.
Given the powers of the many sectoral regulators, of other agencies such as COFEMER and PROFECO,
and of state and municipal entities, the CFC could improve its effectiveness by working more closely
with them. The CFC could benefit from monitoring the sectoral regulator's regulatory postings and
comment on them. Likewise, the CFC is not taking advantage of data available from other regulators,
such as PROFECO, that could be useful in detecting collusive behavior. This collaboration and taking
advantage of the possibility of exploiting synergies could also prove usefulgiven the declining budget of
the CFC. Although the numberof cases has increased, the CFC's budget has fallen 9 percent in realterms
between 2000 and 2004.

99.    Mexico needs to improve its competition policy through a variety of actions. Based on the
various reviews of competition law and policy in Mexico, empirical evidence, and assessment by the
Bank, actions that will increase the general support for competition policy, remove the constraints in
statutory authority andjudicial review processes, make more efficient use of CFC's declining resources,
and enhance CFC's collaboration with other government entities would be needed to improve CFC's
ability to address anticompetitive conditions and improve the effectiveness of competition policy.
According to IMCO's research, a 10 percent improvement in the variables that compose the
"competition" dimension could increase investment per worker inMexico in7.5 percent (Table 3).




34 Global Competition Review, Volume 6 Issue 6 June 2003.

and Fiscal Justice Court, which mostly considers tax cases, but also assertsjurisdiction to review any agency.

POLICY AREA3: LOGISTICS TRADE    AND          FACILITATION

Description and Challenges
101. Mexico hasbeenlosingU.S. marketshare, particularlyto China(Figure 4). Exports to the U.S.,
particularly since NAFTA, have been an important pillar of the Mexican economy and the US.absorbs
88 percent of total Mexican exports. Hence, this fall in exports has generated significant concern.

102. There are considerable potential gains to trade facilitation reform in Mexico. Based on
Wilson, Mann, and Otsuki (ZOOS),       Table 6 outlines Mexico's trade gains (increase in exports plus
imports) from an improvement to the world (75 countries) average on four trade facilitation indicators.
The largest increase in trade would come from domestic reforms to port efficiency and technology
infrastructure in Mexico ($29 billion and $18 billion respectively).

                    Table 6: Mexico's Trade Gains from Trade Facilitation Improvement
                     Port           customs        Regulatory       Infrastructure
                  Efficiency     Environment      Environment        (technology)        Total
   ($billion)        28.8             7.8              10.1              17.7              64.3



103. Evidence suggests that internal conditions are limiting Mexico's export growth. Hanson and
Robertson (2005) suggest that in the 1990s import-demand conditions in the United States and other
destination markets for Mexican exports were not the dominant factor constraining growth in Mexican




                                                    32

exports.36They use a gravity model of trade to develop a framework for decomposing Mexico's export
growth into components associated with export-supply capacity, import-demand conditions, and other
factors. The result shows weak growth in export-supply capabilities, even during the 1990s expansion.
The preliminary evidence suggests that internal conditions inMexico associatedwith its ability to expand
export-supply capabilities-areas      directly related to trade costs and facilitation-are      a more important
factor in limiting the country's export growth than China's expanded trade into the U.S.

104. Doing Business "trading across borders" indicators show that Mexico has much room for
improvement. This is especially the case when comparing Mexico with its NAFTA alliance partner,
Canada. For example, the number of signatures required for exports in Mexico i s 4, compared with 2 in
Canada and time for export is 18 days, while it i s 12 days in Canada. Signatures required in Mexico for
imports total 11 while only 1is required in Canada. The total time for import i s 26 days compared with
12days in Canada.

105. A variety of measures are needed to advance broad trade facilitation goals. They include: (1)
lowering and harmonizingtariffs levels on imports for domestic firms to further reduce barriers for firms
in acquiring lower cost inputs for final prod~ction;~~(2) expanding use of information technology in
government activities related to imports and exports to lower trade costs for firms; (3) continued
streamlining of regulatory requirements for firms, includingthose in the maquiladora and PZTEX, sector
to lower trade costs; and (4) lowering logistics costs. Government actions to address these are discussed
in section IV (The Government's Program) and Annex 6.

106. Logistics costs are an important determinant of trade and competitiveness. Logistics costs
include transportation infrastructure and services (roads, ports, railways, and trucks), warehousing and
inventory financial costs, and the associated firm administrative costs resulting from supply chain
organization. At the micro level, the reduction of logistics costs enables firms to improve their financial
performance, resulting in positive economic outcomes for the country. With better logistics performance,
a firm will be able to reduce its inventory level and costs as well as the total costs of goods delivered,
allowing it to remain responsive to the client and consumers' preferences and ultimately increase its
market share. In the context of global sourcing of goods, this results in increased trade and higher-value
employment, leading to economic growth. Moreover, studies have shown that if a reduction in logistics
costs takes place, products that were unviable to produce for export could become viable after this cost
reduction. This is likely to be the case for primary products and commodities, such as fruits and
vegetables, which are likely to be produced inpoorer areas.

 107. Recent trends in international trade are increasing the importance of logistics, yet costs in
Mexico remain high. After the decrease in tariffs following bilateral, regional and multilateral trade
agreements, countries are relying less on preferential tariffs to promote exports in the long run.
Therefore, the improvement of logistic performance (cost and quality) i s progressively becoming a key
factor in countries' competitiveness. Despite this trend, logistics costs inMexico are particularly high (an
estimated 20 percent), while the OECD average is 9 percent.38A pilot survey of a new Logistics
Perception Index (LPI), aimed at measuring operators' perceptions of countries' logistics environments,




 36 Gordon H. Hanson and Raymond Robertson, "Booms and Busts in Mexican Exports: A Gravity Framework for
 Evaluating Supply and Demand Factors" paper presented at the World Bank Seminar, October 2005.
 37This strategy also will make more productive chains viable by allowing the importation of inputs and raw materials
 under competitive conditions.
 38Guasch(2004).


                                                         33

gives Mexico a score of 3.57 (7 being the best and 1 the worst), below other Latin American countries
like Argentina (4.46) and Brazil (3.83).39

108. One important logistics challenge is the need to improve some key internal corridors, as
manufacturers encounter high costs in delivering their products frodto the border or ports/airports.
Elevated transport costs and times have also ledto high inventory levels for businesses:duringthe 1990s,
Mexico had 90 percent higher raw materials inventory levels on average than the U.S.40 The lack of
efficient multi-modal transportation platforms, such as logistics centers, increases the time to deliver or
receive a product, as well as the associatedcost.

109. Customs is another key component of logistics costs. Average customs transactions involve
many parties, documents and data. Administrative costs relating to customs procedures were estimated at
7-10 percent of global trade41and Mexico is not an exception. The lack of flexibility in working hours
and location for product clearance adds to delays and costs. The customs administration would also
benefit from a modernization of its infrastructure, including better use of information technology.

110. Manufacturing companies, especially small and medium size enterprises (SMEs) often incur
higher costs due to a lack of skills in modern supply-chain management techniques. This involves
rethinking the complete supply chain from material and services sourcing to the use of specialized
distribution companies to deliver the end product to the client. Another factor slowing the adoption of
modem supply-chain management techniques i s the limited availability of efficient operators able to
deliver integrated logistics services.

Measures to be Undertakenas Part of the Proposed Operation
 111. These actions to strengthen trade facilitation and reduce logistics costs are achievable in the short
to medium term. They also reinforce policy reform measures already in place that will contribute in a
concrete and sustainable way to longer-term competitiveness, job creation, and poverty alleviation plans.
The benefits of continued reform in trade facilitation and logistics costs reduction promise significant
expansion of competitiveness for Mexican exporters. The matrix in Annex 2 summarizes the Bank-
supported actions and indicators, Annex 3 contains the full government program matrix, and Annex 6
contains more details about the supported programs.

     (i)     Prior Actions
             a. Streamline trade procedures and lower trade costs by: reducingtariffs between 3 and 10
                  points on more than 9,000 tariff lines in the Mexican tariff schedule (this reduction
                  affects 76% of all tariffs inthe schedule); simplifying requirements for export enterprises
                  to import inputs (6,500 PITEX and Maquila enterprises benefited, 90 percent of
                  manufacturing exports); implementing "Operation Paperless" in SICEX for operation
                  reports, import license applications, etc. includingpilots in SICEX of new SE paperless

POLICYAREA4: ENERGY
Description and Challenges
112. Energy is a major driver of economic growth, productivity and competitiveness. The energy
sector i s of high strategic importance to the economy. Even slight changes in the availability and price of
hydrocarbon fuels or electricity can have serious effects on economic growth. An increase in the relative
price of energy inputs increases the cost of production of goods and services, thereby putting pressure on
profit margins. A modeling exercise by the IMF (2000) shows that a sustained US$5 per barrel increase
in the price of oil results in a 0.1 percent decrease in GDP-even in oil exporting countries such as
Mexico. Since January 2002, oil prices have increased from about US$20 to US$55 per                Another
study showed that a ten percent increase in electricity infrastructure stock (generation capacity) could
lead to a general increase of 2 percent in manufacturinggrowth with a higher impact on 31 percent of all
manufacturing

 113. Price volatility and uncertainty associated with fuel availability and the quality of service
significantly affects consumers and producers in the economy. Both energy and non-energy
businesses' investment decisions are based on expectations about energy prices and service quality
levels. The countries that have achieved a secure supply of fuels and electricity at competitive prices




~




42West Texas IntermediateCrude Oil Price.
43CastaAeda Sabido, Alejandro (2003). Infrastructureand ManufacturingGrowth, ElColegio de Mexico.


                                                      35

have exhibited high levels of economic development and consequently an increase in their level of
competitiveness.44

114. The Mexican hydrocarbons industry faces important challenges. About 60 percent of oil
revenues are diverted to finance at least 35 percent of national budgetary needs. This has a significant
impact on the capacity of the Mexican oil company (PEMEX) to finance new infrastructure for
sustainable and competitive growth. Furthermore, meeting the growing demand for energy will require a
significant increase in investment which the government will not be able to finance unless it severely
curtails spending on other important social goals (such as health, education and rural infrastructure).
Breaking out of this vicious circle is one of the most important challenges facing Mexico today. To a
large extent, the technical and operational efficiency of state-owned enterprises depend on the levels of
spending approved by Treasury and the Congress every year. But beyond the spending constraints,
various national and international experts agree that PEMEX needs to make additional efforts to increase
operational efficiency, lower costs and improve the quality of service. A more detailed analysis of the
sector is found in Annex 7.

115. Despite the fact that Mexico has the sixth-largest gas reserves inthe Western Hemisphere, the
country imports 15-20 percent of its domestic demand.45These imports are mainly supplied by the U S
natural gas market, one of the most volatile of the world. Natural gas prices inMexico have increased by
236 percent over the last six years and are among the highest prices in the world. The lack of economic
and technical efficiency in the hydrocarbons sector has also translated into high fuel oil prices, which
have increasedby 183percent since 1999.

116. Electricity prices are among the highest in the world. Yet, average tariffs still fall short of
covering costs despite steady nominal increases over the past 15 years. In 2003, electricity subsidies
financed by the Federal Electricity Commission (CFE) absorbeda significant amount of public resources
(72.9 MxP Billion or 1.08 percent of GDP). Despite the subsidy, electricity tariffs in Mexico have
increased 77 percent since 1998, compared with a rise of only 14 percent in the US. In particular, the
commercial tariff inMexico is 80 percent higher than the US average. Transmission tariffs inMexico are
also among the highest inthe world, below only Japan and Italy.

117. The service quality of electric utilities on the other hand still lags behind international
standards and client expectations. For instance, when annual interruptions and distribution losses are
compared with Latin American private distribution companies, CFE performance i s characteristically
poor. Labor efficiency indicators also remain below international benchmarks (PER, 2005). Despite the
fact that industries are exposed to high peak electricity tariffs and low levels of service quality, there are
a number of barriers preventingthe installation of self-generation and co-generation facilities. Examples
include complex grid interconnection protocols, high fees, inflexible conditionality in interconnection
contracts, and difficulty in obtaining the necessarypermits.

 118. In the manufacturing sector, the cost of energy as a percentage of total production costs
increased by 2.25 percentage points to an average of 10.5 percent between 1995 and 2002. This is
comparable to labor costs, which are in the range of 4-20 percent. The energy-intensive industrial
sub-sectors are particularly vulnerable to volatility in prices and availability uncertainty. The cost of
energy as a percentage of the total production costs for these types of industries i s particularly high (see
Table 7 below). The quality and price of the products from energy-intensive industries are of

Largo Plazos, in Situacih de la Competitividad en Mexico, Instituto Mexican0 para la Competitividad, Mexico.
45SENERdata. 2004-2005.


                                                      36

fundamental importance, as they are used as raw materials in the manufacturing of other diverse
products. For instance, the price o f cement in Mexico i s about twice the price in the US, the European
Union and other countries around the world (115 USD/ton, CEMEX Data 2004). Steady increases in
energy prices have had serious effects on the costs o f production across different industries.

119. Over the last five years, there has been a steady fall in the number of energy-intensive
processing facilities in Mexico. This has been largely attributed to the increasing costs of energy and to
uncertainty regarding the availability o f natural gas and other fuels (Arizmendi, 2005). The studies and
surveys conducted by the Center for Studies on Competitiveness (CEC-ITAM) to analyze the competitive
status and prospects o f different industries - including mining, electronics and vehicle parts - have
concluded that high energy costs and the low quality o f infrastructure services are factors contributing to
lower economic efficiency and prospects for growth and competitiveness (CEC, 2004).

 120. Reforms to the energy sector maybe extremely difficult to achieve. Over the last decade,
different groups have called for the implementation o f far reaching reforms in sector policies as a
necessary condition to attract sufficient private financing, improve efficiency, lower costs, and expand
the sector's contribution to the federal budget and the economy. While a national consensus has not been
reached with regard to the nature and extent of energy sector reforms, the World Bank considers that the
G O M has gradually taken conservative steps to allow limited private sector participation and improve
transparency and regulatory accounting.

Measures to be Undertakenas Part of the Proposed Operation
 121. To fully address the aforementioned issues regarding price, subsidies, service quality, and
 efficiency, far-reaching reforms will be required. Since a consensus has not been reached on these issues,
 this DPL is supporting measures to promote transparency and regulatory improvements which will
 enhance competitiveness. They can be seen as a first step toward energy sector reformthat will shed light
 on how the sector currently performs. The matrix in Annex 2 summarizes the Bank-supported actions and
 indicators, Annex 3 contains the full government program matrix, and Annex 7 contains more details
 about the sector.

     (i)Prior Actions: Implement transparency measures and regulatory instruments for the energy
         sector through the integration o f an inter-institutional regulatory accounting working group
         (comprised o f SENER, CFE, Luz y Fuerza, CRE) to implement the measures established in the



                                                      37

        "Plan de Acciones Concretas en Materia de Mejora Regulatoria que FormanParte de la Agenda
        Competitividad Inmediata en Materia de Energia" (publishedin the DOF on August 11, 2005;
        issuance of a status report on quality and performance indicators for the electricity sector; and
        programfor the evaluation of performance indicators for PEMEX.
    (ii)Triggers for Phase 2: Increased transparency on electricity pricing and quality through
        publishing an agreement by SENER with the regulatory accounting elements; issuing a
        COFEMER agreement to increase transparency regarding costs; publication of cost,
        management, quality of service indicators for the electricity sector; official publication of
        performance indicators for PEMEX; and official publication of the regulations for the operation
        of hydrocarbon sector. These regulations include: LP Gas regulation; agreement that establishes
        the regulation of first-hand sales of petroleumproducts; agreement that establishesthe terms and
        contractual conditions of the delivery-receipt of petroleum products or basic petrochemicals
        obtained as sub-products from the production of non-basic petrochemicals.


                             VI.    OPERATION IMPLEMENTATION

POVERTY AND SOCIAL IMPACT ANALYSIS
122. Overall, the policies supported by this loan are expected to have significant positive poverty
and social effects, as described below. Many of these effects will be indirect and will emerge over the
medium to long-term. Through periodic household surveys and other activities to track progress on
poverty reduction, the Government will have the capacity to monitor and managethese effects.
123. The proposed program seeks to enhance competitiveness, which is an important driver of
growth and therefore of poverty reduction. Nearly all the countries that have had long term success in
reducing poverty have done so with higher rates of economic growth (left panel of Figure 11).& This
association i s also true for Mexico, where poverty reduction has tracked growth in GDP per capita over
the last decade (right panel of Figure 11). However, after experiencing robust GDP per capita growth
following the 1994 Tequila Crisis, income levels have been more or less stagnant since 2000. The recent
growth slowdown has meant significant lost poverty reduction opportunities, although programs such as
Oportunidades have helped offset this trend.47For Mexico to break out of its low-growth trajectory and
cut poverty, it will have to boost growth and improve its productivity and competitiveness. The measures
supported by this proposed operation can reasonably be expected to contribute to this, although it would
be impossible to isolate and quantify the precise impact, given that many other factors cause growth and
poverty reduction.
