124. In addition to driving growth, the actions supported by this operation would have significant
positive social benefits at the microeconomic level. These include impacts on small businesses, the
creation of job and training opportunities for educated youth and high-skill labor, and the capacity of
COFEMER to improve the cost-benefit ratio of regulations for the overall society. The sections below
describe the potential impacts. A larger-scale poverty and social impact evaluation4* will be carried out
for the second phase. This evaluation will try to assess the size and depth of the impacts in more detail,
considering that most of the strategies proposed by the GOM originated before the contracting of this
loan, which may require the analysis of a more comprehensive time period.49

Impacts on Small Businesses
125. An important set of actions supported by this loan is targeted at facilitatingthe entrance of
new small businesses in the formal market and at improving their profitability.This is extremely
important considering that small and mediumenterprises (SMEs) represent as much as three quarters of
total employment in Mexico. The specific actions having a positive impact on the entrance of new small
businessesin the formal market are: (1) Actions aiming at reducingcosts and time of business entry; and
costs of regulatory compliance are expected to have two positive impacts. First they will reduce fixed
costs and the burden of bribery. Small businesses that have greater difficulty bearing these costs will
benefit from these measures. According to the Center of Economic Studies of the Private Sector, on
average, small and mediumbusinessesinMexico spend 6 percent of their revenues on informal payments
to public officials. This cost is significantly higher than the cost of similar payments for big businesses,
which on average is 4 percent of their revenue.50Second, they will create incentives for theformalization
of businesses currently operating in the informal sector. As 40 percent of the active population in
Mexico works in the informal sector, formalization will have an important social impact by increasing
employees' access to the services provided by the Znstituto Mexican0 del Seguro Social, training


48 The GOM and the World Bank will create an interdisciplinaryanalysisgroup with the participationof academia
and poverty experts, in order to define the proper methodologyto carry out this evaluation, accordingto the
availabilityof sources of information.
49 For instance,the trade liberalizationbeganin 1986 and deepened in 1994 with the NAFTA. Therefore, the impact
won't necessarilybe the result of the import duties reductionconsideredunder this loan.
50 Centro de EstudiosEcon6micosdel Sector Privado-CEESP (2005), Encuestasobre gobernabilidad y desarrollo
empresarial2005, available at www.cce.orp.mx/ceesp.


                                                                  39

opportunities, and pensions. (2) The enhancement of the anti-trust law and its enforcement at a
decentralized level will also contribute to improve the business environment for the entrance in the
market of new small businesses and will reduce the prizes for the consumers. (3) Actions enhancing the
efficiency of customs and ports will reduce corruption and benefit businesses interested in improving
their delivery time. This i s relevant, since services at customs are perceived by Mexicans to be the 3rd
major cause of ~orruption.~'Additionally, higher performance of ports resulting from the actions
supported by the loan, will scale-up port activity, and thus create opportunities for port-related business
in the areas and cities surrounding the port. This will have a positive impact on employment and the
development of small business.

126. The specific actions having a positive impact on the profitability of small businesses are: (1) the
implementation of the Logistics Practices Innovation Program, which will considerably reduce the
operation costs of small businesses that participate in the program; and (2) the programs promoting
technological innovation, which will increase the access to and the effective use of technology by small
businesses.

127. Nevertheless, an important percentage of small businesses in Mexico are experiencing large
difficulties in recovering operational and investment costs. A study conducted by the Znstituto
Mexicano para la Competitividad (IMCO) shows that small businesses operating in all economic sectors
have rates of returnbelow the cost of capital.52This situation, which translates into a high mortality rate
for these types of businesses, contributes to undermining the cost-effectiveness of those actions aiming at
improvingthe opportunities for the creation of small business.

Impacts of Trade Liberalization
128. Though further trade liberalization and trade facilitation are expected to bring long-term
benefits in terms of competitiveness, economic growth, and employment generation, some potential
effects require further analysis and monitoring. In the long run, the maquila sector, which accounts
for approximately 46 percent of Mexican exports, will benefit from the tariff reductions, presentedby the
GOM as an undertaken action, which translates into an increase in secure employment in the sector.53
Additionally, a World Bank study estimates that this tariff reform will result in an increase of 0.1 percent
in the level of employment of unskilled labor. However, since the beginning of the trade liberalization
processin Mexico, benefits have not beendistributed equally among all the sectors of the population and
all the regions of the country. Unfortunately, there i s no official mechanismto systematically monitor the
social impacts of trade liberalization. That makes a detailed poverty and social impact evaluation
advisable in the preparation of the second phase of the operation, which would assess the size and depth
of the impacts of the trade liberalization actions, including the actions supported by the loan on different
stakeholders, and suggest specific measures to monitor and mitigate potential negative impacts on the
poor.

Impacts of Innovation, Technology Development, and Training
129. The actions supported by the loan, especially with respect to the development of innovation
technology research and businesses, have a direct and positive impact on the levels and the quality
of employment of high-skilled labor force and educated youth. Overall, these actions also positively
impact consumers in so far as new technologies will improve the quality and reduce the price of products
manufactured by industries that have adopted those innovations. Additionally, these measures also


"CEESP(2005).
j2Efecto de algunos escenariosde Reforma Tributaria en la rentabilidad de las empresas.IMCO, 2002.
j3Economist Intelligence Unit (2005). Country Profile Mexico.


                                                       40

contribute to reducing the brain drain from Mexico to other countries. Universities and educational
institutions will also benefit from a positive indirect impact of these actions, since more pressure will be
placed on their capacity to meet the demands of the productive sector, which may lead to constructive
changes in their cumcula and researchagendas.

130. Actions to ensure that low-skilled labor takes advantage of these opportunities, through an
improvement in the quality of data on training and the issuance of secondary school certificates for
youth having dropped out of school, will have a positive impact. The dimension of this impact will
rely on the adequacy of the education received by graduates to respond to the needs of the labor market.
According to the XI1Census of Population and Housing, there are around 15 millionpeople that have not
finished secondary education in Mexico. The potential increase in the revenues of poor families resulting
from this measure i s important. Head of households having completed secondary education have the
potential of obtaining an income that is 43 percent higher than those without education.54However,
according to the World Bank poverty assessment in Mexico, in the past ten years an increase in the
educational level has failed to pay off in higher incomes because the lack of expansion in earning
opportunities. The gains resulting from the completion of one additional year of secondary education
(yearly rate of return of secondary education) have decreased considerably since 1993. In 2002, when
estimated at the median of the conditional earnings distribution, upper complete secondary education was
associatedwith only, on average, 13 percent more income compared with a person with lower secondary
complete education.jj These results contrast sharply with the high rate of return of migration for
individuals having completed upper secondary education. Under these conditions, the impact of this
measure on the poor i s highly dependent on: (1) the ability of the GOM to increase earning opportunities
for low-skilled labor; and (2) the capacity of educational institutions to adapt their curriculums in order
to match the demands of the labor market. The efforts of the GOM, with Bank support, to improve the
quality of secondary education programs and the implementation of 22 Labor Information Centers, as
part of the GOM Program on Competitiveness, will help to more readily connect firms seeking workers
with job seekers and their skills.

Impacts of theActions to Enhance the Transparency of the Regulatory Process
 131. According to the OECD56,excessivediscretionary power during the making of new regulation
has decreased in Mexico as a result of the regulatory reform, and public officials are more
accountable for their decisions and actions. COFEMER has been created in order to legitimate
government action on regulatory policy by increasing the quality and transparency of the regulatory
process, and by promoting systematic public consultation with the groups targeted by regulations.
Overall, COFEMER actions will positively impact poor and vulnerable groups, since with the new
regulatory process, regulations affecting them can be modified or safeguards can be included in order to
prevent or compensate for negative impacts. Legally, COFEMER i s a "deconcentrated administrative
body" of the Ministry of Economy. As such, COFEMER has important functions, and has considerable
technical, operational, financial independence, although it maintains organic links with the Ministry.j7
The accountability framework of COFlEMER includes the creation of a political body, the Federal
Regulatory Improvement Council, which includes representatives of major ministries as well as of labor
and business representatives. This council has the mandate of playing a role in the oversight of
COFEMER's actions, along with the Congress.5*A poverty and social impact evaluation for the second

phase of the operation will help to evaluate the implementation of the accountability framework of this
quasi autonomous agency, as well as its capacity to involve different stakeholders in its governance
structure and to operate in a more transparent way.

Impacts of theperformance indicatorsfor the Electricity Sector

132. The improvement of the performance indicators for the electricity sector in the medium term will
have a positive impact on the quality of the provision of power. Poor users will benefit from this effect
since a poor quality of power provision has primarily affected low income households and micro-
businesses. Additionally, the use of new performance indicators opens the opportunity to involve an
ample range of users in the evaluation of the quality of the services based on their own experience. On
the other hand, the experience of other countries shows that the use of performance indicators leads to
tighter controls over power connections. Poor households in urban areas that are currently makinguse of
non-billing power connections will suffer from these controls. A poverty and social impact evaluation
will assess the feasibility of using participatory performance evaluation mechanisms such as citizens'
report cards, and will informthe preparation of the second phase of the operation. It will also estimate the
dimension of the economic impact of this measure on poor households and will suggest a menu of
mitigating actions.


PUBLICPARTICIPATION
133. The preparation of this programmatic loan has contributed to the dialogue among various
partsof the government as well as with business sector stakeholders. Duringthe initialpreparation of
the DPL, the Bank with the support of the GOM organized 10 focus groups with approximately 90
businesspeoplefrom various sectors of the economic activity; directors of associations and chambers of
commerce; and more than 30 officials from the federal, state and municipal governments. The Consejo
Coordinador Empresarial, the major business umbrella organization in Mexico, was instrumental in
coordinating the implementation of these focus groups. International and domestic firms participating
had different sizes and represented the following sub-sectors: (i)textile and garments; (ii)                 metallic
products, machinery and equipment; (iii)         agro-industry and food; and (iv) chemical and rubber and
plastic products. Together, these four sub-sectors represent 80 percent of the manufacturing GDP and
more than 80 percent of employment in the manufacturing sector, and about 92 percent of exports. The
inputs of the focus groups were valuable in helping to understand some of the most important obstacles
to improving competitiveness inMexico.

134. Several clear messages from the entrepreneurs and public officials emerged from this
dialogue. The following major obstacles to Mexico's competitiveness were identified: lack of
contractual legal security; lack of capacity and efficiency of road, air and maritime transportation; poor
coordination among Federal, State and Municipal levels and lack of regulatory stability; limited focus of
the government on promotinginnovation, and by firms on investing in R&D; and the high price of public
utilities services. Additionally, obstacles were mentioned that had a markedly regional, size or sectoral
incidence such as the impact of crime and insecurity on the textile, garment, electronics and computer
sectors, and the lack of policies to support exports as a major constraint to the textile and the agro-



General Director of COFEMER, the Governor of the Bank of Mexico, five business sector representatives, at least
one representative from the labor, agricultural and academic sector, the President's Legal Counsel, the President of
the Federal Competition Commission (Cornisidn Federal de Cornpetencia - CFC) and the President of the Federal
Consumer Protection Agency (Procuraduri'a Federal del Consurnidor - PROFECO).




                                                         4L

industrial sectors and to the small and medium enterprises. Several sectors complained about the lack of
domestic suppliers of their inputs. Overall the perceptions of public officials and entrepreneurs were
similar for most of the issues. However, public officials considered lack of infrastructure, and
distribution and transportation costs to be much more important obstacles to competitiveness than
entrepreneurs did. On the other side, entrepreneurs assigned greater importance to the lack of export
promotion mechanisms as a major constraint for competitiveness than public officials did. The results of
the focus groups were presentedto several institutions including the Consejo Coordinador Empresarial,
universities and other public and private institutions. This process helped to bringtogether the priorities
as perceived by the private and public sectors for the competitiveness agenda. This processhas continued
through the implementation of Phase Iof the Trade and Competitiveness AAA, through which the Bank
has reachedout to other stakeholders such as Congress and the states.

135. Aside from the focus groups process, the government has engaged in dialogue with private
sector leadership, such as the Consejo Coordinador Empresarial and other private chambers and
associations. This helped broaden the GOM's Competitiveness Agenda. Also, complementary to the
government efforts, the World Bank held discussions with the private sector during the DPL preparation
mission in September. Lastly, the World Bank team has reachedout to the Academia and think-tanks and
developed a partnership with the Znstituto Mexicano para la Competitividad (IMCO), a leading think-
tank on competitiveness issues in Mexico. The IMCO carried out a comprehensive analysis of
competitiveness issues (partially supported by the Bank) that has been a major analytical piece on
competitiveness and has helped bringcompetitiveness matters to the forefront.

136. The impact of IMCO's report has been huge and continues to be felt. The report was presented
to President Fox in February 2005, at which time the President made a speech highlighting the
importance of competitiveness. A working group has also been formed inthe Senate to address the issues
raised in the report. In September 2005, the first part of a "Competitiveness Pact" was convened by
Carlos Slim, with 800 of the most important business and civil society representatives in the country in
attendance. The participants signed an accord-based      on IMCO's report-that     presents a vision of the
country and a series of petitions to future presidential candidates from all political parties. Also in
September 2005, IMCO releasedits secondyear of competitiveness monitoring indicators.

 137. Dialogue will continue with relevant stakeholders. The dissemination of the SecondPhase of the
Trade and Competitiveness AAA and the Policy Notes being prepared for the next administration would
allow dialogue to continue during 2006. As part of the proposed poverty and social impact analysis that
could be carried out duringthe preparation of the second stage of this operation, extensive consultations
with different stakeholderswould take place.


IMPLEMENTATION, MONITORING AND EVALUATION
 138. Each one of the sectoral ministries and agencies involved in this operation will be responsible for
implementation of the proposed components of the operation and for reporting progress. The Secretariat
of the Economy (SE) will be responsible for coordinating actions among the sectoral agencies. The
individual sectoral ministries and agencies will be responsible for collecting the data on the selected
indicators, and as the executing agency, the SE would also coordinate the information to be provided by
the GOM for the elaboration of any Report related with this loan. The administration of this loan will be
the responsibility of Nacional FinancieraS.N.C. (NAFIN),as financial agent of the Federal Government.
NAFINwill be in charge of the coordination and fulfillment of the terms and conditions set forth in the
Loan Agreement.




                                                     43

FIDUCIARY ASPECTS,DISBURSEMENT,AND AUDITING
139. The Mexico Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CFAA) was completed in October
2003. The assessment focused on the federal public sector, which was considered to have generally
sound financial management systems and institutions. Country financial management(FM)riskwas rated
moderate, and all individual risk factors were rated low or moderate. The results of the CFAA have a
direct impact on this project, as funds will flow into Mexico's public FM systems. The Bank has recently
supported government efforts to strengthen some of the areas considered by the CFAA to be
opportunities for improvement, such as the accounting processes and information systems.

140. The proposed Competitiveness DPL will follow the Bank's disbursement procedures for
development policy lending. The loan proceeds will be disbursed against satisfactory implementation of
the development policy lending program and not tied to any specific purchases. The US. dollar loan
would be made to the United Mexican States. The loan would cover the first of the three operations
under the program.

141. Flow of funds (including foreign exchange) is subject to normal FM processes. It i s not
possible to track the ultimate use of the foreign exchange provided by the Competitiveness DPL
proceeds, but loan proceeds flow into a dedicated account59and from it to Mexico's budget, and are thus
subject to normal public FM processes. By way of a letter, the Government will provide confirmation to
the Bank when the loan amount has been credited to an account used to finance budgeted expenditures
(as soon as step 3 in the following flowchart i s concluded). The Bank camed out activities to provide
reasonable assurancethat loan proceeds flow into Mexico's normal public FM systems. Given the ample
experience of Mexico managing Bank's DPLs, the Bank analyzed projects' flow of funds and reviewed
the audit report of Banco de Mhico (the Central Bank) and the Country Financial Accountability
Assessment report (CFAA). The IMF's report on the Safeguards Assessment of the Central Bank has not

ENVIRONMENTALASPECTS
144. The country policies supported by the proposed loan do not entail significant effects on
Mexico's environment, forests, or other natural resources. The policies will not result in any
significant commitment of such resources. Although some minor effects are possible, any effects are
likely on balance to be either positive or benign. For example:

0   Increased trade through trade facilitation can impact the environment through three channels: scale
    effects, composition effects, and changes in techniques / technology. Scale effects, meaning overall
    increases or decreases in trade, from policies supported by this loan are quite small. According to a
    World Bank study, the Bank-supported tariff reduction program was estimated to increaseexports by
    0.7 percent and GDP by 0.1 percent. Composition effects refer to the growth or decline of more-
    versus less-polluting industries. The same Bank analysis found that relatively non-polluting
    industries would experience the largest gains in output. These include electronic equipment,
    construction, machinery and equipment, and motor vehicles and parts (mainly assembly). More
    heavily polluting industries were expected to experience drops in output.60Changes in techniques
    refer to more efficient (less polluting) technologies or processes that are introduced through
    increasedtrade. While impossible to measure, this effect is expected to have a positive environmental
    impact.

0   The innovation support programs supported by this operation could lead to some small effects by
    encouraging the adoption of new technologies that use resources, like energy or water, more
    efficiently. Energy regulatory measures that promote improvements in technical performance could
    also have positive impacts on the environment. Other potential small positive effects are illustrated
    by recent experiences inMexico showingthat increasedcompetitiveness leading to economic growth
    has taken pressure off the environment by making more resources available for environmental
    protection. Currently the GOM devotes 0.60 percent of GDP to public environmental expenditures.

 145. Although the Bank-supportedpolicies are expected to have only minor environmental effects,
Mexico recognizes the importance of proper environmental policies and proactive government
monitoring within an adequate environmental regulatory infrastructure. To address such concerns,
Mexico has established a state-of-the art environmental mainstreaming program aimed at promoting
clean production mechanism and sustainable development. The GOM's environmental mainstreaming
program (Programa de Transversalidad Ambiental) includes institutional strengthening actions in the
implementingagencies of this operation (Secretary of Finance and Public Credit, Secretary of Economy,
Secretary of Energy, and Secretary of Communications and Transport). The Bank, through the
Programmatic Environment DPL (ENVDPL), i s                 supporting the GOM in: (i)mainstreaming
environmental concerns in the development agendas of key sectors-tourism,        energy, forestry and water;
and (ii)  improving the effectiveness and efficiency of local environmental management processes in
Mexico. Most relevant to the proposed Competitiveness DPL i s the energy area, which supports the
development of a package of legal reforms and regulations needed for promotion of renewable energy
sources. Since energy i s a critical factor of production, ensuring a secure and sustainable energy supply i s
important to Mexico's long-termability to compete.


   Evidencefor determiningmore-versus less-pollutingindustriescomes fromManiandWheeler (1998).


                                                       45

146. Mexico's Environmental Mainstreaming Program has strengthened the GOM's country
systems particularly at identifying and addressing environmental problems and priorities in an effective
manner and taking into account concerns of stakeholders (including the most vulnerable groups).
Mexico's Environmental Mainstreaming Program also embodies processes to adequately monitor and
evaluate progress to overcome these environmental priority problems. Simultaneously, Mexico has in
place environmental safeguards aimed at offsetting and minimizing potential environmental risks
associated with economic growth. The Legal framework LEGPA, and NAFTA and other trade
agreements contain provisions to strengthen enforcement of environmental regulations. Reduction of
export and import tariffs has been accompanied with strong environmental policies and higher
environmental standards that have resulted in shifts in the composition of production, exports, and FDIto
energy and resource efficient sectors.



RISKS AND RISKMITIGATION
147. The proposed program faces two main risks: one political and one institutional. The political
risk is associated with the presidential elections planned for July 2006. While the programhas developed
a significant sense of ownership and commitment among the different stakeholders, a new administration
may choose to shift priorities within or away from the program. As a mitigation strategy, the second loan
i s planned for mid 2007 in order to provide enough opportunity for the Bank to establish a dialogue with
the new administration on the competitiveness program. The choice of instrument also reduces this risk,
as a single-tranche DPL in a medium-term framework is adaptable to changes on the ground. Another
mitigating factor i s that the second phase of the Mexico Competitiveness AAA will be completed by
early 2006 and will provide an opportunity and basis for engagement with the various stakeholders and
the new administration on the proposed competitiveness-enhancing reforms.

148. Another political risk mitigation strategy has been to include the private sector in the
formulation of the country's competitiveness priorities. As outlined in the Public Participation section
above, engagement with the private sector has meant that the GOM's Competitiveness Agenda and the
areas that the DPL addresses are in line with the constraints perceived by the private sector. This broad
consensus around the bases of the program decreases the risk that the next administration will shift
priorities. Lastly, political risk is limited by not including major labor and energy structural reforms that
are politically sensitive and could engender opposition from one or more of the major political parties. In
this sense, the Bank-supported actions are non-controversial. An alternative, to avoid the political risk of
the transition, is to only continue engagement on competitiveness through the AAA. However, this
alternative has been ruled out since it does not provide continuity to existing programs or promote a
mediumterm holistic, cross-governmental program for improvingproductivity.

 149. Institutional coordination issuesalso present a risk. A competitiveness program is necessarily
a broad operation that involves many secretariats and agencies and there i s a risk that all of these
agencies could have difficulties coordinating among themselves. This risk, again, has been mitigated by
the choice as lead counterpart in the GOM of the Secretariat of Economy, which has the technical
credibility and has proved to be a capable coordinator during preparation. Past experience from
elsewhere also suggests that this design can be workable.

 150. The program also faces risks associated with potential impacts of the Bank-supported policy
areas. Improvements to the investment climate could place additional pressure on small businesses
already in the market, to improve their efficiency and some of them might be forced to exit the market.
Though further trade liberalization and trade facilitation are expected to bring long-term growth and
employment benefits, there might be risks for import-competing sectors in the short term. It i s also


                                                        46

important to highlight that an improvement in competitiveness in the private sector will have uneven
results giving existing regional disparities in Mexico.

151. Mexico faces macroeconomic risks related to a possible deterioration of the currently
favorable external environment. Debt Sustainability Analysis shows Mexico's overall resilience to
plausible shocks. According to the analysis undertaken by the IMF as part of its 2005 Article IV
Consultation, standardized stress tests, based on the historical volatility of key macroeconomic variables
for the past 10 years, indicate that the level of public debt would not increase substantially over the
medium-term if macro conditions turned out to be significantly worse than projected. However, the
analysis also notes that more extreme shocks or a combination of shocks -such as a sudden permanent
decline in oil prices, a "hard landing" in the United States, or an arbitrary shock to market confidence in
Mexican assets- would result in a rise in the public sector financing requirement, reflecting the still
relatively short average maturity of domestic debt. Aware of the potential threats to financial stability
stemming from such shocks, the GOM has dedicated significant effort over the past few years and made
substantial progress in developing the domestic government bond market, diversifying the investor base
and extending the yield curve. Additional measures to mitigate the public debt refinancing risk have been
taken over the past few months including the complete prefinancing of external debt repayment
obligations with private creditors due in 2006 and 2007, the issuing of warrants to swap dollar
denominated bonds for peso debt as well as the introduction of a programof exchange of domestic bonds
due in 2006 and 2007 for longer term domestic securities. Strengthening the pipeline of loans with
multilateral banks, including the present fast-disbursing DPL, also fits into this refinancing risk
mitigating strategy.

                                    ANEXO I

     ESTRATEGIASPARA IMPULSAR LA COMPETITIVIDAD EN MEXICO

Uno de 10s retos que Mexico enfrenta actualmente en materia econ6mica es el de
elevar su competitividad. Acelerar el crecimiento, promover el bienestar, ofrecer

crkicamente del &xito que tengamos en el fortalecimiento de nuestra capacidad

La competitividad se sustenta en un ambiente propicio para la inversi6n y para la
generaci6n de empleo; se nutre de un esfuerzo continuo de innovacion y de
capacitacibn; aflora en un marco de certidumbre normativa y legal, en donde la
respuesta de las autoridades, a todos 10s niveles, sea predecible y transparente,
oportuna y apegada al derecho.

La competitividad requiere de mercados intensamente competidos que conduzcan
a una mayor inversi6n en investigacibn y desarrollo, al desarrollo de habilidades y
a la reducci6n continua de costos, ademas de una mejora continua en nuestra
logistica a fin de satisfacer nuestros mercados, internos y externos, con mayor
certidumbre y menores costos.

Elevar el bienestar requiere de la instrumentaci6n de acciones en distintos

mejora regulatoriay cornpetencia, y mejora institucional,

Es por ello que, el Gobierno Mexicano, con el apoyo y participaci6n de las distintas
entidades y dependencias de la Administraci6n Pdblica Federal ha desarrollado una

    competencia, apoyando a empresas con alto componente tecnologico.

    Mejorar el clima de negocios, reduciendo el costo que impone la regulaci6n
    sobre quien invierte y crea empleo, sobre quien innova y asume riesgos,

    mercados;

                                             Mexico City, January 27th,2006

Mr.PaulWolfowitz
President
World Bank
Washington, D.C. 20433
UnitedStates

Dear Mr.Wolfowitz:

During the last years, the issue of Competitiveness has represented one of the main guidelines to
foster growth with quality and economic development in our country, in order to successfully
enter the new world economy. This recognition has been reflected in the National Development
Plan (PND) 2001-2006, which incorporates the vision of strengthening Mexico towards the year
2005 as a country of high world competitiveness, with equitable, inclusive, and sustained
economic growth closely linked to globalization.

Inthis new environment, Mexico is not only calling for the better use of the natural competitive
advantages it has, like geographical location, but also building new sustained advantages in
training, investment and organizational innovation.

The Mexican economic conditions are described through this Sector Policy Letter, as well as the
main areas that have been considered as strategic to attain the goal of increasing and
 strengthening the competitiveness of the country in the medium and long term. The working
 areas selected are related to technological innovation, labor training, investment climate
 improvements, logistics, trade facilitation, and energy. To achieve the strengthening of the
proposed subjects and ensure their continuity in the long term, the Mexican government
 considers it fundamental to obtain the financial and technical support of the World Bank through
 the "Programmatic Competitiveness Development Policy Loan."

 MACROECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT

 The recent economic evolution of Mexico has demonstrated the close link that exists between
 internal industrial activity and the international industrial cycle. For that reason, the PND
 considers among its objectives the driving of the reactivation of high and sustained productivity
 growth, in order to make the national economy improve its competitiveness and move toward
 more accelerated growth.




                                                 67

                                               Subsecretariade Industriay Comercio
                                               (Under-Secretariatof Industryand
                                               Commerce)

SECRETARIA DE
 ECONOMIA                                      Official Letter No. 400.2006.003

                                               Mexico City, January 27,2006

For the above, the lines of action of the current administration have focused on promoting the
strengthening of macroeconomic stability, based on responsible economic and financial policy
management, such as developing a business climate that favors the development of productive
projects and strengthens the economic conditions of the country.

As a result of saidpolicies, Mexico has achieved the lowest interest and inflation rates of the last
thirty years, as well as the lowest country risk of its history; similarly, since the end of 2004 there
has been an appreciation, and net international reserves have reached historic highs-reaching
almost US$60 billion. All of these conditions, without doubt, have favored the balanced
development of the components of the aggregatedemand.

Inthis way, private investment spending showed a meaningful recovery in 2004 of 8.5 percent
with respect to the previous year, highlighting large purchases of imported machinery and
equipment, as well as national transportation equipment. Toward the first quarter of 2005, this
variable increased at an annual rate of 4.4 percent, which demonstrates the increasing interest that
national and international investors are showing in our country as headquarters for their
operations.

Related to external demand, the recovery has been notable. In 2004, total exports increased at an
annual rate of 11.5 percent, thanks mainly to growing demand from the United States, which
helped reverse the stagnation seen in previous years. Duringthe first quarter of 2005, exports of
Mexican products grew 4.8 percent with respect to the same period of the previous year, due to
the advance shown by crude oil sales abroad, and to a lesser extent to manufacturingproducts.

The productive activity showed a different panorama during the first semester of the current year.
The tertiary sector evolved favorably, driven by macroeconomic stability and greater
development of the financial system, registering growth of 4.1 percent in the period January -
June 2005 with respect to the same period of the previous year.

 Nevertheless, industrial activity showed less dynamism with respect to the past year: reaching a
 growth rate of only 1.4 percent duringthe first semester of 2005, compared with the same period
 of 2004. Within this sector, the component o f manufacturing activity showed an real annual
 growth rate of 1.2 percent, coming mainly from the increase in the production of automotive
 vehicles; non-electric equipment and machinery; food processing and dairy; and bodyworks,
 engines, partsand automotive vehicle accessories.

ECONOMIC GROWTHTO IMPROVETHE QUALITY OF LIFE

In order to attain sustained and sustainable economic growth, the Federal Government has
intensified actions to promote and encourage economic activities, so that the productive and
service units operate with growing productivity standards and competitiveness, preparing them to
successfully compete ininternational markets.

Inthe current year, this strategy has achieved the incorporation of a bigger number of productive
units into export chains, in addition to fostering the strengtheningof the internal market through
the modernizationof supply and commercialization systems.

To support these efforts, the following have been important: greater exploitation of the
technological supply in the productive processes; permanent support to the education and labor
training; greater macroeconomic flexibility supported by the development of a business
environment with clear rules and norms similar to those in developed countries; and the
strengthening of the physical and energy infrastructure of the country. Each one of these
strategies, as well as the goals to be reached through their strengthening and continuance, are
detailed in Annex Iof this Letter.

REQUESTFOR SUPPORT FROMTHE WORLD BANK

B y obtaining the funds requested through this Loan, the Mexican government i s trying to attain
the following objectives: support the guidelines established in the government program for the
strengthening of the competitiveness of the country; assure continuity in the areas considered to
be triggers for said objective; establish specific and quantifiable goals for each area; get
specialized technical support that accompanies the execution of policies; and coordinate the
efforts carried out by the different ministries and agencies within the theme of Competitiveness.

During recent years, the Mexican government has already achieved important advances in the
issue of competitiveness, through the design and launching of programs regarding technological
innovation, labor training, improvement of the investment climate, logistics, trade facilitation and
energy. Nevertheless, it i s recognized that it i s still necessary to advance and strengthen these
 actions to achieve the objective of making Mexico one of the most competitive nations
worldwide, allowing it to increase its market share internationally with value added products,
 generate more and better employment, intensify the use of new technologies and attract a greater
flow of foreign investment.

                                             ANNEX I

               STRATEGIESTO BOOSTCOMPETITIVENESSINMEXICO

One of the challenges Mexico currently faces in economic issues i s increasing its
competitiveness.    Accelerating    growth,   promoting well-being,      and offering prosperity
opportunities to more regions and sectors depends critically on our success in strengthening our
competitive capacity.

For that reason the National Development Plan 2001-2006 established as one of its objectives to
increase and extend the country's competitiveness as a necessary condition to attain more
dynamic growth and inclusive development.

Competitiveness i s sustained in a propitious environment for investment and employment
generation; it i s nourished by continuous innovation and training efforts; it flourishes in a
framework of regulatory and legal certainty, where the response of authorities at all levels i s
predictable and transparent, timely and governed by the rule of law.

Competitiveness requires intensively competitive markets that lead to greater investment in
research and development, the development of skills and the continuous reduction of costs, in
 addition to continuous improvement in our logistics in order to satisfy our markets, internal and
 external, with greater certainty and lower costs.

 Increasing well-being requires the instrumentation of actions in different fields: investment and
 savings, innovation and training, logistics and infrastructure, better regulation and competition,
 and institutional improvement.

 That i s why the Mexican Government, with the support and participation of different entities and
 dependencies of the Federal Public Administration, has developed a Competitiveness Agenda
 based on the following strategies:


 0   Stimulate more aggressively innovation, training and education, by fostering modern training
     schemes based on competition standards and supporting companies with high technology
     components;


 0   Improve the investment climate, by reducing the cost imposed by regulation on those that
     invest and create employment and on those .that innovate and assume risks, by guaranteeing
     an intense and transparent competitive environment in all markets;


                                                  71

                                              Subsecretaria de Industria y Comercio
                                              (Under-Secretariat     of   Industry    and
                                              Commerce)


SECRETARIA DE                                 Official Letter No. 400.2006.003
  ECONOMM
                                              Mexico City, January 27,2006

    Reduce logistics costs, in order to translate into investment and employment the comparative
    advantages offered by our geographical location and the network of trade agreements that
    open the main global markets to Mexicanbusiness;

    Increase the energy supply, by having transparent access and interconnection rules,
    strengthening the regulatory authority so as to offer transparency and guarantees to all the
    market participants, and developing alternative fuels sources;

    Foster financial deepening, by reducing the financial requirements of the public sector in
    order to open spaces for the private sector in the credit market, fostering the popular savings
    and credit network in an orderly manner, supporting the use of electronic payments,
    promoting more intense competition, and improving the regulationof brokers.

The Federal Administration has centered efforts in each one of these lines, by designing,
implementing, and attaining results with specific actions and defining new guidelines so that the
results achieved are translated into sustained productivity growth, associated with an
improvement inthe life style levels of all social groups.

O f the previous subjects, the progress attained in financial deepening has exceeded the
expectations of the present administration by reaching a financial savings level greater than 50
percent of GDP. Even though we are far from our main partners in this area, the current and
prospective financial environment in the medium term allows us to anticipate that a favorable
trend will be maintained.

In summary, to face the challenges of the current world environment, apart from maintaining
 solid macroeconomic stability, it i s important to build and maintain permanent microeconomic
efficiency that results in fair competition, higher quality of goods and services, development of
productive resources, and lower costs to do business in the country. In fact, the objective i s to
understand broadly that globalization i s a reality, productivity an obligation, and competitiveness
 a challenge.

 It has to be considered that, in the five current strategic lines, the agencies and entities of the
 Mexican Government develop specific actions with particular proposals and meaningful results.
 Nevertheless, for purposes related to the program with the World Bank, we will only focus on
 four of them, as will be described below.

INNOVATION,TRAINING AND EDUCATION

Essential elements to reach an accelerated and sustained increase in productivity are innovation,
the capacity to turn those innovations into productive capital, internal skills formation in the
company, and human capital investment.

The Mexican shortfall in research and development, training systems, and education for work i s
reflected indifferent spheres:

      Insufficient investment levels in research and development, production of science and
      technology, and training of high level scientific and technological humanresources.
  0   Low education levels of workers which hinder the optimal use of training received.
  0   Lack of flexibility and relevance of training programs to the changing needs of the labor
      market.
  0   Little participation of private institutions inthe design and teaching of training.
      Incompatibility between training services and the requirements of the population and
      productive apparatus.

The efforts carried out internally in the government to decrease, dismantle, and reverse the
 shortfall in this area are the result of the development of employment support programs in the
Ministryof Social Welfare andWork; the awarding of scholarships by CONACYT; the awarding
 of stimuli for scientific and technological development (with the participation of Nafin, the
 Secretariat of Economy and CONACYT); the allocation of resources for research and
 development projects through Mixed and Sectorul Funds; support for companies and research
 centers to transform their discoveries into concrete cases with the Program of High Value Added
 inBusinesseswith Knowledge and Businessmen (AVANCE), among other results.

 There has been a dual focus, on one side incorporating companies in the innovation circuit, and
 on the other creating human capital through training.

 At the same time, training includes the association of diverse aspects that include: adults'
 education to complete basic studies (elementary and secondary school); the development of
 capabilities so that unemployed people have the possibility of being inserted into the labor
 market with better work arguments and even the improvement of labor skills intheir work place.




                                                    73

                                              Subsecretaria de Industria y Comercio
                                              (Under-Secretariat     of   Industry    and
                                              Commerce)


SECRETAGIA DE                                 Official Letter No. 400.2006.003
  ECONOMIA
                                              Mexico City, January 27,2006


It is worth mentioning that the integration of education, training and innovation was inlarge part
caused by the new science and technology law of 2002. The great contribution of this decisive
step to stimulate innovation was, on one hand, that it gave the institute in charge of designing the
science and technology policies (CONACYT) its own personality and did not divide it into
sectors, and on the other hand, it coordinated efforts with diverse sectors of the society so as to
involve them inthe technological development process.

The pioneering character of this initiative to drive research and development i s recent and this
makes it not well-established yet. These incipient but firm results demand that we look for ways
to protect the initiatives, allowing themto go beyondthe current governmental administration.

In this sense, we will continue our efforts with the Program supported by the World Bank
 through the following key actions:


   0  To promote technological innovation by driving technological innovation programs to
      promote actions with highvalue added through:

      a) Creation of the National Business Incubation System (SNIE) which will allow: having
           the security of counting on recognized and successful models of the Secretariat of
           Economy; integrating in one system all the traditional incubators, of intermediate
           technology and high technology; and establishing a national communications network
           that will permit knowledge management. The incubators will be able to interact,
           exchange experiences, establish best practices and strengthen the collaboration scheme.

       b) Creation of the Acceleration Program to strengthen successful Mexican companies in
           global markets and, inthis way, position Mexico as a technology-creating country.

       c) Launching of the website of the National Incubators System (SNIE) in Spanish and
           English versions (www.siem.rrob.mx/snie) which will facilitate interactionbetween the
           directors of incubators of different models recognized by the Secretariat of Economy,
           entrepreneurs and/or institutions that want to create a new business incubator.

INVESTMENTCLIMATE

The business environment i s important in shaping the behavior of businesses and propelling them
toward more aggressive investment and innovation strategies.

To achieve this, a transparent business environment i s required, with clear and easy to comply
with regulation, intense competition, with precise rules in order to stimulate investment and
encourage innovation. Improving the government capacity to produce good regulation i s a
necessary condition to improve competitiveness.

During this Administration, actions have been developed to stop the tendency to increase the
number of procedures and regulations (as the Regulatory Moratorium that ends in November
 2006); to improve the procedures that have the greatest impact on the operation of the companies
 and citizens (proposed by the private sector); to facilitate the rapid opening of companies; to
 intensify the competition in the markets (amendments to the Federal Competition Law); to
 reinforce the commercial and sector policy (with sector promotion programs); to modernize the
 property and commerce public registries; and others.

 In spite of the above mentioned, there are still challenges and obstacles to face:
     The perception of businessmen about the quality of the processing and resolution of
     procedures and specific management has not changed.61
     Several factors affect economic competition: the perception of rivalry between the regulatory
     and competition policies, the excessive lawsuits (amparo proceedings); the difficulties in
     collecting fines; the legal limitations and the lack of means to prevent or sanction the abuse
     of market power, to make house calls, and to carry out amnesty and leniency programs.

 Inorder to counteract these difficulties, strengthen the advantages and increase the quality of the
 legal and normative framework that promotes investment and the development of productive
 activities of businesses, the Program backed by the World Bank supports the following key
 actions:


 61See: CEESP, Survey of governability and businessdevelopment2005 (Sept. 2005).


                                                      76

                                               Subsecretaria de Industria y Comercio

                                               Commerce)

  ECONOMIA
                                               Mexico City, January 27,2006



        Facilitate the entrance of new businesses through the implementation of the SARE (Rapid
        Business Opening System) in 85 municipalities, meaningthat:

             1. Operations start in 72 hours, allowing for the generation of resources and
                recovery of initial investment inless time,
             2. The time that the installed infrastructure for the development of a business i s idle
                i s reduced, which generates more resources during its useful life,
             3. The number of visits that have to be carried out to different governmental offices
                are reduced, decreasing time and travel costs,
             4. The number of procedures to be carried out is reduced, meaning that the cost to
                complete various procedures falls, as well as the time lost to comply with them,
             5. Administrative costs for the government as a whole are saved; by establishing a
                single module for the reception and resolution of the paperwork required for
                opening a business,
             6. Municipal collection is formalized by makingthe necessary normative adaptations
                to give legal support to the SARE, which at the same time translates into legal
                certainty for individuals (there is no discretionary collection or "voluntary"
                quotas). Likewise, when formalizing municipal collection, municipal authorities
                are offered budgetary certainty and municipal treasuries are strengthened.
             7. Incentives to be established in the informal market are reduced by facilitating the
                opening of businesses. Similarly, this encourages businesses already established in
                the informal market to incorporate formally and
             8. Corruption i s reduced, by influencing a decrease in the number of businesses
                established informally, and as a consequence, the wrongful payments made to
                operate in this situation. Also municipal finances are made stronger by expanding
                the universe of formal businessesthat pay taxes.


     0   Reduce transaction costs for the private sector and citizens through the review of 355
         Regulatory Impact Assessments (MIRs) in 2005 in order to improve the cost benefit
         analysis of regulation, which will:

             1. Contribute to the correct design of regulation avoiding: i)the generation of
                unnecessary obstacles for business activities, ii)an increase in the prices of goods
                 and services, iii)the discouragement of productive investment, iv) the generation
                of spaces for corruption, and v) a reduction inthe quality of services,

             2. Increase the quality of the legal system as a whole and of legal instruments in
                particular, with the purpose of increasing its benefits, reducing its costs and
                increasing its efficiency and
             3. Create incentives for business innovation and competitiveness, by eliminating and
                 simplifying paperwork, reducing costs and promoting the initiative of individuals.
             4. Promote transparency and public consultation by having documents available on

                 distortions in the markets; iii)i s consistent with the legal framework; iv) produces
                 benefits which are greater than costs; and v) i s built jointly with the productive
                 sectors.

         Strengthen the economic competition policy and its application by promoting
         homogeneous competitive criteria within the Public Administration through the
         establishment of Cooperation Agreements between the CFC and 15 states and with
         Cofemer, CRE and Profeco, allowing greater coordination and efficiency between the
         Commission, regulatory bodies, and state governments, which will promoting efficiency
         inthe prevention, detection and sanction of monopolypractices.

LOGISTICS COSTS

One of the Mexican competitive advantages i s related to its geographical location, because it
facilitates the transportation of merchandise. Exploiting this advantage requires that the Mexican
borders turn into efficient ports of entry and exit for goods, services, people and businesses. It
 also requires the simplification and harmonization of international trade procedures and an
 adequate infrastructure level that facilitates the transport of merchandise once it has cleared
customs.

The growing dynamics in the movement of merchandise related to international trade made
 necessary the development and modernization of a maritime port infrastructure to accommodate
 this growth, to strengthen the integration of the Mexican ports in the main international logistic
 routes, offering world class services.

 The above resulted in significant increases in the installed infrastructure capacity, in the
 modernization of port equipment, in the productivity of handling containers which exceeded
 international standards, in the reduction of the time ships remained inport, among others.



                                                     78

                                              Subsecretaria de Industria y Comercio
                                              (Under-Secretariat    of   Industry    and
                                              Commerce)


SECRETARIA DE                                 Official Letter No. 400.2006.003
  ECONOMIA
                                              Mexico City, January 27,2006


In spite of the above, there are still challenges to overcome, like improving the coordination
between the different authorities of the ports, achieving a single inspection, having an electronic
system of information exchange between the different port actors, and continuing with the
process of modernization and expansion of the infrastructure and maritime-port equipment.

In the issue of foreign trade procedures, the schemes to cover customs proceedings were very
complex: multiple formats, discretion of the customs authorities for fee classifications, lack of
legal codes harmonization, among others.

Regardinginfrastructure, just five years ago we had a highway network whose axis was Mexico
City, which meant high costs for transportation and thus loss of competitiveness.

Additionally, budget restrictions had an effect on the performance of logistic networks. As a
result, very creative public-private alliance schemes were developed to foster the transportation
and communication systems in an integral way.

The above mentioned actions are integrated with other policies that had direct and positive
impacts on logistics costs reduction inthe year 2005:
    For the second consecutive year, total infrastructure investment (public plus private) was at a
    historical high, around 340 billion pesos.
    Modernization of more than 75% of the highway corridors, with the signing of the
    Multimodal Corridors Development Coordination Agreement (with the participation of the
    different actors that participate in the transportation and merchandise distribution logistics
    chains).
    Fiscal stimuli to small carriers for the elimination of obsolete units (scrap vehicles) with
    amounts from 15% of the value of the new vehicle.
    Record levels in the transportation of cargo by railroad (more than 90 million tons) and air
    (more than 492,000 tons)
    Increase in telecommunications coverage (fixed telephone lines grew 50 percent, the density
    of mobile telephony increased three-fold, and an accelerated expansion of the Internet users
    that reached 16 million).

                                                Subsecretaria de Industria y Comercio

  ECONOML4
                                                Mexico City, January 27,2006


0    Modernization of physical infrastructure in the country's customs and the battle against the
     illegal introduction of merchandise, among others.

The path to reach levels of competitiveness comparable with the leader countries regarding
logistics i s still arduous. That i s why Mexico should make an active effort to develop and exploit
the first level transportation infrastructure to maximize the benefits of opening, speed up customs
clearance, modernize the port infrastructure, and a create a better regulatory environment.

In parallel, it is necessary to alert Mexican companies about the relationship between the
competitiveness of their products andor services and their key logistic processes (customer
service, transportation, inventory management, and order processing) and support (storage,
merchandize handling, purchasing, and supplying, product planning, bottling, packaging and
packing and information management).

In other words, in an environment of growing global competition where a relatively small
movement in costs may define the permanence of a company in the market, logistic turns into a
key link inthe competitiveness chain of the country.

It i s necessary to guarantee strategic actions regarding logistics which give continuity and go
beyond the results that have been attained. For this, the Program backed by the World Bank
supports the following key actions:


     0   Make more efficient commercial processes, reduce foreign trade costs and strengthen
         logistic practices through:

         a) The harmonization of tariffs through the reduction of 3 and 10 points on 76% of the
              imports tariffs (TIGIE), equivalent to 9,177 tariffs lines, which covers a wide variety
              of inputs, articles, machinery and indispensable equipment for the productive life of
              national companies.

              This generalized reduction has benefited approximately 130,000 national companies
              devoted to foreign trade, because the advantages will be reflected in companies'
              imports operations, due to the favorable impact on production costs of at least 3
              percent. In this item, estimated general savings for the companies will be about US$
              446.4 million dollars. Likewise, this program allowed for a reduction in the tariff
              dispersion from 14tariff levels to only 8 Most FavoredNation tariff levels.

             ships.

             etc.) decreasing operations and logistics costs in the merchandise commercialization
             chain.

             scales, among others) to improve the commercial logistics, speed up the import and
             export merchandise flow, and decrease operations costs.

         e) Completion of the "Study for the increase of efficiency and productivity in the port of
             Veracruz" which will permit the analysis of cargo flows inthe port inorder to identify
             the problems which affect the productivity of its different companies and authorities,
             and propose the necessary measurements to increase its efficiency and operational
             coordination.




 The energy industry is an important supplier of inputs, such as electricity and fuels, which have a
 direct influence on the competitiveness of productive activities. Thus, it is vital to have a modern
 and efficient energy sector, which guarantees a timely and sufficient supply, with high quality
 standards and competitive prices, which continuously supports the development of the rest of
 national industry.

                         ANNEX 5: ANALYTICAL UNDERPINNINGS

Factors Markets and TotalFactor Productivity
1.    A complementary framework to the growth diagnostic (section III) elicit key elements and
                                                                                to
priorities for competitiveness/productivity is to look at the factors of production, their markets, and how
well the factors are used. National output i s determined by the quality and quantity of inputs-labor
(human capital), capital, and infrastructure-as    well as a residual known as total factor productivity
(TFP). TFP, which can also be thought of as know-how, is determined by some combination of
innovationhowledge and investment climate characteristics. Well functioning factors markets and the
efficient use of factors of productions are key for competitiveness and increasing productivity and
growth. A brief summary of those themes i s provided in the following sections, further bolstering the
analytical underpinnings for the DPL agenda. Detailed rationalizations for specific policies within each
subject area are includedin section IV describing the Bank-supported program.

Labor
2.    Labor regulations were identified as a constraint in the WEF executive opinion survey. Doing
Business in 2006 gives Mexico a 60 out of 100 (higher numbers mean more rigid regulation) on both its
"Rigidity of hours index" and its "Difficulty of firing index." In contrast, Chile scored a 20 on each of
these indices. As part of the AAA, the World Bank estimated the impact of a proposed labor law reform
in 2004. The analysis found that the proposal to relax the regulationof working hours alone would cause
the "Rigidity of hours index" to drop from 60 to 40. Partly as a result of strict labor regulation, Mexico
has a large informal sector, and competition from the informal sector was one of the complaints of
entrepreneursinthe focus groups. IMCO has also highlighted a number of labor market shortcomings.

3.    Despite these shortcomings, labor does not emerge as one of the top constraints. This is
corroborated by the IMCO quantitative estimates (Table 3) and other studies. An IDB (2004) study
shows that the Mexican labor market performs well relative to other labor markets in the region, with
little adverse impact on unemployment and mobility. Figure 12 shows that Mexican's consider
unemployment, low wages, and job instability to be less of a problem than every other country in the
Latin America sample. The same IDB report also shows that Mexico has the best "quality of labor
relations" and "overall efficiency in labor market performance" in the region (Figure 13). Partly because
labor is not a top constraint and partly because the subject, of labor reform i s extremely politically
sensitive, major structural reforms in labor markets are not included in the DPL.

4.    Labor productivity is impacted by the quality of human capital, which i s largely determined by
education and training. This DPL concentrates on adult education and training (e.g., SME support)
programs, while traditional education issues (primary/secondary/tertiary) are addressed through a series
of separate Bank projects. The WEF survey named an "inadequately educated workforce" as one of the
most problematic factors for doing business and the average education attainment of the Mexican adult
population is very low-7.6   years-compared with 11-12 years for other OECD countries. IMCO also
considers the education shortfall to be a factor that stunts Mexico's competitiveness, with the prospects
for the poor beingparticularly dismal: the poorest 10 percent of the population averages only 4 years of
formal education. Ideally, SME support programs can help overcome education shortfalls. However, a
2004 World Bank study6* (associated with the AAA) highlighted several shortcomings in existing
Mexican SME programs, including a pressing need for impact evaluations. The rationale for specific
training programs supported by the proposed DPL is discussed in section IV.

Capital
5.    Credit and capital markets are not very effective in Mexico. A telling indicator is the level of
domestic credit to the private sector, which was a paltry 17 percent of GDP in 2004. In comparison,
values in many East Asian countries are over 100percent. Not surprisingly, all the surveys highlight that
issue. Both the focus groups and the WEF executive survey identified it as the most problematic factor.
The IMCO study shows that while relatively small improvements have an impact, the big payoff comes
with significant improvements in that sector. Given the importance and complexity of this topic, it i s
being handled as a separate Programmatic Finance and Growth DPL, which will be presented to the
Board inFY06.

Znfrastructure
6.    There is ample evidence of the strong impact of infrastructure on economic growth and poverty.
Yet, Mexico lags behindin that area relative to its major partners and competitors. Calderon and Serven
(2004) have shown that if Mexico were to improve its infrastructure stock and quality to the levels of
Costa Rica (the region's infrastructure leader), Mexico's GDP would increase by 1.7 percentage points
and inequality would be significantly reduced. Thus, not surprisingly, various components of

  World Bank (2004b).


                                                    102

infrastructure have been identified as highly problematic by several sources. The focus groups considered
distribution, transport costs and logistics to be an important competitiveness constraint. They considered
railroads to be limited and costly, roads to be slow, expensive, and unsafe, and airports and ports to lack
capacity for high load volumes. A 2005 study by the U.S. International Trade Commission confirms that
Mexico falls short on a number of indicators of logistics quality, especially when compared with Chile
and countries in East Asia. Their evidence comes from a survey of 51countries which derived scores for
customs, airports, seaports, regulation, burden of security procedures, and availability of complementary
resources.With the exception of airports, Mexico falls inthe bottom 35 percent of the countries surveyed
on all of these measures (Table 8).

the quality, efficiency, and cost of customs procedures, whether specific to airports and seaports or not specific to

productivity, the degree of transparency of regulation, and the degree to which foreign firms receive the same

processing cargo through seaports, both overall and for individual cargo procedures, including the cost of
repositioning equipment.The complementary resources score captures the presence of complementary resources

on efficiency.

7.     A separate piece of empirical work has shown the importance of infrastructure in transport costs
and trade flows. Specifically, an analysis of bilateral trade data showed that a deterioration of
infrastructure from the median to the 75" percentile raises transport costs by 12 percentage points and
reduces traded volumes by 28 percent.69

 8.    The Mexico focus groups highlighted the absence of intermodal facilities ("dry ports") that would
improve logistics management and reduce the cost of transporting goods, especially exports. Customs
laws were seen as very complicated and difficult to comply with. The data supports this assessment:
logistics costs inMexico are over 20 percent of product value, compared with an average of 9 percent for
OECD countries.70A reduction of those logistic costs to a level of twelve percentage points would have
 significant impact on economic activity and job creation, as shown elsewhere. Elevated transport costs
 and times have also led to high inventory levels for businesses: during the 1990s, Mexico had 90 percent


69 Limao and Venables (2001).
70 Guasch (2004).


                                                          103

higher raw materials inventory levels on average than the U.S."         IMCO finds that Mexico is not
capitalizing on its enormous competitive advantage of proximity to the U.S. because transport costs are
so high. Indeed, Mexico spends almost double what the U.S. does on transport as a percentageof GDP.72
This DPL has chosen to support various improvements in logistics and trade facilitation. However, it
does not include major transport infrastructure projects as they are beyond the scope of this policy-based
loan.

9.    Over the last decade, different groups have called for the implementation of far reaching reforms in
the energy sector as a necessary condition to attract sufficient private financing, improve efficiency,
lower costs, and expand the sector's contribution to the federal budget and the economy. While a national
consensus has not been reached with regard to the nature and extent of energy sector reforms, the GOM
has gradually taken conservative steps to allow limited private sector participation and improve
transparency and regulatory accounting. This DPL focuses on measures to improve efficiency, quality,
and price in the energy sector, independent of major reforms. Energy costs comprise 10.5 percent of the
total production costs of manufacturing industry products (as an aggregate). This is comparable to labor
costs which are in the range of 4-20 percent. High energy prices were identified as a constraint (although
not a top one) by the focus groups and unit electricity costs in Mexico in 2002 were higher than every
other country in IMCO's list of 44 comparator countries. Moreover, while in most countries energy
prices have dropped in the last five years, they have increased in Mexico. It should be noted, that while
most of the discussion in Mexico (as in the China debate) focused on labor costs, for many products in
manufacturing, energy costs as a share of total costs are larger than labor costs. Quality of service is
particularly poor for SMEs, which often experience variations in frequency and voltage and interruptions
in service. High energy prices and poor quality then translate into reduced SME competitiveness. Large
enterprises have fewer quality problems because they are generally served by more reliable, high-tension
wires. The DPL's approach in the electricity sector is informed by the World Bank's Infrastructure
Public Expenditure Review for Mexico, which was completed in September 2005.

        compared with other countries. Service interruptions and voltage fluctuations affect the overall
        productivity levels of industries and prevent the installation o f modem electronic equipment.
        Electricity transmission and distribution also needs to be improved considerably to reduce losses.
        Capacity. To meet projected demand, large investments in new capacity are needed.

11. There are also issues in the telecom sector impacting productivity, but overall, they are of a lesser
magnitude than those in the other infrastructure sectors. Mexico's telecommunications sector grew
considerably as a result of the privatization of Telmex in the early 1990s and the gradual sector
liberalization that began in 1995. The Fox administration's efforts to transform Mexico into a knowledge
economy, mainly through its e-Mexico initiative, have also resulted in notable improvements in e-
government. However, despite having been the early reformer in L A C (after Chile), Mexico lags behind
all OECD countries and many Latin American countries in terms o f wide-spread access to telephone and
broadband internet infrastructure. Telecommunications prices are much higher in Mexico than in similar
countries. This holds true for business and residential telephone monthly subscription fees, residential
telephone connection charges, and the cost of a three minute local call. The installation and monthly
service charges for businesses to lease a local line (known as a local loop) in Mexico City are also high
compared with other major cities in Latin America.

12. Mexico's telecommunications regulatory framework and institutionsremain weak and have to some
extent failed to foster competition. This has benefited the incumbent, Telmex, and led to the high prices
mentioned above, adversely impacting economic activity. The lack of competition will be addressed
through enhancements to the Federal Law on Economic Competition supported by this DPL.While other
telecom sector improvements ought to be secured, the priority in terms o f impact i s in the other
infrastructure sectors. As a result, this DPL focuses on the logistics, trade facilitation and energy
components of infrastructure.

Total Factor Productivity: Innovation and the Investment Climate
 13. Over 1960-2000 the bulk of the differences in growth between countries (45-90 percent) was
accounted for by total factor productivity (TIT)-the      productivity contributions above those made by
physical and human capital.74A recent study on Mexico supported these results, finding that swings in
growth have been mostly accounted for by changes in the contribution of TFP, as opposed to factor
inputs.75The bad news is that the contribution of TFP to GDP growth was negative on average inMexico
between 1980 and 2003, although there has been an improvement since 1996 (see Figure 2). A separate
study found that national learningcapacity, as proxiedby TF'P, has accounted for the vast majority of the
difference in output between Korea and Mexico since 1960 (Figure 15). Korea developed its national
capacity to absorb knowledge in the 1970sand 1980s, whereas Mexico did not.76
