14. Early growth research emphasized technological progress in explaining TFP, suggesting that
differences in growth rates were driven by differences in the technologies adopted. The dramatic
acceleration in income levels among the fast growing countries over the last 200 years can be understood
by improvements in technology. "Technology" in this sense, however, i s not limited to scientific
breakthroughs of the kind that might merit a patent. It can also include more modest advances, as well as
new and better ways to organize productionprocesses, interact with consumers, or distribute goods.

15. Recent research has emphasized that TFT can also be understood to encompass more than just
differences in technology. The broader environment in which firms operate-in           other words, the
investment climate-matters     too. A better investment climate can directly improve productivity by
reducing unjustified costs and risks flowing from government policies and behaviors. By making it more
attractive to develop and adopt new and better ways of doing things, a better investment climate will help
productivity through its impact on technology as well.77

16. A separate way to measure productivity is through labor productivity, or GDP per hour worked.
Mexico has the second lowest GDP per hour worked of all OECD countries, which equates to a -70
percentage gap with respect to U.S. levels (Figure 16). Growth in labor productivity has also been low.
GDP per hour worked grew only 1.3 percent annually between 1995 and 2004 in Mexico, making it the
fourth worst performer in the OECD (Figure 17).

yielding negative total growth duringthe period 1990-2004 (Figure 18).78

18. Basedon the above productivity evidence, this DPL supports measures to encourage innovation and
technology adoption and improve the investment climate through better regulation, competition, and
trade facilitation.

Innovation and Technology Development
19. According to a recent World Bank flagship report, "technological innovation is a key source of
economic, income, and employment growth. This premise has been validated by theory and empiricism
and by now has been accepted by most                         Another World Bank flagship finds that what
distinguished the successful resource-abundant countries (Australia, Canada, and the Scandinavian
countries) from the disappointingLAC experience was precisely their ability to learn from abroad-their
national innovative capacity.80 Current research has classified the Mexican national science and
technology (S&T) learning model as one of "passive FDI-dependent learning." This means that the
country does not undertake significant efforts to facilitate local absorption and dissemination of foreign
S&T knowledge, e.g., by upgrading local workers' capabilities or by targeting the kind of FDI most
relevant to the country's long-term technological learning interests. This contrasts with "active FDI-
dependent learning" countries, such as Ireland and Singapore, that have a proven ability to actively
facilitate and promote technological learning from FDI." Finally, entrepreneursinthe focus groups noted
the lack of government support for R&D. Given the centrality of innovation in driving productivity
growth, it i s a major focus of this DPL. The proposed loan i s also complemented by the Innovation for
Competitiveness loan to Mexico (approved inMay 2005).




79De Ferranti and others (2003). Closing the Gap in Education and Technology.
''De  Ferranti and others (2002).Natural Resources to the Knowledge Economy.
  Soubbotina (2005).


                                                       108

Regulation
20.     Regulation deficiencies were highlighted as a key issue in several big-picture sources. The focus
groups complained about the high numbers of norms and regulations, as well as frequent changes to
them, which create uncertainty and provide opportunities for corruption. The WEF survey found that
inefficient government bureaucracy was one of most problematic factors for doing business in Mexico.
Doing Business in 2006 found that starting a business takes 58 days in Mexico, compared with 5 days in
the U.S. Dealing with licenses takes 222 days at a cost of 159 percent of income per capita, while it only
takes 70 days in the U.S. at a cost of 17 percent of per capita income. IMCO also identifies regulation as
a problem, ranking Mexico 36 out of 45 countries in terms of ease of opening a business. According to
COFEMER (Comisidn Federal para la Mejora Regulatoria),the cost of regulation inMexico amounts to
about 15 percent of GDP. COFEMER calculates that the benefits of potential deregulation could reduce
this cost to 5 percent of GDP.

21.     A final piece of evidence regarding regulation comes from a study of the contribution to economic
growth of changes in different structural policies. The study analyzed the contribution to the projected
change in Mexico's growth rate-during the period 2000-2010 versus the period 1991-1999-of                          an
improvement in the government burden to the 75" percentile of Latin America and the Caribbean.
Specifically, the study found that reducing the government burden in Mexico would add 0.15 percentage
points to per capita output growth during2000-2010 with respect to the 1990~.'~

Competition
22.     Barriers to competition benefit some firms but deny opportunities and increase costs to other firms
and to consumers. They also weaken incentives for protected firms to innovate and improve their
productivity. Increasing competitive pressure can increase the probability of firm innovation by more
than 50 per~ent.'~Therefore, reforms to enhance competition could have significant effects on growth. A
study of the impact of pro-competitive regulatory reform in several industries in the United States found
that annual welfare gains in the part of GDP affected by reform were more than 7 percent, with 90
percent of the benefits flowing to consumers.84Increasing competitive pressure can increase the
probability of firm innovation by more than 50 percent. According to IMCO's research, a 10 percent
improvement in the variables that compose the "competition" dimension could increase investment per
worker in Mexico by 7.5 percent (Table 3).

23.     That said, competition in Mexico is less than optimal. Deficiencies in statutory authority and
judicial review processes constrain the Federal Competition Commission's (CFC) ability to address
anticompetitive conditions effectively and efficiently. The CFC's decisions and proceedings are open to
amparo actions ("protection appeal^")'^ from dissatisfied parties, which can take many years to resolve.
Even if CFC's orders survive the "amparos," the imposed fines are not collected by the CFC but rather
by the fiscal department of the municipality of residency of the fined party. From 1993 to September this
year, only 3.3 percent of the total amount fined by CFC has been collected, while 72 percent i s under
reconsideration and 20 percent i s under amparo and fiscal nullity action.

24. According to IMCO's research on the impact of 10 dimensions on investment for 45 economies,
Mexico ranks 33 in terms of level of competition and formality of the markets. Furthermore, according to

a recent governance survey, the two largest market obstacles to business development identifiedby firms
are government monopolies and private monopolies. The Global Competition Review 2003 ranking of
competition regimes in 25 countries ranks Mexico near the bottom with a score of 2.25, in a rating from
0-5, only above Greece (2.0) and Argentina (1.5) since Mexico's competition regime is considered to be
severely undercut by courts.

Trade facilitation
25.   Exports to the U.S., particularly since NAFTA, have been an important pillar of the Mexican
economy. Research suggests that Mexico's global exports would have been about 25 percent lower
without NAFTA, and FDIwould have been about 40 percent less. Also, the amount of time requiredfor
Mexican manufacturers to adopt U.S.technological innovations was cut in half with NAFTA (see Box 1
for lessons from NAFTA).86Despite the gains from NAFTA, Mexico has been losing export market
share within the U.S. in recent years. Of the top ten types of Mexican imports in the U.S., only two-
petroleumand agricultural products-gained    market share between 2001 and 2005 (Table 9). The largest
falls were seen in communications equipment, computer equipment, motor vehicles, and audiohideo
equipment. China was the biggest gainer in all but one of the categories where Mexico registered a loss.

26. A separate study found that the productivity of non-maquiladora plants in Mexico that start
exporting grows substantially in the 2-3 years prior to entering the export market. Their productivity
continues to grow relative to that of exporters and non-exporters for at least 2-3 years after entry,
suggesting that learning-by-exporting effects exist. Newly entering maquiladoras also seem to improve
their performance in the years after entry. The study also found that export experience is a significant
determinant of productivity growth: each year of exporting experience i s associated with a 4.5 percent
rise in TFP.~~

27.    Despite the changes wrought by NAFTA and the benefits of exporting, the U.S. market i s not
always easily accessible, especially for smaller firms and those far from the border, and costs to comply
with export procedures can be high. Doing Business in 2006 reports that "time for export" i s 18 days in
Mexico, double the number of days in the US. Focus groups also identified the lack of mechanisms to
facilitate exports as a major constraint.

Box 1: The Lessons from NAFTA
A recent World Bank study, "Lessons from NAFTA for LatinAmerica and the Caribbean" (Lederman, Maloney, and
Serven (2003)) highlights the following lessons:

        NAFTA can be credited for boosting Mexico's growth in the post 1994 period. By inducing a reciprocal
        reduction in trade barriers and locking-in the reforms of recent years, it has yielded a positive impact on
        trade flows, foreign direct investments, and growth of industrial productivity. It is also responsible for the
        creation of many newjobs and somereduction in poverty rates of recent ye

        Despite the positive growth effect, NAETAhas not sufficed to guarantee income convergence among North
        American countries. This reflects mainly pending items in Mexico's policy agenda including institutional
        gaps (i.e., corruption and rule of law), deficiencies in education (both coverage and quality), a passive
        innovation policy, the lack of critical infrastructure (especially in lagging regions) and some weaknesses in
        macroeconomic policy. The experience has demonstrated that while positive, an FTA with a developed
        partner is not sufficient to increase growth on a sustainable basis unless an agenda of complementary
        reforms i s pursued.

        NAFTAbenefits have been concentrated in states in the North and Center of Mexico. Southern states have
        not seen much of an impact, due to key deficiencies ininstitutions, education and infrastructure.

         Contrary to some predictions, NAFTA has not had a devastating effect on Mexico's agriculture. In fact,
         both domestic production and agricultural trade rose during the NAFTA years. The negative effects did not
         materialize because aggregate demand expandedrapidly inbothMexico andthe US.inthe latter half of the
         199Os,some segments of Mexican agriculture recorded substantial incre               productivity (esp. irrigated
                and outdated subsidies were transformed into targeted efficient             ms (e.g., Procampo) which
                  transfers to farmers from current and future production levels.

                   experience also shows that there are some key remaining barriers to trade that have limited the
         growth potential of certain sectors. Inparticular, restrictive rules of origin affecting clothing exports to the
         U.S. market seem to have limited the ability of Mexican exporters to take full advantage of NAFTA
         preferences.

Source: World Bank (2005b).

                   ANNEX 6: DETAILEDPOLICY AREA DESCRIPTIONS

POLICY AREA1: INNOVATION, TECHNOLOGY                  DEVELOPMENT,TRAININGAND

INNOVATIONDEVELOPMENT
                AND TECHNOLOGY

Description and Challenges
1.    Innovation is a major driver for economic growth and productivity. Roughly half of cross-
country differences in per capita income and growth are driven by differences in total factor productivity,
generally attributed to technological development and innovative capacity." These should be understood
in a broad sense, referring not only to the ability to engage in researchanddevelopment (R&D) activities,
which eventually may or may not be transformed into new outputs, but also to the efficient use of modem
operating technologies and to the adoption and adaptation of new ones. According to Prescott (1998) to
understand large international income differences, it is necessary to explain differences in productivity
(TFP). He argues that one of the main candidates to explainthose gaps is the resistanceto the adoption of
new technologies and to the efficient use of current operating technologies, which in turn are conditioned
by the institutional and policy arrangements a society employs (investment climate variables). Escribano
and Guasch (2005a, 2005b) using firm level data from investment climate surveys, investigate the
determinants of productivity using a large array of variables in four large categories: redtape, corruption
and crime; infrastructure; quality, innovation and labor skills; and corporate governance. They find that
in most countries of Latin America and Asia covered by their study variables related to innovation,
quality and training are important determinants of productivity.

2.    Lederman and Saenz (2003) present econometric evidence that suggests that innovation outcomes,
namely patents per capita, are an important explanation of the levels of development observed around the
world. In the other hand, spending on R&D, which often includes not only basic research but most
expenditure on adaptation of technologies, has typically been used as an indicator of innovation inputs.89
Lederman and Maloney (2003) examine the relationship between the R&D effort and the development
process and find that not only the share of GDP dedicated to R&D increases with income per capita in
the average country, but also that several high growth comparator countries (such as Finland, Korea and
Israel) had dramatic take-offs relative to the benchmark, a path that China and India have recently
followed. Several studies have estimated economic gains to the firms investing in R&D ranging from 25
to 30 percent, a returnfar above the average return on capital estimated around 7 percent." Furthermore,
if one considers the impact of firms' R&D spending on the economy through knowledge spillovers
improving other companies' products and production techniques by technology adoption and learning,
the return to R&D increase several fold. Lederman and Maloney (2004) in a panel of countries estimate
that the social returns to R&D exceed the return to investments in physical capital by a factor 6 to 10
dependingupon the initial level of income per capita. For Mexico, the calculations suggest a social return
to R&D above 60 percent.

3.    But R&D expenses only (or derived input measures from them) do not reveal all the intricacies at
work. The newly available data from innovation surveys has opened new routes for the assessment of the


88 Hall and Jones (1999), Dollar and Wolf (1997).
89 Although it would be possiblethat firms may have innovative sales without spending one dollar on R&D, it has
beenhypothesizedthat in general firms and countries need to spend on R&D to develop absorptiveor learning
capacity (see Cohen and Levinthal(1990), Forbesand Wield (2000), Griffith, Redding and Van Reenen(2003),
Pavitt (2001)).
  See Jones and Williams (1998) for areview.


                                                       112

contribution of innovation to productivity by enabling an explicit estimation of the innovation production
function." Recent examples of this line of researchthat follow a structural modeling approachg2-which
owes much to Griliches (see e.g., Griliches, 1999)-are             presented in CrCpon, Duguet and Mairesse
(1998), Griffith, Reddingand Van Reenen (2000), Loof and Heshmati (2001), Klomp and Van Leeuwen
(2001) and Correa, Sgnchez Garcia and Singh (2005). These studies investigate the channels linking
investment in knowledge and innovation to productivity performance at the firm level and, in general,
they find a significant impact of innovation output on the level of productivity.

4.    The Mexican innovation system performs below par compared with economies of its income
level with a low ability to translate investments into innovation. Lederman et a1 (2003) analyze two
key indicators of innovation performance, total factor productivity (TF'P) growth and the number of
patents produced, and conclude that Mexico is currently under-performing in both dimensions when
compared with other Latin American, East Asian and OECD countries. Dahlman and Kuznetsov (2005)
construct knowledge economy indexes for Mexico and find that its relatively low ranking is due to
weaknesses in all four knowledge economy pillars, namely economic incentive regime, education,
innovation, and information and communication technologies. The low performance with regards
innovation stems from a combination of factors:
   Insufficient private sector involvement in innovation activities. According to the innovation survey
    (2001), in the period 1999-2000, only 28 percent of the Mexican firms reported to have been
    involved in an innovation project and 13 percent to have performed R&D activities. There is no
    information available with respect to the total amount that Mexican firms spend in innovative
    activities. However private investment in R&D, both as a proportion of GDP and as a percentage of
    total national R&D expenditures, i s very low. Mexican firms invest a little above 0.1 percent of GDP
    inR&D, which comparesto 1.5 percent inthe OECD area, 0.4 percent inBrazil, 0.3 percent inChile,
    and 0.8 percent in China. Large companies (above 500 employees) account for the majority (69
    percent) of private R&D.
    Total R&D investment,public and private, is alsofar below the OECD levels: expenditures on R&D
    as a percentageof GDP inMexico (0.4 percent) i s the lowest of OECD countries and inferior to other
    emerging economies such as Chile (0.6 percent), Brazil (1.Ipercent), or China (1.O percent).
    High cost, excessive economic risk and lack of access tofinance are the most important limitations
    to innovation according to Mexican firms. The innovation survey investigates the factors that
    represent a major constraint to increased innovation. The most important ones are summarized in
    Figure 19.

e Low level of cooperation in the development of new products'and processes: in the period 1999-
  2000, most new or significantly improved products were developed within the firm; only 15 percent
  of f i r m s reported to have collaborated with other firms. In the development of new processes,
  cooperative behavior is somewhat more prevalent with 26 percent of the firms working with other
  firms.

  Weak environment for entrepreneurship resulting in few potential high-growth start-ups suitable for
  venture capital funding. Enterprises are constrained by traditional business values and by lack of
  culture of risk-taking.Lack of venture capital funding i s also a limiting factor for the appearance of
  technology-based start-up companies. Local and national groups that foster networking among
  entrepreneurs, angel investors and venture funds, remain weak.

  Low Science and Technology skill base: Investment in human resources follows a similar pattern to
  that of R&D intensity. In 1995, there were 0.6 researchers per thousand employment in Mexico,
  compared with 0.8 researchers in China (1.1 researchers in 2002) and the average of 5.8 researchers
  per thousand employment for OECD countries. The balance of researchersin industry and the public
  sector is too much skewed towards the public sector, with only 16 percent of all researchers working
  inindustry. By contrast, the OECD averageis 64 percent.
  Inadequate linkages between public RbD and the productive sector: few companies have important
  linkages with public R&D institutions (12 percent of innovative firms) and universities (12 percent).
  This implies limited private sector access to basic and applied research, reduced relevance and
  commercialization of R&D; and a low return to public R&D investments. L6pez-Acevedo (2001)
  finds that when firms have linkages with public research institutes, their likelihood of increased
  technology absorption increases significantly. Similar findings exist for the majority of other OECD
  countries. Evidence of the low level of public-private interactions i s provided by the low provision of



                                                                 114

    technological services in the public R&D centers. Figure 20 shows the level of self-financing in the
    centers for technological developments associated with CONACYT. Even though the number of
    projects developed with firms has increased from 1,391 in 2000 to 3,695 in 2003, more progress
    needs to be made.
                      Figure 20: Proportion of Self-resources Over Total Budget, 2003




    Limited international participation and linkages: Despite being one of the ten largest economies,
    Mexico has relatively few and scattered links with foreign knowledge centers, only funds 0.25
    percent of the world's R&D, and its science base produces only 0.7 percent of new global S&T
    knowledge, as measured by publications in top S&T journals. Mexico's contribution to the global
    pool of science and technology based knowledge and its integration with other OECD countries'
    innovation systems, is deficient.
    Heavy reliance on foreign technology: In 2003, Mexico earned the negligible amount of US$54
    million from technology intangibles (patent acquisition, technology licensing payments, protected
    knowledge and know-how transfer services), compared with the US$608 million it had to buy from
    abroad. With atechnology coveragerate of 9 percent, Mexico i s at the bottom of OECD countries.
GovernmentActions
5.    The Mexican Government has made competitiveness a national priority and has embarked on a
comprehensive strategy in order to strengthen Mexico's position recognizing that a key challenge for
Mexico competitiveness and trade is the low level of involvement of the private sector in innovation
activities and the very limited industrial production with high value-added.

6.    The Government's strategy to boost innovation in the country i s articulated in the 2001-2006
Special Plan for Science and Technology (PECyT), which i s an integral part of the 2001-2006 National
Development Plan. The PECyT defines a set of strategic priorities for the S&T sector that address the
sector's shortcomings mentioned above by focusing on three overarching objectives: first, implement a
coherent policy framework for S&T; second, increase S&T capacity; and third, promote private sector
competitiveness and innovation (Figure 21).




                                                     115

7.     Mexico has a new legal framework for the Science and Technology Sector. To provide
structure to the National Innovation System, Mexico enacted in 2002 a new legal framework for the S&T
sector through the Law for Science and Technology and the Law for CONACYT. Within the framework
set by these laws, several governing bodies for the S&T sector were created: the Consejo General de
Investigacio'n CientljCca y Desarrollo Tecnolo'gico plays an essential role in defining and coordinating
public policies across different ministerial institutions at the Federal level; the Conferencia Nacional de
Ciencia y Tecnologia brings together state level S&T governing institutions; and the Foro Consultivo
CientljCco y Tecnoldgico, important as a regular consultation mechanism for public and private S&T
partners. The Laws also redefine the status of CONACYT as a "quasiministry," depending directly from
Presidency. In the recent years, CONACYT's mission has refocused on meeting the S&T needs of
different government agencies and society in general, particularly the productive sector.

8.     Decentralization, information and networking are also important components of the new
institutional setup. An important decentralization mechanism that facilitates the coordination of
innovation public policies at the federal and state level are the Consejos Estatales de Ciencia y
Tecnologia. At the moment 25 Consejos have been created and 7 more are underway. CONACYT itself
i s also committed to the decentralization process and has created 7 Regional centers that help broaden the
base of potential participants inthe CONACYT's programs. With regardsto information and networking,
several initiatives have developed: RENIECYT, as the national registry of private and public institutions
that perform innovation activities (in the first semester of 2005, there were 1,699 registered entries, of
which 924 correspond to firms; these figures are respectively 35 percent and 54 percent larger than inthe
previous year); SINECYT, another registry that compiles data on individuals qualified to perform
evaluation of S&T projects, a crucial ingredient in the implementation of innovation support programs
(10,400 entries by June 2005, 10.2 percent more than in the previous year); and SIICYT, an integral S&T
information website created in 2001 that receives an average of 7,570 monthly visits.




                                                      116

9.   Although there have been renewed efforts to increase S&T capacity in Mexico, financial
efforts are still very limited. The PECYT establishes as an objective for the period 2001-2006 to
increase R&D expenditure as a percentage of GDP from 0.4 percent to 1 percent. It i s estimated that at
the end of 2005, public and private expenditures in R&D will amount to about 40,000 million pesos, or
0.51 percent of GDP. Although there has been progress since 2000 when these expenditures only
represented0.37 percent of GDP, the goal set in PECYT for 2006 is certainly too ambitious (Figure 22).

10. In 2005, federal public expenditures for Science and Technology, comprising federal R&D
expenditure, scientific and technology services and graduate and postgraduate education, will total
33,520 million pesos, which in real terms i s 11.3 percent higher than in 2004. They represent 0.41
percent of total GDP, and are allocated among different ministries as follows: Education (31 percent);
CONACYT (28.3 percent); Energy (16.5 percent); Agriculture (6.1 percent); and Health (4 percent) and
others (14 percent). CONACYT's budget amounts to 8,143 million pesos of which 33 percent is
transferred to Public Research Centers, while the remaining 67 percent is allocated to S&T projects,
scholarships, the National Researchers System (SNI), and to cover administrative costs. To this budget
need to be added 1,313 million pesos that are self generated by the 27 Research and Technological
Centers coordinated by CONACYT.

 11. There has been a reorientation in the allocation of resources. Even though the public resources
have not increased significantly to breach the gap between Mexico and other OECD countries, the
PECYT emphasizesa structural change regardingthe use of resources along the followingpremises:

0   Preferential support to projects that: i)increase the competitiveness of the productive sectors and
    generate linkages among firms, universities and research centers; ii)have an impact on regional
    development and contribute to the decentralization of S&T activities; and iii)address social
    problems.
0   Consolidation of a base of highly qualified human capital by: i)strengthening doctoral and
    postdoctoral quality programs; ii)refocusing the scholarship program to better address the demands



                                                      117

     o f the productive sector; and iii)providing additional monetary incentives to top researchers and
     technologists both in the public and the private sector.

12.    The government is aware of the crucial role that the private sector has to play to boost
innovation in the country. Private sector innovation i s at the heart of the new S&T strategy pursued by
PECYT, hence the goal to increase the proportion of R&D financed by the private sector from 26 to 40
percent of total R&D expenditures in the period 2001-2006. In 2005 this proportion was already about 38
percent. Although this represents considerable progress for Mexico where private sector involvement in
innovation activities has traditionally been very low, it i s still far from many OECD countries where on
average private participationrepresents 65 percent.

13.    Several programs coordinated by CONACYT are underway with the objective to provide
incentives for private sector competitiveness and business innovation. CONACYT work plan
obviously reflects this priority and has progressively shifted away from the supporting pure research and
toward developing applications that will help firms to become more productive. Since 2001 a series of
support programs that address different needs in the innovation strategy of firms have been launched.

14. Fiscal incentives. Development of new products, materials or processes i s promoted by providing
Jiscal incentives to firms that can claim up to 30 percent of their annual investment in R&D projects. The
tax-credit scheme budget was increased fr,om 500 million pesos in 2003-similar           to that of the two
previous years-to      about 1,000 million in 2004 and tripled this amount in 2005. The number of
participating enterprises (150) and supported projects (548) in 2001 has increased to 357 and 1308
respectively in 2004. 63 percent o f the beneficiaries and 43 percent of the projects were associated with
micro, small and medium firms. The scope of the projects varied greatly, from new agncultural products
research to improvement in reactor technology. In2004 the total investment associated with the eligible
projects amounted to 6,5 14 million pesos.

15.    CONACYT has made some efforts to evaluate the impact o f this program, by analyzing the sample
o f firms that have benefited from the fiscal incentives in the years 2001 and 2002. Rough estimates of
improvements in some performance parameters are given inTable 10below.

 16. The regional distribution of projects that have benefited from fiscal incentives appears in Figure 23.
As can be seen three states, Nuevo Leon, Mexico DF and Chihuahua, concentrate most of the projects.
Firmsfromthe southern states did not participate inthe programinthe period 2001-2003.



                                                      118


chain linkages to SMEs. University and research centers can also be benefit from the program if they
collaborate with the productive sector. Funded projects seek to develop new products, services,
manufacturing processes and materials in selected industries, following certain evaluation criteria (see
Box 2).




20. The Fundprovides three "windows" for project proposals:
      a) Innovation and Technology Development: Pre-competitive: to complete the last phases of
          product, services or processes development, previous to commercialization; and Competitive:
          to introduce innovations and for product, services or processes development in a commercial
          stage.
      b) Creation and Consolidation of R&D Groups within Companies:to create or strengthen R&D
          groups within companies or in public institutions associated with companies, to build
          innovation capacity.
      c) Creation and Strengtheningof TechnologyInfrastructure: to strengthen R&D infrastructure, for
          instance around the creation of a laboratory, a pilot plant or a department for product
          development in a company.

21.   In the three years that the Sectoral Fund for Economic Development has been in operation 181
projects have been supported; 68 percent correspond to MSMEs and the remaining 32 percent to large
firms linked with, on average, two smaller firms participating in the corresponding productive chain (see
Box 3).




                                                  120

22.   All these Funds, incombinationwith the fiscal incentives program, play a crucial role inexpanding
the base of Mexican enterprises that initiate or consolidate their innovation activities. Moreover, the joint
administration of the Funds, with CONACYT in charge of the evaluation of the projects' technological
merit and their monitoring and evaluation, allows the exchange of information and the coordination with
other initiatives that provide more general support to private sector development, such as for instance the
Fondo PYME, fully administered by the Ministry of Economy. One of support lines of this program
especially oriented to SMEs, i s the promotion of innovation through the adoption and adaptation of new
technologies that helpbringnew products, materials and processes to the market.

23. Programs to support technology-based business are also an important ingredient in
CONACYT's strategy. While the lines of support reviewed so far try to generate critical mass by
facilitating technology upgrading and innovation activity in established firms, other recently created
programs focus on new high value added lines of innovative business. One such program called
AVANCE has recently launched several initiatives, such as a Pre-Competitive Fund and a Venture
Capital Fund. The program intends to create a critical mass of successful cases that bring innovations
from the lab to the marketplace.

24.    The objective of the Pre-Competitive Fund known as, Ultima Milla (Last Mile) i s to help
transform mature scientific and technological developments into viable high value products and services,
bridging the gap between development and commercialization. It targets two different types of
beneficiaries and, correspondingly, supports different types of activities: firms and R&D institutions. On
the one hand, firms undertaking basic or applied research or technological development can receive
support to develop: i)the last round of prototypes before market introduction and final tests to determine
industrial scale of production and investment; ii)the business and marketing plan and other studies to
evaluate the economic and technical feasibility of the investment; and iii)the requirements necessary for
the protection of industrial property rights. On the other hand, universities and other R&D public or
private institutions will be supported to carry out pre-competitive studies and patent processing, and to
hire technical assistance (Promotores de Negocio) to exploit commercially the results of their research.
These specialists will provide advice in the different phases the project will have to go through before
becoming a new business or a new business line. The program started full operation in 2004 with a
budget of 159 million pesos. By June 2005, 40 new business have been formalized, 5 more than the
statedPresidential goal.




                                                    121

25. The other component' of AVANCE, the Venture Capital Fund known as Progruma
Emprendedores, has as its objective to help start and consolidate new technology based businesses,
including market introduction and scaling up, through investment in capital funds and guarantee funds
constituted by financial institutions with legal status. The capital participation gets formalized through
collaboration agreements with Nacional Financiera, S.N.C.(NAFIN), which administers the fund and
convenes private sector investors (e.g., angel investors, private venture firms) to leverage public funds.
CONACYT's contribution i s used to buy equity in the start-up company, not exceeding 20 percent of
total capital. This program may be an important element in the still nascent venture capital industry in
Mexico. By June 2005, the impact on direct private investment was satisfactory, since for the 32.8
million pesos contributed by the Fund, the private contribution has been 171.5 million pesos.

26.   Researchers and Technologists in Industry. The Program to Incorporate Scientists,
Technologists and High-Level HumanResourcesin the Industry i s a labor market insertion program, with
the dual purpose of strengthening innovative capacity within firms, and creating linkages between the
universities and private R&D sector. The program will support firms willing to hire highly qualified
human resources to develop innovation initiatives or to identify, describe and implement new R&D
proposals. While the firm must provide proof of a contract, the support will take the form of a
scholarship to the researcher that will complement the salary provided by the firm. Although the initial
scholarship will last for one year, conditions for extension will be modulated so as to provide incentives
for the permanent insertion of the researchersand technologists inthe firm. CONACYT i s also devising a
mechanism to facilitate the exchange of information and the match between the pool of researchersready
to enter the labor market and potentialemployers.

27.   The impact of this program would be measured by its effect in the long run, in particular, by
following the proportion of doctors and technologist effectively incorporated to the private sector when
the support ends, and the changes observed in the S&T activity of the firms before and after participating
in the program. This programis expectedto be launchedby end2005 and operatein2006. It is estimated
that, with the support of this program, about 270 researcherswith doctoral or master degrees would start
working infirms inthe 12 months following the launching of the program.

 28.  Public-privateInnovation Consortia and research networks. This program intends to increase
 support to linkages between groups of enterprises with existing R&D absorption capacity, and public
 R&D centers.Each consortiumhas to define a portfolio of R&D projects, which are being carried out in
 collaborative fashion with public research centers according to their capabilities. Consortia and Research
 Networks would operate within the framework of strategic areas defined in the PECyT and within ten
 specific fields aligned with those strategic areas that have been pre-identified as demand areas for
 potential consortia (Box 4). Priority would be given to consortia with internationalparticipation in order
 to advancethe internationalization of the Mexican innovation system.




                                                     122

29. Networks would not only include research groups and companies, but also actors and stakeholders
from government (at the federal, state, and local levels) and civil society when their participation could
facilitate and further the objectives of the network.

30. The program will build on CONACYT's two pilot experiences from the formation of consortia in
medical information technology (Consortium MCdica Sur) and manufacturing of electricity transformers
(Consortium Xignux). At the moment, there are more than 10 negotiations taking place among firms and
researchcenters, with CONACYT assuminga facilitator role.

31. Technology services: firm incubators and accelerators. All the above measures, specially
oriented to support innovation in the private sector, need to be complemented in the medium term by
other initiatives aiming at strengthening the supply of technological services. As an example, Fondo
PYME, a fund focusing on SMEs and administered from the Ministry of Economy, supports the creation
of incubators and firms accelerators, in addition to innovation and technology projects presented by
firms. Incubators are physical or virtual centers that provide information, training and technical
assistance to create, evaluate and implement businessplans. They cover not only traditional business, but
also firms incorporating medium and high technology to their products. In 2004 the National Business
IncubationSystem was created and by the end of the year there were 95 incubators in the network and 67
projects were supported with equipment, infrastructure, and technical assistance servicing a total of 74
incubators.

32.    Business accelerators are also being promoted by the Ministry of Economy. Their objective i s to
facilitate the rapid growth of Mexican technology-based MSMEs through strategic alliances, access to
international venture capital and global markets penetration. So far, two such initiatives have been
launched: Techba-Silicon Valley and in Techba-Austin and started operation with 40 and 15 Mexican
enterprises, respectively.


Moving Forward
33.    Mexico should seek to increase its productivity through knowledge-based integration into global
value chains. Several steps need to be taken to make progress in the building of a coherent innovation
system with a more active role of the private sector and stronger academic-industry linkages. Efforts
should focus on:

0    Continuing the consolidation of funding mechanisms and incentives for the National Innovation
     System across all areas and levels of government. Even though there has been some progress through



                                                     123

   the creation of the sectoral and mixed funds, synergies and complementarities need to be further
   exploited. There are still many enterprise support programs under different ministries and state and
   local authorities, which have an indirect impact on technological capabilities since they are geared
   toward upgradingquality of inputs and outputs. CONACYT has to continue leveraging resources by
   pushingdifferent institutions' innovationagendas.

    Scaling-up of programs through the consolidation of funding, particularly in programs that
   emphasize technology upgradingand development, and speed up the formation of the venture capital
   industry, such as those recently launched by CONACYT. Demand support programs need to be
   complemented by strengthening the regionalinfrastructure that provides technological services.

   Evaluating more rigorously the success of the programs. Evaluations, independently performed
   according to an accepted common methodology, are necessary to determine whether programs are in
   fact helping to improve performance of firms involved in the programs. Evaluation results, translated
   into design and operational improvements, could also help to transforming the science system from
   supply to demandbased.

   Facilitating interactions between R&D institutions and industry. Although the AVANCE and the
    consortia programs can be viewed as entry points for facilitating such interactions, more efforts need
   to be allocated. To make science more relevant to industry requires providing more funding to
    enhance such linkages (support programs focusing explicitly on this objective) but also a deep reform
    to enhance incentives of research organizations to cooperate with industry. This will require to
    review the intellectual property rights system and to establish a framework for public institutions to
    reward staff by success in productive researchand linkages with the productive sector.

    Strengthening state-level innovation sub-systems, by improving the design and implementation of
    decentralized funds emphasizing the roles played by the state-level science and technology councils
    and the economic development agencies; and enlarging the focus and sphere of the collaborative
    R&D networks inorder to establish regionalinnovationclusters.

    Supporting internationalizationpolicies, by financing activities that strengthen the integration of the
    Mexican innovation system into the global system and by engaging successful Mexicans abroad in an
    international knowledge network.


POLICYAREA2: INVESTMENT CLIMATE: REGULATORY IMPROVEMENT AND COMPETITION


REGULATORY      IMPROVEMENT

Description
34. Over the last two decades, Mexico has undergone a reform processthat has redefined the economic
role of the state. Market liberalization especially through the establishment of NAFTA, privatization of
state-owned enterprises (SOEs), and regulatory reforms affecting 90 percent of the legal framework, have
moved the country closer to free and competitive markets. However, much remains to be done to address
structural weakness that constraint productivity growth. Poverty remains high; approximately 52 percent
of the population lives in poverty, a similar level than in the early 90s, and 20 percent live in extreme
poverty. The informal sector represents 33 percent of GDP. At a time when Mexico i s facing increased
competition from China and Central American countries, Mexican entrepreneur's competitiveness is



                                                     124

hindered by a series of micro-economic constraints, including the increased cost of doing business
created by the regulatory burden.

35.      Mexicois under performingonreducingthe cost of doingbusiness.Several studies demonstrate
how the regulatory burden in Mexico i s still hindering business                                The OECD, in its most recent
review94of the effect of product market regulations on investment and growth found that when regulation
i s restrictive, productivity growth i s generally below OECD average (Figure 24).95As the OECD points
out, this does not imply that governments should dismantle regulations. Regulation i s necessary for well-
functioning market economies. But regulation may also contribute to impede competition with
detrimental consequences on resource allocation, innovation and productivity. The challenge for Mexico
i s to design regulations that help to safeguard the public good without compromising economic
performance. It i s an area where learning from best performers i s crucial. The OECD estimates that if
those sectors that lag in terms of productivity were to modernize their regulatory framework and align it
on best practice, then productivity could increase by as much as 10per cent.96


      Figure 24: Product Market RegulationRestrictiveness is High inOECD Emerging Market Economies

 36.     Benchmarhng the cost o f doing business in Mexico i s telling as to where improvements can be
achieved through targeted regulatory improvement reforms. The "Doing Business" report of the World
Bank                  has benchmarked indicators of the cost of doing business in Mexico against more that 150
countries around the world. The report covers entry regulations, licensing regulation, employment
regulations, registering property, court efficiency, corporate governance, creditor rights and credit
 information, bankruptcy and tax issues.

 37.     Policy implications for Mexico are exceptionally important: when benchmarked against other
 countries in the Latin American region, Mexicoclearly lagsbehindinseveral indicators,includinga)
 time and cost to opena business; b) time and cost to obtaina license; c) cost of hiring; and d) cost

of tax compliance. The indicators contribute to a better understanding of progress in regulatory reform
and demonstrate the efforts that remain to be achieved to improve the regulatory environment so as to
allow the Mexican private sector to become more competitive.

38.  The Mexican Institute for Competitiveness (IMCO) produced its own benchmarking report in 2005,
which concludes that the cost of the regulatory burdencurrently represents 15 percent of Mexico's GDP,
while the benefits from regulatory improvement could yield a 5 percent increase in GDP (Figure 25). The
IMCO and Doing Business reports explain more clearly the underlying linkages between enactment of
reforms and their impact on the economy. They strengthen ours and the government's ability to focus on
particular regulatory areas.

39.   Overall, Mexico's performance in DoingBusiness indicators is better than the average, reaching the
top 40 percent. However, competitors such as Chile, Malaysia and Thailand are in the top 20 percent. On
the specific indicators listed above, Mexico, which is losing US trade grounds to China, is also clearly
behind its Latin American competitors.

40.   One of the first regulatory hurdles that entrepreneurs encounter when trying to translate their
business ideas into profitable companies is the time and cost it takes to open a company. The Doing
Business reports that it is cheaper and faster to open a business inChina (41 days, 14.5 percent of income
per capita) than in Mexico (58 days and 16.7 percent respectively) (Figure 26). The picture darkens
considerably when comparing Mexico to its Latin American neighbors. In,Chile, which competes with
Mexico on trade with the EU, it takes less than half (27 days) the time than in Mexico to create a
business. Mexico also falls well short of countries like Argentina (32 days), Colombia (43 days), and
even Bolivia (50 days).




                                                       126

                l l m t o S a t a&ness(ogs)                     Costto Start a Business(%of incom per capita)




                                                     I  I
Source: DoingBusiness.

41. The regulatory cost associated with operating a business i s one of the main factors that drive
companies into the informal sector and which can reduce the competitiveness of entire sectors of the
economy. The construction sector i s particularly affected. The Doing Business report shows that it takes
222 days to obtain a license to build a warehouse in Mexico, placing it in the bottom quarter of Latin
American countries. In comparison, obtaining a license takes 150 days in Colombia, 187 days in Bolvia,
and 191 days in Chile. Costs to obtain a license inMexico (159 percent of income per capita) are not bad
compared with many countries in Latin America, but there is still significant room for improvement. For
instance, it only costs 48 percent of income per capita in Argentina and 125 percent inChile. These times
and costs are indicators of the weight that the regulatory burden can play on investment and/or
compliance decisions that investors have to face while trying to grow their market share.

42.    Other indicators also point out to regulatory weaknesses: Mexico is in the bottom third of the
world-wide distribution of the ease of enforcing contracts. Regarding property registration, there are as
many procedures in Mexico as in Australia and Ireland, but it takes at least twice as long. The
registration at the registry of property i s the main source of bottlenecks; this procedure may take between
1 and 3 months. Costs to register property are high in Mexico, encouraging informal transactions that
leave the property registry out of date and reducingthe availability of collateral for firms trying to access
financing.98

43.    Another example is the cost of hiring workers. Doing Business estimates that hiring an employee
costs just under a quarter of that employee's annual salary in Mexico, well above all but three Latin
American countries. Comparable numbers (percentage of salary) are 3 percent in Chile, 10 percent in
Peru, and 14 percent in Bolivia. Finally, tax regulations are complicated enough that it takes the average
Mexican entrepreneur 536 hours per year to pay taxes. Meanwhile, entrepreneurs in both Colombia and
Chile only spend432 hours payingtaxes.

44.    In conclusion, the costs related to the complex regulatory framework, non-transparent business
procedures and weak judicial structures remain factors inhibiting investment in Mexico. They create
indirect costs that impede Mexican manufacturers intheir competition with other countries, and translate
intojob loss and growth reduction.

45.    Lack of harmonization in regulatory environment negatively impacts businesses. Since
regulatory responsibilities are shared among the federal, state and municipal level, coordination i s
necessaryto eliminate the overlaps, duplication and inconsistency with federal regulations. A 2003 study


98 See ~ww.doingbusiness.orgfor the    2006 data.



                                                         127

commissioned by the Business Coordination Council (CCE) showed marked differences in the quality of
the regulatory framework among states.99On the positive side, improvements in some sub-national
entities have attracted reforms in others, as has been the case with the Rapid Business Start-up System
(SARE). Benchmarkingdifferent states and municipalities can spur this positive competition to improve
regulations. The result of two state by state studies will be released in early 2006 by IMCO and
FIAS/Doing Business.


Challenges and GovernmentActions to Address Them
46.    The           government            has              Figure 27: Institutional Design for Regulatory Review

47. COFEMER i s a technically and
functionally autonomous agency of
the Ministry of Economy created to
co-ordinate and to supervise the Regulatory Improvement Program of the government, to ensure the
transparency of the regulatory process and promote regulations that produce benefits greater than the
costs. Mexico i s one of the few developing countries which created a mechanismto challenge the quality
of new regulation through the establishment of mandatory Remlatorv Impact Assessment (RIA) for all
new regulation that imposes costs on citizens. COFEMER plays the key role of systematically reviewing
 RLAs and regulatory improvement proposals (Figure 27).

 48. The systematic review of administrative proposals is now well-grounded in law, and COFEMER is
 fairly effective in improving "new regulations" (Figure 28). Though COFEMER has the power to present
 reforms to existing regulation, it is sometime difficult to achieve results without a good partnership with
 the responsible Ministry. In other words, COFEMER can push for the structural reform agenda, but it
 cannot carry it out on its own.

51.  For other agencies, COFEMER has "optional or not optional" authority to review and analyze all
proposals with general exemptions in fiscal policy, criminal justice and nationaldefense. COFEMER can
hence develop reform proposals in collaboration with these agencies. In the Fox administration,
COFEMER has made use of its optional authority to propose new measures in at least three areas: (1)
with Interior, it co-sponsored the Transparency Act (2003), (2) with Treasury, Foreign Affairs, and
Labor, it co- sponsored administrative reforms to reduce federal procedures for start-ups and (3) with
states and municipalities it sponsoredthe federalhub-federal fast track start-up program(see below).

52. Regulatory planning activities have been undertaken. The amendments to the LFPA also
included the creation of the Regulatory Betterment Programs (RBP) a mechanism to organize future
regulations that give the public the opportunity to participate in regulatory planning.At least every two
years Ministries and regulators most prepare a RBP to inform the public of their plans for regulation
proposal and the possible creation of new formalities. These RBPs are beginning to build a discipline of
periodical review and planning of the regulatory framework and its amendments. The RBP also help to
achieve the central purpose of the federal regulatory improvement policy: to create and to modify
regulations and formalities according to processes based on planning, transparency, analysis potential
effects, and public consultation, in order to obtain the highest social benefit.

53.   The first draft of the RBPs is submitted to COFEMER, and posted for public comments on
COFEMER's Web site (www.cofemer.gob.mx) for at least twenty days. After the period of public
consultation, COFEMER sends to each ministry and decentralized agency its comments to the programs
as well as other public comments received. Each regulatory agency then makes any corresponding
changes to its program or explains the reasons for rejection, publishing the final version in the Official
Gazette within a month. Each agency must submit to COFEMER a periodical report on compliance with
the programand possible modifications to the programs are assessed.


                                                      I29

54.   The last version o f RBP, was presented in the summer of 2005, with a novelty, this time the RBP
where prepared with a Private/ Public dialogue, that took place before the first draft of the RBP was
prepared. This approach made it possible to unify efforts in a new "systemic approach" to regulation,
creating a coordination mechanism between the public, academic and private sectors. The idea was to set
regulatory priorities in conjunction with the private sector. The "systemic approach" also aimed to
incorporate a holistic analysis o f regulatory system in particular economic sectors. Together, the Mexican
public and private sectors defined 36 specific actions related to regulatory improvement that could
influence competitiveness in a positive way. Those actions have impact in specific areas, such as:
Transport and Telecommunications, Labor and social security, Health, Energy, Foreign trade, Finance,
Technical standards, Migration, and States and Municipalities.

55.   Progress has also been achieved at the sub-national level. COFEMER does not have direct
jurisdiction over the states and municipalities. However, one of the functions o f COFEMER i s to provide
technical assistance in regulatory improvement to states and municipalities. Also, the Program for
Regulatory Improvement 2001-2006 (Progrumu de Mejoru Regulutoriu 2001-2006) establishes the
promotion of a culture of regulatory improvement in the three levels o f government as one of
COFEMER's strategic objectives. COFEMER has thus signed coordination agreements with all states
and the Federal District in which states agree to implement regulatory programs similar to the one at the
federal level, and has signed 50 Memorandums of Understanding with municipalities. COFEMER' s
support to states and municipalities has varied from providing technical assistance in establishing
registries of formalities to conductingreview of regulatory authorities to designing enforcement schemes.
As an illustration, 67 municipalities' regulatory improvement systems are currently being inventoriedby
the COFEMER.


                                    Figure 30: SARE Municipalities to Date




                                                       130

56. SimplifyingBusinessStart-ups. A cornerstoneof this strategy is the "Sistema de Apertura R6pida
de Empresas" (SARE) created by COFEMER. SARE was issued at the federal government in 2002 and
allowed businesses to complete two federal formalities (obtaining a fiscal identification number and
name registration) in one business day. Building on this mechanism, some 85 municipalities
(representing 35.7 percent of Mexican GDP) have also implemented SARE to simplify the regulatory
burden when trying to set up a business, 100 reducing the time to start-up a business from over 30 days to
1-2 days (Figure 30). COFEMER i s to have had implemented SARE in a total of 100 municipalities by
the end of 2006. SARE is considered an important tool in developing compatible systems at the state and
local levels. However, SARE has been only partially implemented in some locations. Moreover, even
with SARE, the entrepreneur still has to register separately with labor and social security authorities as
well as the statistical office.

57.    The FederalRegulatory Improvement Council. The Federal Regulatory Improvement Program,
designed and co-coordinated by COFEMER, i s supported by the Federal Regulatory Improvement
Council (Consejo para la Mejora Regulatoria Federal), whose membership was also modified by the
LFPA. The president of the Council is the Minister of Economy. Other members are the Minister of
Finance, the Minister of Labor, the Minister of the Public Function, the General Director of COFEMER,
the Governor of the Bank of Mexico, five business sector representatives and at least one representative
from the labor, agncultural and academic sector. The President's Legal Counsel, the President of the
Federal Competition Commission, and the President of the Federal Consumer Protection Agency
(PROFECO) were added as new members after 2000. This Council mirrors the experience of other
OECD countries, involving labor and business representatives in official advisory bodies to the
government for regulatory matters.

58. Regulatory Moratorium. In a                         Figure 31: The Regulatory Moratorium
meeting of the Council in April 2004,
President Fox announced a moratorium
for one year on regulations as part of an
effort to further reduce burdens on
businesses. Under this moratorium, any
new federal regulations must be shown to
have benefits that significantly surpass
the costs, or be driven by an emergency
 situation (Figure 31). This moratorium
 has caused a significant reduction in
 issuing and renewing regulations. The
 moratorium process has been effective in
 reducing the number of regulations being
 issued, illustrated by the fact that 38 percent fewer regulations are now "produced" by the federal
 authorities, compared with the number before the moratorium. But it also has had a positive impact
 upstream, within agencies and institutions, which are now submitting 34 percent less proposals for new
 regulations, compared with the volume of proposals they used to forward for review before the
 moratorium."' In 2005, the Moratorium was extended for another year at the demand of the Private
 Sector.

 59. Public Access to Information and Stakeholder participation. COFEMER has succeeded in
 increasing transparency in implementation of regulations. Through open communication with the public

and the private sector in particular, transparency is a key element that has been reinforced. COFEMER
has emphasized its importance not only with RIA'Sand commentary on draft regulations, but also with

60. Within the government, COFEMER disseminates knowledge about RIAs among institutions

has also developed a Federal Registry of Procedures and Services (Registro Federal de Trdmites y
Sewicios, RFTS) that contains all formalities and services of the federal administration with general
exemptions in fiscal formalities.

61.   Transparency mechanismshave also been strengthenedthrough the creation of the Federal Institute
of Access to Public Information (Instituto Federal de Acceso a la Informacidn Pziblica, IFAI), which
enforces the Federal Law of Transparency and Access to Public Information (Ley Federal de
Transparencia y Acceso a la Informacidn Gubernamental), enacted in 2002. FA1 guarantees the
effectiveness of both the right to access public information and the right to privacy through data
protection and promotes transparency and public sector accountability.


COMPETITION

Description
62. Competition, and the benefits it brings with respect to lower prices, more variety and new products,
has been recognized as crucial for markets to work well and for increasing the wellbeing of a society.
Policies that enhance competition are expectedto bring not only static improvements inefficiency, due to
competitive pressures on the monopoly's "sleeping on its laurels" but also dynamic improvements in
efficiency, through improvements in productivity and innovation. Recent empirical research indicates
that regulation that lowers entry barriers and state control increases productivity and technological
innovation,"'    stimulates business investment and increases long-run employment rates.Io3Therefore,
reforms to enhance competition could have significant effects on growth. A study of the impact of pro-
competitiveregulatory reform in several industries inthe United States found that annual welfare gains in
the part of GDP affected by reform were more than 7 percent, with 90 percent of the benefits flowing to
consumers.'04Increasing competitive pressure can increase the probability of firm innovation by more
than 50 percent.'05

63.    Although Article 28 of the Mexican Constitution of 1917 prohibits monopolies and monopolistic
practices, competition policies and their implementation are relatively new in Mexico. It was only
in1993, during the important wave of reform of the mid OS, that the Federal Law of Economic
Competition (Ley Federal de Competencia Economica) was enacted and the Federal Competition
Commission (Comision Federal de Competencia, CFC) was created. Regulations to the law, that defined
the actual proceduresthat the CFC would use to implementthe law, were later publishedin 1998.

64.    The Competition Law prohibits either absolute (price-fixing, output and distribution restrictions,
market division and concerted bidding in public tenders) or relative monopolistic practices if they


 IO2Nicoletti and Scarpetta, Regulation,Productivity,and Growth, OECD Evidence.The World Bank Policy
ResearchWorkingPaper. 2003.
    Nicoletti and Scarpetta, Product Market Reforms: Macro Linkages and Effects on Growth, A Partial Survey.
2004.
 `04OECD, Regulatory Policies in OECD Countries: FromInterventionismto Regulatory Governance.
 IO5World Bank (2004a).


                                                        132

demonstrably limit competition (vertical market division, restrictions on resale, tied sales, exclusivity
contracts, boycotts, predatory pricing, discounts, cross-subsidies, imposition of different prices or sales
conditions of goods or services). The Competition Law provides administrative sanctions, including
corrective conduct orders and fines, for monopolistic practice violations. The Competition Law
investigates concentrations including mergers and acquisitions that combine corporations, associations,
partnerships, shares, interests, assets or trusts between competitors, suppliers, customers or any other
economic agents. Only large concentrations require economic agents to notify authorities prior to their
completion. CFC assesses the legality of concentrations and could sanction them by ordering partial or
full divestiture and fines. CFC also oversees State regulations and cancels them if they constitute
interstate trade barriers.

65.   The CFC is an independent agency of the Federal government with technical and operational
autonomy but is attachedto the Secretariat of Economy for budgetary purposes. CFC does not collect any
fines but rather they are remittedto a general treasury fund. The CFC's President and four commissioners
are appointed for staggeredten year terms by the President of Mexico. This provides independencesince
the commissioners are protected by the duration of their tenure and avoids the usual turnover during
change in administration every six years. The CFC has a plenum, composed by five commissioners, of
which one i s the president. Resolutions of the plenum are adopted by the majority vote of commissioners,
the president havingthe casting vote.

66. In addition to the CFC, competition and consumer protection is also enforced through the Federal
Consumer Protection Law by the Federal Prosecutor for Consumers (PROFECO). The objective of the
Consumer Protection Law is to promote and protect consumer rights and procure equity and legal
security in relationships between suppliers and consumers. Also, regulatory commissions have been
established for specific economic sectors: the Federal Telecommunications Commission (COFETEL)
within the Secretariat of Communications and Transportation to regulate telecommunications, the Energy
Regulatory Commission (CRE) to regulate electricity and gas, the National Insurance and Bonding
Commission to regulate insurance and bonds, and the National Retirement FundSystem Commission that
regulates pension funds. The transportation sector i s regulated directly by the Secretariat of
Communications and Transportation. The Secretariat of Finance and Public Credit, the National Banking
and Securities Commission, and the Mexican central bank regulate the financial and banking sector.
Although these institutions can impose price controls to limit monopolistic power, the CFC has first to
determine if there is an absence of effective competition in the market. Also, although these other
"regulators" can award concessions, licenses, or permits intheir regulated sector, they could only award
them after the CFC has given a favorable opinion.

Challenges
67. An OECD assessment of Mexico's competition law and policy completed in 199816concluded that
the law was well-conceived and that the CFC had strong enforcement powers and important authority to
 affect direct market regulation by other agencies. There was, however, no clear base of support for
competitionpolicy, and the CFC's enforcement record could be questioned. Since then, a new review by
the OECDlo7confirmedthat the strengths identified inthe first review still pertain, butthere were now no
doubts about the CFC's willingness to engage powerful economic interests. It also ascertained that the
 CFC had matured into a credible and well-respected agency that had compiled a remarkable record of
 achievement given the difficulties of its environment. It highlighted, however, the following challenges



 lo6OECD, BackgroundReport on the Role of Competition Policy inRegulatoryReform, in OECD,
 Regulatory Reformin Mexico. 1999.
    OECD, CompetitionLaw and Policy in Mexico. an OECD Peer Review. 2004.


                                                     I33

and opportunities for improvement: (i)limited degree of general support for competition policy; (ii)
certain deficiencies in statutory authority and judicial review processes constrain the CFC' s ability to
address anticompetitive conditions effectively and efficiently; (iii)a decline in resources despite an
increasing workload; and (iv) some of the CFC's procedures and methods o f interface with other
government entities reduce its efficacy as a law enforcement agency and competition advocate.

68.   As both OECD assessments show and despite having made progress incompetition law andpolicy,
there are several aspects that still limit competition in Mexico. The OECD barriers to competition
indicator that measures legal barriers to competition and antitrust exemptions for public enterprises
shows that this index has gotten worse in Mexico and that Mexico has the largest barriers to competition
among OECD countries (Figure 32).Io8 The challenges to effective competition in Mexico are reflected
both in their impact on investment and on the perceptions of entrepreneurs. According to IMCO's
research on the impact of 10 dimensions o f competitiveness on investment for 45 economies, Mexico
ranks 33 in terms of level of competition and formality of the markets.log Furthermore, according to a
recent governance survey, the two largest market obstacles to business development identified by firms
are government monopolies and private monopolies (Figure 33).'1 The Global Competition Review 2003
ranking of competition regimes in 25 countries ranks Mexico near the bottom with a score of 2.25, in a
rating from 0-5, only above Greece (2.0) and Argentina (1.5) since Mexico's competition regime i s
considered to be severely undercut by courts."'

 69. One major limitation to CFC's action arises from two proceedings at the Federal Judiciary that are
 brought by dissatisfied parties to seek judicial relief. These two proceedings, the amparo actions
 ("protection appeals") at the Federal District Court and the fiscal nullity actions at the Federal

Administrative and Fiscal Justice                can take many years to resolve and have proliferated over the
years. On average it takes 401 days for these suits to be resolved and between 1994 and 2004 the number
of suits (amparos and fiscal nullity) increased from 41 to 170. Even if CFC's orders survive the amparos
and fiscal nullity actions, the imposed fines are not collected by the CFC but rather by the fiscal
department of the municipality o f residency of the fined party. From 1993 to September 2005, only 3.3
percent o f the total amount fined by the CFC was collected, while 72 percent i s under reconsideration
and 20 percent i s under amparo and fiscal nullity action.

70. Another important limitation to the CFC's ability to address anticompetitive conditions effectively
and efficiently i s that the CFC lacks many powers that competition commissions in other countries have
(Figure 34). Among these are the ability to divest monopolies, undertake searches, implement leniency
programs, and order preventive measures. Due to limitations in the Competition Law, the CFC i s not able
to prosecute and divest or impose price controls on a dominant firm that restricts supply and raises prices.
Also, without the use o f search and seizure authority, it i s difficult for the CFC to detect surreptitious
collusion. The lack of a leniency program for the CFC restricts its ability to provide reductions in
monetary penalties to conspirators who voluntarily reveal collusive agreements.
