71. CFC has limited coordination with other regulators at the federal, state and municipal levels. Given
the powers o f the many sectoral regulators, of other agencies such as COFEMER and PROFECO, and o f
state and municipal entities, the CFC could improve its effectiveness by working more closely with them.
The CFC could benefit from monitoring the sectoral regulator's regulatory postings and comment on
them. Likewise, the CFC i s not taking advantage of data available from other regulators, such as


11* An arnparo is a remedy against acts by any authority that violate any of the individual guarantees recognized by
the Mexican Constitution. It is available inall legal matters and may be invoked in criminal, civil and administrative
trials. If CFC's resolution imposes a fine, then the private party can request a review by the Federal Administrative
and Fiscal Justice Court, which mostly considers tax cases, but also assertsjurisdiction to review any agency.


                                                          135

PROFECO, that could be useful in detecting collusive behavior. This collaboration and taking advantage
of the possibility of exploiting synergies could also prove useful given the declining budget of the CFC.
Although the number of cases has increased, the CFC's budget has fallen 9 percent inreal terms between
2000 and 2004.

GovernmentActions
72. To face the many challenges outlined above, the federal government is taking action to enhance
both competition policy and the role of the CFC. For this, the CFC is providing technical support to
legislative proposals that could amend the Federal Competition Law in order to improve CFC's ability to
address anticompetitive conditions effectively and efficiently. One major aspect of this amendment
would be to avoid excessive legal challenges by.dissatisfiedparties to the resolutions issuedby the CFC.
Other challenges to be addressed by the amendment to the Federal Competition law include expanding
the CFC's ability to divest monopolies, undertake searches, implement leniency programs, and order
preventive measures.

73. The CFC has also started a process of strengthening its internal procedures. It has undertaken an
internal restructuring aimed at reallocating resources to operative areas that are responsible for major
procedures. It is also prioritizing investigations and deepening the analysis. To maximize the probability
that CFC resolutions are ratified by courts, it is developing a comprehensive strategy for each
investigation. It i s also outsourcing the study of industries or sectors requiring a high level of
specialization to external experts or consultants.

74. The CFC is also strengthening its cooperation with other institutions. It i s promoting greater inter-
institutional cooperation for which inter-agency agreements are being signed with other agencies. In
September of this year, the CFC signed administrative collaboration agreements with COFEMER and
PROFECO. The collaboration agreements establish mechanisms and bases for collaboration within its
respective scopes of power. The agreement signed between the CFC and PROFECO focuses increased
collaboration on improving consumers' protection and preventing monopolistic practices. The agreement
between the CFC and COFEMER joins efforts for the promotion of the competition principles and
economic efficiency in the regulations and administrative acts of the Federal Government. The
agreements establish concrete mechanisms (exchanges of information, training, joint analysis of
regulations and an early alert system by which CFC will comment on regulations that might affect
competition). During 2004, the CFC participated in several inter-ministerial commissions with the
purpose of promoting the principles of economic competition in the design and application of public
policies and programs. The CFC is also signing agreements of administrative cooperation to provide
training, technical assistance and registry information to state and municipal officials and local economic
agents so that they could identify practices that limit competition. By the end of 2005, CFC would have
signed agreementswith 15 states.

75. In addition to the efforts by CFC to strengthen its cooperation with other federal institutions and
with the states, it has taken several actions to increase the general support for competition policy. The
CFC has created and fortified communication mechanisms with diverse regulatory agencies and
 secretariats of state and has assigned important resourcesto create a "competition culture" among various
 stakeholders (consumers, academia, entrepreneurs, investors, and public officials). In2005 and for fourth
consecutive year, the CFC celebrated the "Competition Day" and has undertaken many seminars to
promote the principles of competition in Mexico. Also, in order to increase transparency and general
 support for competition policy, the CFC has designed diverse means of diffusion to inform the public
about the decisions made in specific cases. During 2005, the CFC presented to the public the decisions
 on several subjects through diverse publications and channels (printed reports on economic competition;



                                                     136

an economic competition gazette in which decisions are published, summaries of the resolutions on its
web page and the Official Gazette of the Federal Government).


POLICY AREA3: LOGISTICS             AND TRADE    FACILITATION

Description
76. The trade and investment environment in Mexico has been transformed over the past two decades.
T h i s i s in part due to the 1994 launch of the North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA) with the
United States and Canada, and, more recently, trade agreements with the European Union and Japan."3
Growing competitive pressures from countries such as China have also engendered policy reform
measures to lower trade transactions costs to expand export opportunities for Mexican firms. The engine
of export-led expansion in the second half of the 1990s, however, has turned to lower growth rates in
recent years. Exports have diversified away from petroleum and related products, however, Mexico
remains heavily dependent on a single market -- the UnitedStates.

77. After implementation of the NAFTA, the growth of manufacturing exports from Mexico intensified
as maquiladora operations along the US. border expanded. Since the early 1990s, manufacturing has
accounted for over 70 percent of the total exports. Mexico's trade surplus with the U.S. has risen
substantially in recent years and totaled US53.7 billion in 2004. Mexico's deficits with other trading
partners (mainly China) have been widening more rapidly. This has led to a growth in the merchandise
trade deficit in 2004, to 1.3 percent of GDP (from 0.9 percent of GDP in 2003).''4 Inaddition, while the
NAFTA has had clear and demonstrable positive impacts on growth and employment, the advantage of
preferential access to the U.S. market i s being eroded by international competition. Mexico has also had
limited success in establishing strong relationships with European and Asian firms and expansion of
exports in these markets, in part due to the lack of incentives for maquilas to establish strategic alliances
outside of NAFTA. Continued high trade transactions costs associated with reaching these markets for
Mexican firms is one important contributing factor inhibitingprogress in export expansion in this broader
picture.

78. In2003, China surpassed Mexico as the UnitedStates' second largest trading partner after Canada.
Mexico is losing its competitiveness to China not only in terms of labor costs but also in areas directly
related to trade facilitation. These include, among other factors, wider use of information technology in
trade transactions, streamlined regulatory requirements on imports, and assistanceto small and medium-
sized firms in trade logistics.

79. During the last decade, growth in Total Factor Productivity (TFP) has been minimal; TFP
contributed only 0.7 percentage points to annual GDP growth during 1996-2003 (see Figure 2).
Increasing private sector labor productivity requires greater access to financial services, infrastructure,
lower cost logistics services, and increased government regulatory transparency - which are all factors
tied to trade facilitation and logistics objectives. In order to regain competitiveness, increases in
efficiency and reduction in costs of the various components of the logistics chain are a priority.




    Currently, Mexico has signed 12Free Trade Agreements with 43 countries in North America, Latin America,
Europe and Asia.
114  Economist Intelligence Unit (2005). Country Profile Mexico.

80. Measures to advance reform in Mexico related to expansion of trade through lowered trade costs
institutedover the past decade are fundamental to address the trends outlined above. There is no standard
definition of trade facilitation in public policy discourse. In a narrow sense, trade facilitation simply
addresses the logistics of moving goods through ports or the cost of transport services to domestic firms
serving export markets.

81.   In recent years, the definition has broadened to include the overall environment in which trade
transactions take place. This includes the transparency and professionalism of customs and regulatory
environments, as well as harmonization of technical regulations to internationalnorms, and expanded use
of information technology in trade transactions, for example. The focus of trade facilitation efforts for
countries such as Mexico, therefore, is now concentrated on "inside the border" domestic policies and
institutional capacity neededto streamline trade rules and lower costs for exporters.

82.   As mentioned before, an element directly impacting trade and competitiveness is logistics costs.
The logistic concept comprises the physical flow of goods and data through the supply chain. Logistics
involves the `lprocess of planning, implementing and controlling the eficient, cost-effective flow and
storage of raw materials, in-process inventory, finished goods and related information from point of
origin to point of consumption for the purpose of conforming to customer requirement^"."^ Logistics
costs include transportation infrastructure and services (roads, ports, railways, and trucks), warehousing
and inventory financial costs, and the associatedfirm administrative costs resultingfrom the supply chain
organization.

83.   At the micro level, the reduction of logistics costs enables firms to improve their financial
performance, resultingin positive economic outcomes for the country. With better logistics performance,
a firm will be able to reduce its inventory level and costs as well as the total costs of goods delivered,


   Institutefor Supply ChainManagement.


                                                     138

allowing it to remain responsive to the client and consumers' preferences and ultimately increase its
market share. In the context of global sourcing of goods, this results in increased trade and higher-value
employment, leading to economic growth. Moreover, studies have shown that if a reduction in logistics
costs takes place, products that were unviable to produce for export could become viable after this cost
reduction. This i s likely to be the case for primary products and commodities, such as fruits and
vegetables, which are likely to be produced in poorer areas.

84. Logistic costs are particularly high in Mexico, badly affecting the country's competitiveness.
Logistics costs are a relevant component of total production and distribution costs. In Mexico they are
estimated at 20 percent, while the OECD average is 9 percent.'I6 A pilot survey of a new Logistics
Perception Index (LPI), aimed at measuring operators' perceptions of countries' logistics environments,
gives Mexico a score of 3.57 (7 being the best and 1 the worst), below other Latin American countries
like Argentina (4.46) and Brazil (3.83).II7

85.   Recent trends in international trade are making logistics a more important factor affecting a
country's competitiveness. After the decrease in tariffs following bilateral, regional and multilateral trade
agreements, countries are relying less on preferential tariffs to promote exports in the long run.
Therefore, the improvement of logistic performance (cost and quality) is progressively becoming a key
factor in countries' competitiveness. It is worth noting that increases in trade ultimately result in higher
incomes and poverty reduction; Frankel and Romer (1999) found that countries "closer" to world markets
enjoy higher levels of trade, and that a 1percent rise in the ratio of trade to GDP increases income per
person by at least 0.5 percent.

86. With the rapid integration of networked information technology, includingtelecommunications for
data flows and use of technology in the internationalvalue-chain, modem definitions of trade facilitation
now include wider application of information technology in government functions. Mexico can continue
to expand the use of information technology to lower trade costs, for example, as part of a broader
competitiveness strategy moving forward. In sum, reform in trade facilitation and logistics constitutes a
major part of the overall context of Mexico's continued expansion of productivity and growth.

Challenges
87. Based on new data in the World Bank's most recent Doing Business Report (2006), Mexico i s
ranked as number 73 of 155 countries assessed on ten businessindicators, including costs and regulations
that enhance or constrain the business investment, productivity, and growth. Mexico, as an upper-middle
income country, remains in the middle rankings of all countries surveyed. A "trading across borders"
indicator i s one of the newly added topics in the 2006 report, which compiles procedural requirements
for trading a standardized shipment of goods across national borders.
88.   When examining the detailed components associated with the trading across borders indicator, the
data in the DoingBusiness Report suggests that Mexico's performance remains at the average of all other
economies in the Latin America and Caribbean region. The numbers of documents required for export
and import are 6 and 8 respectively. The numbers of signatures for export and import are 4 and 11
respectively which indicates continued need for measures to streamline trade procedures. Considering
Mexico's geographic proximity to the United States, the time for export and import at 18 and 26 days
respectively indicates a great deal of work ahead to facilitate trade. The data for the OECD countries are
 12days on average for exports and 14 days for imports to clear borders (Table 12).

89. Compared with China, which i s ranked 48 in overall trading across borders indicators, Mexico is in
the slightly better position overall - but a not significantly better performance. Mexico is more
competitive in terms of the number of signatures and days required for export, as well as documents for
import, while China i s in the better position in terms of the number of signatures and days required for
imports.

90. Compared with its NAFTA alliance partner, Canada, Mexico still has significant room to improve
in trade efficiency. For example, the number of signatures required inMexico for exports is 4, compared
with 2 in Canada and time for export is 18 days while it i s 12 days in Canada. Signatures required in
Mexico for imports total 11 while only 1 i s required in Canada. The total time for import i s 26 days
compared with 12 days in Canada. Under the assumption that the US will remain a major import and
export partner for Mexico, it is crucial for Mexico to continue to improve performance toward Canada,
considering its beneficial circumstancesunder the NAFTA alliance.

91. The challenges for Mexico in lowering transactions costs and facilitating trade include a number of
strategic policy goals. Further lowering and reducing the dispersion of tariff rates of protection on
imports is critical. Lowering tariffs provides a foundation for moving to address non-tariff obstacles to
Mexican firms to expand production and support export competitiveness; meanwhile, the simplification
 would make different productive chains viable."* This is particularly important given the need to
continue to diversify exports beyond oil and the major destination, which continues to be the United
 States. The U S accountedfor 88 percent of export sales in 1998-2004,up from 83 percent in 1991-97.

 92. Among the challenges to Mexico in advancing broad trade facilitation goals include; (1) lowering
 tariffs on imports for domestic firms and harmonizing import duties levels along several productive
 chains to further reduce barriers for firms in acquiring lower cost inputs for final production and to
 enhance their competitiveness, (2) expanding use of information technology in government activities
 related to imports and exports to lower trade costs for firms, and (3) and continued streamlining of
 regulatory requirements for firms, including those in the maquiladora, sector to lower trade costs.

 93. Recent empirical evidence on factors affecting trade costs and facilitation, including those in
 Mexico, generally support a focus on these specific issues within the context of a Development Policy
 Loan (DPL). For example, based on the analytical framework of trade facilitation referenced in the
 World Bank Economic Prospects 2004 report (WMO 2004), Mexico confronts challenges in reform and
 raising efficiency in its ranking among trade competitors to lower trade costs. Table 13 indicates

capacity, import-demand conditions, and other factors. The result shows weak growth in export-supply
capabilities, even duringthe 1990s expansion. The preliminary evidence suggests that internal conditions
in Mexico associated with its ability to expand export-supply capabilities-areas       directly related to trade
costs and facilitation-are    a more important factor in limiting the country's export growth than China's
expanded trade into the U.S.

 95. Nicita (2005) provides a quantitative estimate o f the effect on Mexican households from the
 implementation of the Doha Development Agenda.'"              The findings suggests that multilateral trade
 liberalization i s likely not beneficial for most Mexican households unless complemented by domestic
 reforms aimed at facilitating the response o f households to these new market opportunities. The analysis
 utilizes a two step approach for which changes in prices and factors are estimated through a CGE model


    CalculatedfromWilson, Mann, Otsuki 2005. Levelsrankedfrom 1(highestranking)to 75 (lowestranking).
 120 Gordon H. Hansonand RaymondRobertson,"Booms and Busts inMexicanExports: A Gravity Frameworkfor
 EvaluatingSupply and DemandFactors" paperpresentedat the World BankSeminar, October 2005.
    AlessandroNicita, "Multilateral trade liberalizationand Mexicanhouseholds :the effectof the Dohadevelopment
 agenda", Policy ResearchWorking Paper, The World Bank, September 2005.


                                                         141

and then mapped into the welfare function of the household using household survey data. The findings
suggest that multilateral trade liberalization alone would have a small negative effect on Mexican
households. However, when the implementation of the Doha development agenda is complemented by
domestic policy reforms targeted to increasing productivity and improving domestic price transmission,
the overall effects become positive. It i s these complementary reforms to raise productivity associated
with lowering trade costs that Mexico must focus on inthe decade ahead.

96. Based on this analysis, the poor would likely share in the benefits of such trade reforms, as well.
The differences in impacts across households are more closely associated with geographic areas of
residence rather than income level. Measures aimed at enhancing domestic price transmission could
ensure that any gains from trade reform, when accompanied by productivity-enhancing policies, would
be more evenly spread throughout the country.

97. In addition, Krebs, Krishna, and Maloney (2005) examined the relationship between trade policy
and individual income risk faced by Mexican workers, in order to evaluate the welfare effect of trade
reform.'** Their result shows that trade policy changes have a significant short runimpact on income risk
for industries with high levels of import penetration. Moreover, the welfare results in this analysis do not
show that trade liberalizationis necessarily costly to undertake.

98. In other analytical work, Gonzalez and Maloney (2005) explored mechanisms through which
NAFTA related variables affect trade and foreign direct investment (FDI) flows, jobs, and migration to
the US., based on Mexican census data.'23The result shows that more exposure to FDI and also trade
deters out-migration. On average, a doubling of FDI inflows leads to a 1.5-2 percent decrease in
migration. To the extent to which Mexico can lower trade costs and streamline border regulations, one
would expect a more conducive environment to attract FDI, and therefore employment expansion and
poverty reduction throughjob creation.

99. Furtherreductions in tariffs are also important to Mexico's long-term competitiveness. Kee, Nicita
and Olarreaga (2004) estimate import demand elasticities for 4,625 imported goods in 117 countries,
based on the trade restrictiveness index (TRI), in order to study the effects of tariffs on gross domestic
product (GDP).'24 The result shows that homogeneous goods have higher elasticities, while point
estimates increase as the level of disaggregation increases, at which elasticities are estimated. With
 welfare losses at around US$7 billion per year, the United States i s the country inthe sample whose tariff
 structure imposes the largest welfare costs on its citizens. Mexico has the third largest welfare loss at
 US$2 billion with their existing tariff structure inthe overall sample.

 and Otsuki (2005), the following outlines Mexico's trade gains with an improvement to the world (75
 countries) average on each TF indicator discussed above. Table 14 shows the overall trade gains
 (increase in Export  + Import) with domestic reform in four categories of trade facilitation. The largest
 increase in trade would come from domestic reforms on port eficiency and technology infrastructure in
 Mexico.

102. The benefits of continued reform in trade facilitation and logistics costs reduction promise
significant expansion o f competitiveness for Mexican exporters. New analysis at the Bank suggests, for
example, that exports in the textile sector would expand by 17 percent with improved regulatory
transparency alone-reform      measures aligned with the programgoals outlined in this document.125

103. The logistic agenda covers multiple and systemic components, the internodal coordination in key
corridors being a major aspect. The challenges facing Mexico logistics performance encompass the
development of modern physical infrastructure, the improvement of logistics and transport services
regulations and institutions, and the support for private sector development as regards the organization of
the supply chain and the delivery of modern logistic services.

104. One important challenge facing Mexico i s the need to improve some key internal corridors, as
manufacturers encounter high cost to deliver their products f r o d t o the border or portdairports. Elevated
transport costs and times have also led to high inventory levels for businesses: duringthe 1990s, Mexico
had 90 percent higher raw materials inventory levels on average than the               The lack o f efficient
multi-modal transportation platform, such as logistics centers, increases the time to deliveryheceive a
product and the associated cost.

105. Customs i s another key component o f the logistics costs. Average customs transactions involves
many parties, documents and data. Administrative costs relating to customs procedures were estimated at
7 percent-10 percent of global trade'27 and Mexico i s not an exception. The lack of flexibility in working
hours and location for product clearance adds on delays and costs. The customs administration would
also benefit from a modernization of its infrastructure, including better use of information technology.

106. Manufacturing companies, especially small and medium size enterprises (SMEs) often incur higher
costs due to a lack of skills in modem supply-chain management techniques. This involves rethinking the
complete supply chain from material and services sourcing to the use o f specialized distribution

companies to deliver the end product to the client. Another factor slowing the adoption of modem
supply-chain management techniques i s the limited availability of efficient operators able to deliver
integrated logistics services.

GovernmentActions
107. The government has taken a number of actions over the past five years to lower trade costs and
address the conditions outlined above. This has taken place, in part, within the context of concern that the
opportunities of the NAFTA to stimulate development of linkages between export-oriented sectors and
the domestic economy have not been fully realized. The government has sought to implement a more
robust policy aimed at spreading the benefits o f increased market access more widely throughout the
Mexican economy - through efforts to lower trade transactions costs to facilitate exports.

108. Mexico's tariff have been harmonized and reduced on a number of products.12* Import procedures
and non-tariff technical requirements have been eliminated or streamlined under a number of programs
already in place, such as the maquiladoras and PZTEX. Comparing the first quarter of 2005 with the same
quarter o f 2004, total output of the maquila industry grew by 6.3 percent, to the maquiladora trade
balance o f US$ 10.8 billion as a proxy for value added. Along with the strong growth of jobs in the
manufacturing sector, Mexico i s becoming more closely integrated in the production and distribution
systems of U S industries.

 109. In addition, the government has acted to facilitate trade through the Programa de Zmportaci6n
Temporal para Producir Articulos de Exportaci6n (Pitex) which allows duty-free entry on temporary
imports of raw materials, parts, machinery and equipment used to manufacture exports. Action has also
been taken to further leverage the use of information technology to lower trade transactions cost for firms
through government programs to expand the use o f electronic documents for import control
requirements.

 110. For example, the government has moved to advance a system for transmission of documentation on
certificates o f origins and other technical requirements for imports into Mexico through amendments to
foreign trade rules. The SICEX (Sistema Integral de Comercio Exterior) program was established to
reduce processing time for import and export permits, including electronic transmission of certificates of
 rules of origin. Along with the U.S. and Canada, Mexico has liberalized rules of origin used to determine
 whether a good qualifies for preferential treatment. These changes will make it easier for manufacturers
 of these products to meet the NAFTA rules of origin and to qualify for duty-free treatment. It has been
 estimated that these products represent US$20billion in total trilateral trade.

 111. The government has also allocated fundingto expand information technology systems in ministries
 with responsibilities over import requirements, as part of efforts to lower trade costs for firms. Finally,
 Mexico has moved to address trade facilitation goals through participation in the World Trade
 Organization (WTO) negotiations in the Doha Agenda. The government has also strengthened its
 implementation of commitments made in the Asia Pacific Economic Cooperation (APEC) on trade
 facilitation to meet the goal of a five percent reduction in trade transactions costs made as part of the
 APEC action plan. According to the APEC 2004 Individual Action Plan, Mexico selected the actions and

                                                          144

measures that will be implemented by federal level in four key areas: movements of goods, mobility of
business people, standardsand conformance, and electronic commerce.129

112. In regard to customs procedures, Mexico's policy is focused on analyzing and solving specific
problems in dealing with customs operations. The Mexican Integral Automated Customs System (SAAI)
has been launched. This is an automated environment with the goal of paperless trading, incorporating
electronic signature and coordinating several agencies within an integrated information system. The
SAAI-to      be completed in several stages in which the different control agencies, "Secretadas", are
progressively incorporated into the System-includes         information exchange: payment of customs duties,
deposit certificates, cargo shipping lines manifests, temporary importation for some transport modes,
certain carrier's documents; and electronic transmission of certain government permits and certificates,
such as sanitary and phytosanitary requirements.

113. On the infrastructure side, strong efforts have been made at improving highways and border
crossing^.'^^ As regards regulations, several important steps have been taken in the last years: the
deregulation of the trucking industry in 1989, and the concessioning of ports in 1993 and railways in
1997. Ports improved their performance significantly through the implementation                         of the
Administraciones Portuarias Integrales (APIs), landlord agencies responsible for the organization of port
operations and development. The active participation of the private sector as terminal operators under
APIs administration allowed for US$ 3 billion investment in the last decade and a substantial upgrade in
operations performance. Railroads-increasingly         relevant for the trade flow with the US-received more
than US$ 2 billion since their reform, almost totally from the private con~essionaires.'~~Other relevant
projects have been a new initiative to coordinate actors in multimodal corridors launched in 2004, and
the efforts to spur private sector logistics processes, like the promotion of trucks fleet modernization and
the enhancement of SMEs logistics organization.

114. Notwithstanding these remarkable efforts, the Mexican logistics performance still presents some
considerable weaknesses. This conclusion results from recent analysis made by the government and the
private sector, in both cases concerned by a potential disadvantage of Mexico in relation to other
competitive countries. The government issued in 2003 a Diagno'stico General sobre la Plataforma
Logistica de Transporte de Carga en Mhico, and the Instituto Mexican0 de Competitividad (IMCO)
performed a complete analysis of competitiveness in 2004, with a robust chapter on logistic^.'^^ Both
confirm the needto further reduce logistic costs.

                         ANNEX 7: THE MEXICANENERGY SECTOR

I.OVERVIEWANDCHALLENGESOFENERGY                        SECTOR INMEXICO

HydrocarbonsSector
The energy sector in Mexico has been essential to the economic strategy o f the country since the
nationalization of foreign oil companies and the creation of PEMEX in 1938. After the international oil
crisis in 1973-1974, the government decided to increase investments in oil exploration and production.
With the discovery of new oil fields duringthe early 1980s, Mexico shifted frombeing an oil importer to
becoming a net oil exporter. In 2004, Mexico ranked as the world's fifth-largest oil producer (including
crude, lease condensate, natural gas liquids, and refinery gain), behind Saudi Arabia, Russia, the US and
Iran.'33Today Mexico i s a major non-OPEC oil producer and has one of the largest oil utilities of the
world (PEMEX).

Despite the success and significant oil revenues, PEMEX i s the world's most indebted oil company, with
a net debt of about 32 billion USD (2003). Historically, the Federal government has relied on PEMEX
for about one third of its budget, with PEMEX and its subsidiaries turning over an estimated 60% of their
annual revenues to Treasury (Hacienda) for the financing of key social and infrastructure programs on
health, education and other. Both PEMEX and the national budget are therefore highly vulnerable to
fluctuations in international oil prices and other shocks affecting the oil market.

Overall, PEMEX's financial obligations to the government complicate the implementation o f a sound
program of investments and capital expenditures, which i s considered essential to sustain efficient
production levels and increase proven hydrocarbon reserves. Reforms to the institutional and fiscal
structures that link PEMEX to Hacienda as well as measures to promote private sector participation in
the industry have been proposed by several administrations. However, these proposals have found little
congressional support.

NaturalGas
Mexico has the sixth-largest natural gas reserves in the Western Hemisphere (after USA, Venezuela,
Canada, Argentina and Bolivia). Yet, over the last decade the domestic demand for natural gas has
outpaced the national production due mainly to the gradual installation/construction of natural gas based
electricity generating plants (OCGTs, CCGTs). Today, Mexico imports about 15-20 percent of its
domestic demand for natural gas from the US and it i s becoming increasingly vulnerable to price
fluctuations. The increasing dependence on natural gas imports and associated high prices has led the
Fox administration to prioritize the implementation of a strategic plan aimed at increasing domestic
natural gas production and lowering dependence on imports (Strategic Gas Plan). The plan includes the
following measures:


0   Increase natural gas production through Multiple Service Contracts
0   Diversify natural gas supply import sources and increase LNG imports
0   Reduce the flaring of associated natural gas
    Expand natural gas transport, distribution and storage facilities (strengthen interconnection capacity of
    pipeline grid with US)
0   Allocate more exploration fundingto increase proven reserves




 133Preliminaryestimates, first three quarters 2004; EIA, DOE, 2004. Mexico Country Analysis Brief.


                                                          146

Mexico's downstream natural gas market has been open to private investors since the passage of the 1995
Natural Gas Law. The constitution was modified to allow private companies to become involved in
natural gas transportation, storage, and distribution.

LiquidPetroleum Gas (LPG)
Mexico has the highest domestic and commercial consumption per capita of LPG in the world and it i s
ranked fourth in the world in terms of the volume of demand. The country's consumption has grown at
5.3% over the last decade and-due to the gradual penetration of natural gas-it        i s projected to increase
at least 3.1% per annum. More than 80% of Mexican households rely on LPG, distributed to the final
user either in portable small tanks or through tank-trucks that supply directly to stationary tanks. Today
Mexico imports about a third of LPG demand, in an international market characterized by high price
volatility. PEMEX's LPG production reports a negative growth rate of 0.55 percent per annum. The
volume of imports, which are subject to permits,134is therefore expected to increase. Since February
2003, the LPG price has been determined by a decree issued by President Fox, which will be in effect
until December 2006. Under this decree, in 2005 price increases were controlled between 0.75 and 1.75
percent per month. For 2006, prices for final users will increaseat a rate of 4 percent.

Electricity Sector
The electricity sector in Mexico is characterizedby a capacity stock that includes 8,250 M W of gas based
independent power production (IPPs), 10,268 M W of hydroelectric capacity, 22,691 MW of
thermoelectric capacity (includingfuel oil and diesel), 2,600 of coal basedpower plants, 1,365 of nuclear
capacity and 2.18 M W of wind generation for a total installed capacity of 46,137 MW as of 2005. The
nationalinterconnected systemhas about 45,000 kmof transmission and distribution lines.

Mexico has two electricity companies: Luz y Fuerza del Centro (LFyC) which serves the Mexico City
metropolitan area (roughly 5 million users) and the Federal Commission of Electricity (CFE) which
serves the rest of the country. Despite the strong technical capabilities of the Federal Electricity
Commission (CFE), one of the largest state-owned utilities in Latin America, the sector presents a
number of challenges on the technical efficiency and quality of service fronts and across segments. In
addition, as in the case of PEMEX, CFE's interdependence with Hacienda complicates the financing
structure and the decision making process regarding investments and consequently the sustainability and
future evolution of the company.

One of the most important concerns of internal and external analysts focuses on the tariff structure and
the level and design of the electricity subsidy. For instance, the residentialelectricity subsidies are highly
regressive. The upper middle income households (income deciles 6, 7, 8) receive the majority of the
consumption subsidy. Despite steady tariff increases over the past 15 years, average tariff levels do not
cover the costs of providing the service. The overall degree of cost recovery has been quite stable
averaging about two thirds of total costs in the period 1997-2003. In general, it is recognized that the
system lacks a coherent national policy framework for setting-and linking- electricity tariffs, subsidies
and cost-recovery goals. Since the early 1990s, different governments have proposed different models for
the liberalization and reform of the power sector. However, these proposals have been the source of great
political debate, public opposition and congressional rejection.

11. DESCRIPTIONRELEVANT PROGRAMSANDSTRATEGIES
                    OF

Energy Sector Program
The Energy Sector Program 2001-2006 (PROSENER) defines the general policies and strategic lines of
the Fox's administration for the electricity and hydrocarbons sectors. The program rests on seven
principles which include 1) national ownership, 2) security of supply, 3) social contract, 4) sector
modernization, 5) private sector participation, 6) sustainable development, and 7) commitments to future
generation.
The program outlines the following nine strategic objectives:

    1. Ensure appropriate security-of-supply levels in a system with international quality standards,
        competitive prices and world class public and private companies.
    2. Offer a solid judicial framework to develop the energy sector, providing economic agents with
        judicial security and certainty, ensuring at the same time national ownership and State leadership.
    3. Foster the participationof Mexican enterprises in energy infrastructure projects.
    4. Increase the use of renewable energy sources and promote energy efficiency and conservation or
        savings.
    5. Use nuclear energy - and pacific applications - in a safe way maintaining the highest
        international standards.
    6. Become a sector leader inrisk prevention for productiveoperations.
    7. Become a sector leader in environmental protection.
    8. Become leaders in the generation, development, assimilation and application of scientific and
        technological knowledge, as well as on the development o f highly qualified human resources to
        promote the sustainable development of the sector.
    9. Increase and strengthen the international energy cooperation and participate in the supply and
        demand sides of global energy markets.

The PROSENER describes in detail what actions would be followed during the period 2000-20006 to
achieve the ten objectives. Specific strategies are listed in Box 5 below:

Programof InvestmentsElectricitySector (POISE)
The Federal Commission of Electricity (CFE) prepares every year a prospective ten year program of
works and investments for the electricity sector (known as POISE). This program i s used to negotiate the
annual CFE budget with Hacienda for congress approval. The POISE program integrates security-of-
supply and diversification policies considering least cost economic and financial considerations. The
program i s based on a number of models used internationally to plan the evolution of electricity sectors.
CFE has also developed its own domestic models to simulate scenarios and the future composition of the
capacity stock.

Programof InvestmentsNaturalGas Sector
The Mexican Petroleum Company PEMEX also prepares every year a ten year program of works and
investments. The program includes an in-depth analysis o f the dynamics of international natural gas
markets as well as an evaluation o f the demand and supply sides o f the domestic market. The program
place special emphasis in natural gas savings, energy efficiency, cogeneration and other considerations.

EnergyRegulatoryCommission(CRE)
In 1993, the GOM created by decree the CRE as an advisory body on gas and electricity issues. In
October 1995, the Energy Regulatory Commission Act (LCRE) transformed CRE into an autonomous
agency in charge o f regulatingthe natural gas and electricity industry.The CRE has a budget determined
by S E E R and SHCP and has technical and operational autonomy. However, despite the number of key
tasks entrusted to CRE,'35the commission does not have the authority to establish tariffs nor attributions
for regulating the efficiency and performance of incumbent utilities (i.e. PEMEX,CFE). As o f today, the
CRE has not been vested with enough independent authority to define the rules for market operations, set
tariffs and regulate natural monopolies. However, the CRE does have authority to establish natural gas
tariffs.

FederalCommissionfor RegulatoryImprovement(COFEMER)
With the reforms to the Administrative Procedures Federal Law in May 2000, COFEMER was created as
an autonomous body of the Ministry of Economy. The COFEMER mandate includes the review of the
federal regulatory framework, the evaluation of regulatory practices and the promotion of regulatory
improvement and sound reforms across sectors. COFEMER's mission i s two fold: ensure transparency in
the regulatory process and ensure the balance between regulatory costs and benefits. The commission
uses four important instruments to conduct its activities: a) a Federal Registry o f Formalities and
Services (RFTS), b) Collaborative Agreements with States and Municipalities and the Accelerated
Business Start Up System (SARE), c) Regulatory Impact Analyses (RIAs) and d) Regulatory Reform
Programs (PMRs).




 135CRE is empowered to perform the following tasks: a) Participate in the setting of tariffs for wholesale and final

 used inpublic service, and f) approve the methodologies for calculatingpayments for electricity transmission,

 LPG.

0  The contribution of energy costs to the total production costs of manufacturing industry products (as

   The quality and price of the products from energy-intensive industries are of fundamental importance,
   as they are used as raw materials in the manufacturing of diverse other products. For instance, the
   price of cement in Mexico i s about two times the price in the US, the European Union and other
   countries around the world.

   In contrast with other OECD and developed countries, the prices of electricity, natural gas, fuel oil
   and other fuels have gradually and substantially increased over the last decade. The prices of fuels and
   electricity in Mexico are among the highest of the world.

   Steady increases in energy prices have had serious effects on the costs of production across different
   industries. In the manufacturing sector (which i s not necessarily energy intensive), the average costs
   of energy as a percentage o f total production costs increased by 2.25 percent points over the period
   1995-2002.

   The quality of the electricity service i s poor compared to other countries. The quality o f the electricity
   service delivery has important effects on the competitiveness o f companies. For instance, service
   interruptions and voltage fluctuations affect the overall productivity levels of industries and prevent
   the installation of modem electronic equipment which requires robust service delivery.

   Over the last five years there has been a steady fall in the number o f energy-intensive processing
   facilities. This has been largely attributed not only to the increasing costs o f energy, but to the
   uncertainty levels regarding availability of natural gas and other fuels.


Effects of Energy Prices on Production Costs and Competitiveness

The energy sector i s of high strategic importance to the economies of both developed and developing
countries. Given the importance of electricity and hydrocarbons in the economy, the uncertainty
associated with energy prices and availability as well as the quality o f service have direct effects on the
modernization and competitiveness of all productive activities. The economies that achieved a secure
supply of fuels and electricity as well as competitive prices, exhibited high levels of economic
development and consequently an increase in their level of competitiveness (Figure 35).

            Figure 35: Relationship between per Capita Energy Consumption and Competitiveness

The energy intensive industrial sub-sectors are particularly vulnerable to volatility in prices and
uncertainty of availability. The cost of energy as a percentage o f total production costs for these types o f
industries i s particularly high (see Table 16below).

The quality and price o f the products from energy-intensive industries are of fundamental importance, as
they are used as raw materials in the manufacturing of diverse other products. For instance, the price o f
cement in Mexico i s about two times the price in the US, the European Union and other countries around
the world (115 USD/ton, Data 2004).

Steady increases in energy prices have had serious effects on the costs of production across different
industries. As shown in Figure 36, in the manufacturing sector (which i s not necessarily energy
intensive), the average cost o f energy as a percentage of total production costs increased by 2.25 percent
points over the period 1995-2002.

Infact, the manufacturingsector is not only affected by energy prices, but also by the higherprice of raw

The studies and surveys conducted by the Center for Studies on Competitiveness (CEC-ITAM) to

Evolution and Level of Energy Tar@s in Mexico

NaturalGas
Natural gas prices have increased substantially over the last decade (Figure 38). In fact, Mexico faces one
of the highest tariffs for natural gas imports in the world (Figure 39). On September 12", 2005, with the
high prices exhibited in the South of United States (Houston Ship Channel Index) after the Katrina
hurricane, the President of Mexico issued an emergency program that capped the price o f natural gas at
7.65 USDMBTU for that month (more than 30% lower than the prices exhibited at the time). The
intention of the President was to "protect families and the competitiveness o f the productive industrial
sector" (Diario Ojkial de la Federacidn, September 12, 2005).137

Quality of Electricity Service in Mexico

The quality of the electricity service delivery has important effects on the competitiveness of companies.
For instance, service interruptions and voltage fluctuations affect the overall productivity levels o f
industries and prevent the installation of modem electronic equipment which requires robust service
delivery (Arizmendi 2005).

                         Table 18: Quality of Electrical Service, 1995-2003(IPER)

According to official data, the quality of electricity services has improved (Table 18). Customer
interruptions caused by operating events and customer complaints declined, while connection time
decreased for both the National Electric Company (Comisi6n Federal de Electricidad, CFE) and Luz y
Fuerza del Centro (LFC). However, when annual interruptions are compared with Latin American
privatizeddistribution companies, the performance o f CFE i s poor (Figure 43).

generation and co-generation, high connection costs for self-generating units or new connections to the
grid, and others.

Macroeconomic Effects

Subsidies for infrastructure services absorb significant public resources in Mexico and encourage
inefficient resource use. For example, subsidies (for operations and consumption) in the electricity sector
amount to about 1.1 percent of GDP (Table 19). Tariffs for commercial and industrial users are set near
levels allowing full recovery of the cost of supplying these consumers. However, average residential and
agricultural tariffs covered only 42 percent and 28 percent of cost, respectively, during 1997-2003.

                              Table 19: Estimateof Subsidies for Electricity, 2003




      Other Subsidres (LFC
      operationand FALS)                                                      73.9              O.35Qb



      Note: FAIS is a fund to support social infrastructure.
      Source: Bank staff calculations from electricity sector report (Samaniego-Breach, 2005).

Residential electricity subsidies are highly regressive: Upper middle income households (income deciles
6, 7, and S), receive the majority of the consumption subsidy. The electricity subsidies also go mostly to
the regions that are already more economically developed. The vast majority of the subsidy-over 90
percent-is    not a lifeline for the poor and encourages inefficiency, especially in the hot areas in the
summer, which benefit from highly subsidized rates. Poverty criteria are absent in the determination of
regional electricity tariffs, unlike in water, where some municipalities set lower tariffs in poorer
neighborhoods.13'

                                  ANNEX 8: FUNDRELATIONSNOTE

On November 9, 2005, the Executive Board of the International Monetary Fund (IMF) concluded the
Article IV consultation with Mexico.'39

Background

The economic recovery that began in mid-2003 has continued, though at a slower pace in 2005. A broad-
based expansion of economic activity in 2004, driven by a rebound of private consumption and private
investment, took growth up to 4.4 percent. The economic recovery was accompanied by a boost to
confidence, capacity utilization, and formal employment. In the first semester of 2005, growth slowed to
2.8 percent, reflecting several sector-specific developments, including a soft patch in US. industrial
production. The most recent indicators point to a strengthening of activity inthe third quarter of this year.

After rising through November 2004, inflation has come down significantly in 2005, approaching the
Bank of Mexico's 3 percent inflation target. The 12-month headline inflation rate peaked at 5.4 percent in
November 2004, but has declined thereafter, to 3.1 percent in the first half of October 2005. Core
inflation also has come down, to 3.2 percent.

After tightening monetary policy since early 2004, the Bank of Mexico (BoM) signaled an end to its
monetary tightening cycle in June 2005, and began to reverse that tightening in August. The firming of
monetary conditions in this cycle was substantial-with          the overnight interbank interest rate risingfrom
less than 5 percent in early 2004 to 9 74 percent by spring 2005. Since August, rates have come down to 9
percent, as the BoM has signaled 25 basis point cuts in the desired floor interest rate, in each of August,
September, and October. From early 2004, the BoM has taken a number of steps to enhance its
communication with markets, including signaling a desired minimum level of interest rates-yet
maintainingthe corto instrument (the borrowed reserves objective).

The fiscal accounts improved in 2004 on the strength of rising oil revenues and restraint of current
expenditures. The authorities achieved their target for the traditional deficit, 0.3 percent of GDP, and the
broadly-defined deficit (the augmented deficit) narrowed to 2 percent of GDP, from 3.1 percent in 2003.
Rising oil revenue helped finance an increase in capital expenditure, particularly in the oil sector. Gross
public debt declined to 46.5percent of GDP, in part through the write-off of some of the bank
restructuring debt.

In2005, oil revenues are rising again, but their impact on the broader deficit is being more than offset by
a deterioration in the non-oil fiscal balance. Staff project that the traditional deficit will narrow slightly,
to 0.2 percent of GDP in 2005, while the augmenteddeficit widens to 2.3 percent of GDP. Oil income for
the year is projected to be up by about ?hpercentage point of GDP from 2004 (and about 1 percentage
point over budget projections). It i s expected that almost all of this unbudgeted revenue will go to
compensate for shortfalls in non-oil revenue, and toward transfers for additional investment spending by
PEMEX and the states, as implied by the budget law, with a small part going to the oil stabilization fund
and reduction of the traditional budget deficit.


13'Under Article IV of the IMFsArticles of Agreement, the IMFholds bilateral discussions with members, usually
every year. A staff team visits the country, collects economic and financial information, and discusses with officials
the country's economic developments and policies. On return to headquarters,the staff prepares a report, which
forms the basis for discussion by the Executive Board. At the conclusion of the discussion, the Managing Director,
as Chairman of the Board, summarizes the views of Executive Directors, and this summary is transmitted to the
country's authorities.


                                                          161

The external current account deficit has remained modest, with a continued rise in oil exports and
household remittances compensating for some widening of the non-oil trade deficit. With the recovery o f
economic activity in 2004 and 2005, imports of investment and especially consumer goods grew strongly,
while weak auto exports to the U.S., and enhanced competition from China and other countries, slowed
non-oil exports.

Mexican financial markets have recently performed strongly, as have other emerging markets. Sovereign
bond spreads have continued to decline, recently to less than 150 basis points. At end-October, the stock
market was up more than 75 percent (in domestic currency terms) since end-2003, with much o f that gain
coming since April of this year. The peso also has tended to strengthen this year though giving up some
o f its gains since August. The BoMhas continued to abstain from market purchases of foreign exchange,
but rising foreign exchange receipts from the state-owned oil company have pushed NIR upward. NIR
reached US$61 billion by end 2004, about 4% months o f Mexico's imports and 1.6 times its short-term
external liabilities; at October 28, 2005, NIR was US$62.3 billion.

The authorities have taken further steps to strengthen the public finances. The average maturity o f the
domestic debt o f the federal government was extended to 3.1 years by June 2005, up from 2.6 years in
early 2004. Exposure to currency depreciation has declined further as the public sector's annual foreign
exchange earnings from oil now exceed US$30 billion, against interest payments on its foreign debt o f
US$9 billion. Furthermore, at mid-year the authorities announced that the federal government had
accumulated enough foreign currency liquidity to allow it to forego any external bond issues through
2007.

Executive BoardAssessment

Executive Directors commended the Mexican authorities for the improvements they had made in
economic policies, institutions and structures over the last decade, which have contributed to greater
macroeconomic stability and reduced financial vulnerability. Fiscal policy has earned broad credibility,
with a track record o f achieving the annual deficit targets, and a prudent monetary policy has established
a low-inflation environment. A series of reforms has strengthened the financial sector and boosted its
development. Directors welcomed that economic policy making i s taking place in a setting of greater
inclusiveness and transparency in government. The challenge ahead will be to launch a new round o f
structural reforms that will enhance the economy's efficiency and competitiveness and ensure the
transition to a more rapid growth path, while entrenching the considerable progress that has already been
made in economic policy design and implementation.

Concerning fiscal policy, Directors commended the authorities for achieving declining annual targets for
the fiscal deficit, reducing the public debt ratio, and improving the debt structure, and they encouraged
them to continue their efforts. At the same time, Directors noted that the overall fiscal balance has been
benefiting increasingly from rising world oil prices, and that a reversal of oil prices could complicate
fiscal policy if expenditures could not be reduced quickly. Most Directors urged the authorities to avoid a
further widening of the non-oil fiscal deficit in 2006. A few Directors recommended monitoring closely
the increase in spending by the state governments.

Directors supported Mexico's intention to implement a medium-term budget framework, with the
objectives of reducing public debt further, strengthening the public finances, limiting vulnerability to oil
revenue fluctuations, and allocating resources efficiently to essential investments, especially in the oil
and social sectors. Noting the important share of oil revenues in total government revenues, Directors
also encouraged the authorities to take measures over the medium term to strengthen the tax system and


                                                     162

broaden its base, including by bringingmore of the economy into the formal sector. Directors considered
that the current high oil prices make it more important than ever to ensure that the operations and
investments of the state-owned oil company (PEMEX)are effective.

Directors agreed that the monetary tightening of 2004 and early 2005 had been necessary to contain the
rise in inflation and reverse inflation expectations. They were reassured that headline and core inflation
are now close to the 3 percent target. With inflation expectations also moving down in recent months, the
improved inflation outlook has allowed the Bank of Mexico to begin gradually loosening its policy
stance. Directors recommended that the Bank continue to proceed cautiously in this direction, noting that
inflation expectations have not yet converged to the 3 percent inflation target.

Directors welcomed the changes in the operational framework of monetary policy since early 2004. The
Bank of Mexico's steps to refocus and sharpen its policy statements, provide an indication of its estimate
o f inflation for the current year, and shift toward defining policy changes through signalling a minimum
level of interest rates have enhanced communication and the market's understanding o f monetary policy
objectives. Some Directors suggested that the authorities make public inflation forecasts for both the
current year and a somewhat longer horizon, and adopt fully and explicitly an interest rate policy
instrument.

Directors considered that Mexico has been well served by its floating exchange rate regime. External
competitiveness appears to be broadly adequate at present, as reflected in the sustainable balance of
payments position.

Directors welcomed the considerable progress made in strengthening and reforming the financial sector
and capital markets and reducing potential vulnerabilities, setting the stage for an environment conducive
to economic growth. Commercial banks appear to be well capitalized, profitable, and provisioned against
nonperforming loans. Directors were encouraged that bank credit has begun to expand after a long period
of stagnation. They noted the efforts to further enhance financial sector supervision.

Directors expressed disappointment with the slow pace of structural reforms in recent years in spite o f
the authorities' commitment to move forward in this area. At the same time, they supported the
authorities' emphasis on implementing small-scale reforms to improve structural competitiveness in
specific sectors. Nevertheless, Directors emphasized that to confront Mexico's external competitiveness,
productivity, and growth challenges effectively in the future, more thoroughgoing structural reforms will
be indispensable, including in the energy and telecommunications sectors, the labor market, the judicial
system, the tax system, and the regulatory and business environment. In that light, Directors were
encouraged that there i s a growing popular understanding of the need to implement an ambitious
structural reform agenda. They advised the authorities to continue to stand ready to move ahead quickly
to implement these reforms at the appropriate juncture.
